إلا ثبوته هناك و ( أنه ) أي المعدوم ) ثابت في الذهن) فلا خلف في ذلك، إذ المعدوم في الخارج يكون ثابتاً موجوداً في الذهن (وأيضاً) إن كان المعدوم متصوراً ) فالحكم عليه بأنه غير متصور كما ذكرتم ( يستدعي تصوره ) إذ لو لم يكن متصوراً أصلاً لامتنع عليه هذا الحكم قطعاً ( لأنا نقول:) في جواب الأول ) الكلام في المعدوم (مطلقاً أي المعدوم في الخارج والذهن معافان، قولنا : الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون ترديد بين الوجود المطلق المتناول للوجود الخارجي والذهني، ما يقابله ( ويمتنع أن يكون له أي للمعدوم مطلقاً ( ثبوت بوجه من الوجوه) سواء كان في الخارج أو في الذهن لأن الثابت بوجه ما لا يكون معدوماً مطلقاً، وبين العدولي أو السلبي وعلى كلا التقديرين الحصر ثابت لتلازم النقيض العدولي والسلبي، وليس الترديد بين الإثبات والنفي لعدم الانحصار فيهما كما مر فما قيل : هذا ظاهر إذا أخذ لا يكون معدولة، وأما إذا أخذت سالبة كما هو الظاهر لأنها مناط صحة الحصر العقلي، فلا لأن التصديق إنما يتوقف على التصورات الثلاث لا الأربع ليس بشيء منشأه قلة التدبر. قوله: (مفهوم ثبوتي) أي ليس السلب داخلاً فيه احترازاً عن محمول السالبة المحمول، فإن ثبوته لا يستدعي وجود الموضوع. قوله : ( أي محال باطل أي ليس الخلف هاهنا بمعنى خلاف المفروض إذا لم يفرض سابقاً عدم ثبوت المعدوم، بل بمعنى المحال باعتبار صدق الباطل الذي هو معني الخلف عليه. قوله : (لا يقال إلخ) منشأ هذا السؤال حمل الموجود والمعدوم في الترديد المذكور على ماهو المتبادر أعني الموجود الخارجي والمعدوم الخارجي. قوله : ( أي المعدوم في الخارج إلخ) يعني أن الإطلاق بمعنى العموم لا مقابل التقييد. قوله : ( وأيضاً إن كان المعدوم متصوراً إلخ) للخصم أن يقول : بطريق الالتزام إن لم يتصور فهو المرام وإن تصور يلزم ثبوته وهو محال فتدد
As for the second, namely its being non-certain, there are four faces. (The first is that) this assent (which is our statement 'a thing either exists or does not exist') depends on conceiving the nonexistent (which is the concept of our statement 'does not exist') necessarily, because assent depends on conceiving its terms and what is considered in them. And (that it is not conceivable at all, rather its conception is impossible, so the assent dependent on its conception is also impossible, thus it does not obtain, let alone being certain. And we only say its conception is impossible because every conceived thing is distinguished; for the perception of a thing necessitates its distinction from others for the perceiver, or it is that very distinction, as preceded in the verification of knowledge. And everything distinguished from others is established in itself, because the distinguished is that which has distinction, and determination is a positive concept; its subsistence for a thing is a branch of the subsistence of that thing in itself. Therefore the nonexistent becomes established in itself, so it is not nonexistent but rather established, existing. This is a contradiction (i.e., absurd, false). It cannot be said: Conception of the nonexistent requires its distinction in the mind, not in the external, and its distinction therein requires only its subsistence there. And (the nonexistent) is established in the mind, so there is no contradiction in that, since the nonexistent in the external is present in the mind. Also, if the nonexistent is conceived, then the judgment that it is inconceivable (as you claimed) requires its conception, because if it were not conceived at all, this judgment would be impossible. We say in answer to the first: The discussion is about the nonexistent absolutely, i.e., the nonexistent in the external and the mind together. Our statement 'a thing either exists or does not exist' is a disjunction between absolute being that encompasses external and mental existence, and what opposes it. And it is impossible for the nonexistent absolutely to have any subsistence in any way, whether in the external or in the mind, because what subsists in some way is not absolutely nonexistent. And it is between the privative and the negative, and in both cases the exhaustive division holds due to the concomitance of the privative and negative contradictories. The disjunction is not between affirmation and negation because they are not exhaustive, as has passed. So what is said—that this is apparent if 'does not exist' is taken as privative, but if taken as negative (as is apparent because it is the basis for the validity of the rational exhaustive division), then it is not (valid) because assent depends only on three concepts, not four—is nothing; its source is lack of reflection. His saying: (positive concept) i.e., negation is not included in it, as a precaution against the predicate of a negative proposition, for its subsistence does not entail the existence of the subject. His saying: (i.e., absurd, false) i.e., the contradiction here is not in the sense of contrary to the assumption, if the non-establishment of the nonexistent was not assumed previously, but rather in the sense of absurd, considering the truth of falsity which is the meaning of contradiction. His saying: (it cannot be said etc.) The source of this objection is applying existence and nonexistence in the aforementioned disjunction to what is commonly understood, i.e., external existence and external nonexistence. His saying: (i.e., the nonexistent in the external etc.) means that the absolute is in the sense of generality, not opposite to restriction. His saying: (And also, if the nonexistent is conceived etc.) The opponent may say, by implication: if it is not conceived, that is the desired; and if it is conceived, its subsistence follows, and that is absurd, so it is forced?
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four faces arguing that the first principle (law of non-contradiction) is not certain. The argument: the assent 'thing either exists or does not exist' depends on conceiving 'nonexistent'. But conceiving nonexistent is impossible because any conceived thing must be distinguished, and distinction implies establishment in itself, turning nonexistent into existent—a contradiction. An objection is raised: conception is only in the mind, so the nonexistent can be established in the mind while being nonexistent externally. The author replies: the disjunction in the principle is about absolute being covering both mental and external; the nonexistent absolutely has no subsistence in any way. Further, the opponent's alternative interpretation (negation vs. privation) is refuted. The commentator clarifies technical terms and notes that the objection arises from restricting existence to the external.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the nonexistent can be conceived in the mind without implying any reality, because mental existence is not real existence.
Correction: The author shows that mental existence is still a form of existence; the principle's 'being' covers both mental and external existence, so the nonexistent cannot have any subsistence at all.

Key Takeaway

?

?
The objector claims nonexistent can be conceived in mind. But the author insists on absolute nonexistence. What would it mean to take them as real and seek repentance?

Proof That Absolute Non-Existence Is Inconceivable
Al-Jurjani proves that absolute non-existence is inconceivable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why absolute non-existence cannot be conceived, based on the necessary link between conceptualization, distinction, and existence.

Core Idea
What cannot be made distinct cannot be thought.

Before we can affirm anything about something, we must first conceive it. But what does it mean to conceive a thing? Al-Jurjani argues that conceptualization inherently involves distinguishing the object from others, and this distinction requires the object to have some reality. This lesson unpacks that argument, showing that absolute non-existence is a logical impossibility to conceive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Focus on the logical flow of the argument.
The passage is dense but concise. Pay attention to the parentheses.
2
Trace the logical chain
Identify the key steps: (1) Affirmation depends on conceptualization. (2) Conceptualization implies distinction. (3) Distinction implies being established (thubut). (4) Therefore, if non-existence were conceived, it would be established, contradicting its non-existence.
The chain is: tasdiq → tasawwur → tamyiz → thubut → contradiction.
3
Understand the contradiction
Why is the result a contradiction? Because if non-existence were distinct, it would have thubut (establishedness), which is an attribute of existence. Thus non-existence would exist—an absurdity.
This shows that absolute non-existence is not even conceivable, let alone a subject of certain knowledge.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the implication: since absolute non-existence cannot be conceived, no affirmative or negative judgment about it can be certain. This undermines any claim to know something about 'nothing'.
This is a foundational point in Islamic theology about the limits of human conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - في إثبات العلوم الضرورية قطعاً، التصديق على تصور أطرافه وما يعتبر فيها ( وأنه لا يتصور أصلاً بل تصوره ممتنع فيمتنع التصديق الموقوف على تصوره أيضاً، فلا يكون حاصلاً فضلاً عن أن يكون يقينياً، وإنما قلنا إن تصوره ممتنع ( إذ كل متصور متميز) فإن إدراك الشيء ملزوم لامتيازه عن غيره عند المدرك أو هو نفس ذلك الامتياز كما سلف في تحقيق العلم ) وكل متميز عن غيره ( ثابت ) في نفسه لأن المتميز هو الذي ثبت له التميز، والتعين الذي هو مفهوم ثبوتي وثبوته للشيء فرع ثبوتِ ذلك الشيء في نفسه ( فيكون المعدوم ثابتاً ) في نفسه فلا يكون معدوماً بل ثابتاً موجوداً ( هذا خلف ) أي محال باطل
The Fourth Observation Post - On Proving the Necessary Sciences. Absolutely, affirmation depends on the conceptualization of its terms and what is considered in them. (And that it is not conceptualized at all, rather its conceptualization is impossible, so the affirmation dependent on its conceptualization is also impossible, so it is not obtained, let alone being certain. And we say its conceptualization is impossible (since every conceptualized thing is distinct), for the perception of a thing entails its distinction from others in the perceiver, or it is that very distinction, as previously established in the investigation of knowledge). And every distinct thing from others is established in itself, because the distinct is that which has distinction, and specification which is a positive concept, and its being for something is derivative from the subsistence of that thing in itself. (So non-existence would be established in itself, so it would not be non-existent but rather established and existent. This is a contradiction, i.e., absurd and invalid.)
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a proof that absolute non-existence cannot be conceived. The author begins by stating that any affirmation (tasdiq) requires prior conceptualization (tasawwur) of its terms. If non-existence were conceivable, then a judgment about it would be possible. However, the argument proceeds: every conceptualized thing is distinct from others. Distinction implies a positive attribute, and that attribute requires the thing to be established (thābit) in itself. If non-existence were distinct, it would be established, meaning it would exist—a contradiction. Therefore, absolute non-existence cannot be conceptualized, and thus no certain affirmation about it is possible.

Key Terms
تصور
تصديق
متميز
ثبوت
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that absolute non-existence is conceivable simply as 'nothingness'—we can think about nothing.
Correction: The argument shows that any act of conceptualization requires the object to be distinct from other things, and this distinction entails a positive attribute (thubut). Thus, what is conceived must have some reality; absolute non-existence, lacking any reality, cannot be an object of conception.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that absolute non-existence cannot be conceived?
A) Because it has no terms to be conceptualized.
B) Because if it were conceived, it would be distinct and thus existent, a contradiction.
C) Because affirmation does not require conceptualization.
D) Because non-existence is not a thing.

Key Takeaway
Absolute non-existence cannot be conceived because conceptualization requires distinction, and distinction requires existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just understood that absolute nothingness cannot be thought. But how often do you act as if your own existence is accidental or insignificant? How does this proof demand that you recognize every moment of your consciousness as a gift from the One who necessarily exists, and what will you change today to live in that awareness?
?
The proof shows that every distinct thought implies existence. Look at your own heart: what distinct desires or fears do you harbor that you have not submitted to the truth? Each of them has a form of existence in your mind. Will you now examine whether these existent thoughts align with the reality of Allah's oneness, or will you let them persist without scrutiny? What step will you take to purify your conceptualizations?

First Objection and Answer on Absolute Non-Existence
Al-Jurjani responds to the objection that mental subsistence contradicts absolute non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between mental subsistence and absolute non-existence in Islamic philosophical theology.

Core Idea
Absolute non-existence cannot have any subsistence, not even mental.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a potential objection to the claim that non-existence is absolutely nothing. The objector argues that if we can conceive non-existence, then it must have some mental subsistence. Al-Jurjani's response sharpens the concept of 'absolute non-existence' and shows why it excludes all forms of being, including mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the objector's two points: (1) conception entails mental distinction, (2) judging non-existence requires its conception.
2
Identify the objector's two arguments
The objector first argues that conceiving non-existence gives it mental subsistence, so no contradiction. Second, he argues that the claim 'non-existence is not conceived' itself requires conceiving it, so it's self-contradictory.
3
Focus on al-Jurjani's distinction
Notice that al-Jurjani distinguishes between non-existence in general and absolute non-existence (which includes both external and mental realms). He clarifies that the discussion is about absolute non-existence.
Absolute non-existence has no subsistence in any way.
4
Understand the disjunction
The statement 'a thing is either existent or non-existent' is a disjunction covering all existence (external and mental). Absolute non-existence is the opposite of that entire category.
5
Confirm the core idea
The key takeaway: mental subsistence is incompatible with absolute non-existence. The objector mistakenly thinks mental subsistence is a form of existence that can apply to absolute non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : ( تصور المعدوم يقتضي تميزه في الذهن لا في الخارج وتميزه فيه لا يقتضي إلا ثبوته هناك و ( أنه ) أي المعدوم ) ثابت في الذهن) فلا خلف في ذلك، إذ المعدوم في الخارج يكون ثابتاً موجوداً في الذهن (وأيضاً) إن كان المعدوم متصوراً ) فالحكم عليه بأنه غير متصور كما ذكرتم ( يستدعي تصوره ) إذ لو لم يكن متصوراً أصلاً لامتنع عليه هذا الحكم قطعاً ( لأنا نقول:) في جواب الأول ) الكلام في المعدوم (مطلقاً أي المعدوم في الخارج والذهن معافان، قولنا : الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون ترديد بين الوجود المطلق المتناول للوجود الخارجي والذهني، ما يقابله ( ويمتنع أن يكون له أي للمعدوم مطلقاً ( ثبوت بوجه من الوجوه) سواء كان في الخارج أو في الذهن لأن الثابت بوجه ما لا يكون معدوماً مطلقاً
It may not be said: (The conceptualization of non-existence entails its distinction in the mind, not in the external world, and its distinction there entails only its subsistence there. And (that is) it is established in the mind.) So there is no contradiction in that, since non-existence in the external world is established and existent in the mind. (Also) if non-existence is conceptualized, then the judgment that it is not conceptualized, as you mentioned, (requires its conceptualization) because if it were not conceptualized at all, this judgment would be absolutely impossible. (We say:) in response to the first (objection), the discussion is about non-existence absolutely, i.e., non-existence in the external world and the mind together. Our statement: 'a thing is either existent or non-existent' is a disjunction between absolute existence encompassing external and mental existence, and its opposite. (And it is impossible for it, i.e., for absolute non-existence, to have any subsistence whatsoever), whether in the external world or in the mind, because something that has any subsistence is not absolutely non-existent.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector says: if non-existence is conceived, it is distinct in the mind, not externally, so its mental subsistence is not a contradiction; moreover, judging non-existence as unconceived requires conceiving it. The author responds: the discussion concerns absolute non-existence (in both external and mental realms). The disjunction 'either existent or non-existent' covers both external and mental existence. Absolute non-existence cannot have any subsistence at all, so mental subsistence is excluded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mental subsistence is a form of existence that can be attributed to absolute non-existence.
Correction: Absolute non-existence, by definition, has no subsistence whatsoever, not even in the mind.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani's answer, what mistake does the original objection make?
A) It assumes non-existence can be absolute.
B) It restricts non-existence to external non-existence.
C) It confuses mental subsistence with absolute non-existence.
D) It rejects the necessity of conceptualization.

Key Takeaway
Absolute non-existence cannot have any kind of subsistence, including mental, so conceiving it does not imply its existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine something that doesn't exist—like a past regret or a future worry—do you ever treat that mental image as if it has a kind of reality? Al-Jurjani says absolute non-existence has no subsistence, even in the mind. How does that change the way you should relate to such thoughts? Commit to reminding yourself that your mental images of non-existent things are not realities.
?
Consider a time you argued about 'nothingness'—perhaps a void or an absence. Did you assume that because you could talk about it, it must have some existence? The objector made that mistake. What does this lesson reveal about the nature of such arguments? From now on, when you speak of 'nothing,' remember it has no subsistence at all.
?
The objector's confusion stems from thinking that mental subsistence is compatible with absolute non-existence. Is there an area in your life where you confuse your mental concepts with reality—perhaps in your judgments about people or your expectations? How will you apply the lesson's distinction between mental subsistence and real existence to correct that confusion? Make a concrete change today.

The Second Objection and Counterargument
Objection that judgment on non-existence requires its conceptualization, and counterargument leading to conflict of certain proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that non-existence must be conceptualized in order to judge it as not conceptualized, and the response using a counterargument that leads to a conflict of apodictic proofs.

Core Idea
Judging non-existence requires its conceptualization, creating a conflict of certain proofs.

This lesson presents a clever objection to the claim that non-existence is not conceptualized. The objector argues that the very judgment that non-existence is not conceptualized presupposes its conceptualization. The response uses a counterargument (muʿāraḍa): this leads to a conflict between two apodictic proofs, which actually undermines the objector's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening lines where the objector states: if non-existence is conceptualized, then the judgment that it is not conceptualized requires its conceptualization.
Notice the logical structure: a judgment about something implies its existence in the mind.
2
Identify the counterargument
The response offers a muʿāraḍa: this objection itself constitutes a proof that conflicts with the original proof, leading to a conflict of certain proofs.
This is a specific type of argument in kalam.
3
Understand the implication
The conflict of apodictic proofs is actually one of the author's own arguments that undermine self-evident premises.
This is a sophisticated dialectical move.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the objector's argument would not solve the problem but rather create a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً) إن كان المعدوم متصوراً ) فالحكم عليه بأنه غير متصور كما ذكرتم ( يستدعي تصوره ) إذ لو لم يكن متصوراً أصلاً لامتنع عليه هذا الحكم قطعاً ( لأنا نقول:) في جواب الثاني ( الآخر معارضة ) أي للحجة الدالة على أن المعدوم المطلق غير متصور ( لا حل ) لتلك الحجة ( وإنها ) أي معارضة ما ذكرتم لما ذكرنا ) تحقق تعارض الحجج (القواطع ) لأنهما قطعيتان (وهو) أي تعارض الحجج القواطع المركبة من المقدمات البديهية ( إحدى حججنا (القوادح في البديهيات كما سيأتي وقد يجاب بأن تحقق التعارض إنما يلزم إذا سلم دليل الخصم المستدل عن المنع الذي سنذكره في الجواب عنه الوجه (الثاني) من تلك الوجوه الأربعة ( أنه ) أي قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون ) يقتضي تميز المعدوم عن الوجود ) إذ لولا تميزه عنه لما أمكن الحكم بالانفصال بينهما ( ولو كان المعدوم ( متميزاً لكان له حقيقة) وماهية بها يمتاز عن الوجود (و) كان للعقل سلبها أي سلب تلك الحقيقة ورفعها فإن كل ما له حقيقة يشير العقل إليها يمكنه رفعها وإلا لم يكن لذلك الشيء مقابل فلو لم يكن للعقل رفع حقيقة العدم لم يكن لها مقابل هو الوجود، وهذا معنى قوله : ( وقد يجاب إلخ) لا يخفى أن مقصود ذلك القائل دفع هذه المعارضة بأنه على تقدیر صحتها لا يضرنا، فالقول بأنا لا نسلم تحقق التعارض لأن دليل الخصم المستدل غير سالم عن المنع مما لا وجه له . قوله: ( الوجه الثاني إلخ لا يخفى أن أوله يدل على أن الكلام في المعدوم، وآخره على في العدم فلا بد من التطبيق بأن يقال : المراد بالعدم المعدوم أو يضم لقوله، ولو كان المعدوم متميزاً قولنا ولا تميز له إلا باعتبار العدم إذ الذات المبهمة والنسبة مشتركتان فيكون للعدم حقيقة . قوله : ( وماهية) عطف تفسيري للإشارة إلى أنه ليس المراد بالحقيقة الماهية الموجودة . قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن لذلك الشيء مقابل لأن المقابل إما رفع الشيء أو أخص منه . قوله : ( عن المنع الذي سنذكره وهو قوله والجواب أن المقصود إلخ، ولك أن تقول : لو سلم تحقق التعارض فلا نسلم أن مقدمات الحجتين بديهية. قوله : ( لكان له حقيقة قال سيف الدين الأبهري : فيه نظر لأن اللاحقيقة متميزة عن الحقيقة واللاهوية متميزة عن الهوية، مع أنه ليس لهما حقيقة وهوية ورد بأن اللاحقيقة حقيقة نوعية مغايرة للحقائق النوعية الصادق على كلها اسم الحقيقة لها أفراد اعتبارية هي أسلوب الحقائق ولا استحالة فيه. قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن لذلك الشيء مقابل لأن مقابل الشيء إما رفعه كالعدم للوجود، أو ملزوم رفعه كالوجود للعدم وعلى كل تقدير يلزم من عدم إمكان رفعها عدم تحقق المقابل
(Also) if non-existence is conceptualized, then the judgment that it is not conceptualized, as you mentioned, (requires its conceptualization) because if it were not conceptualized at all, this judgment would be absolutely impossible. (We say:) in response to the second (objection), the other is a counterargument, i.e., to the proof that absolute non-existence is not conceptualized (not a solution) for that proof. (And that it) i.e., the counterargument of what you mentioned to what we mentioned (realizes) a conflict of (apodictic) proofs, because both are certain. (And it) i.e., the conflict of apodictic proofs composed of self-evident premises (is one of our arguments) that undermine self-evident truths, as will come. And it may be answered that the realization of conflict only follows if the proof of the opponent is free from the prevention that we will mention in the answer to it. The second (of those four aspects) is that (our statement 'a thing is either existent or non-existent') necessitates the distinction of non-existence from existence, because if it were not distinct from it, the judgment of separation between them would not be possible. (And if non-existence were distinct), it would have a reality and a quiddity by which it is distinguished from existence. (And) the intellect can negate it, i.e., negate that reality and remove it, because everything that has a reality that the intellect points to can be removed; otherwise, that thing would have no opposite. So if the intellect could not remove the reality of non-existence, it would have no opposite which is existence. And this is the meaning of his statement: (And it may be answered...). It is not hidden that the intention of that speaker is to repel this counterargument by saying that if it were correct, it would not harm us. So the statement that we do not admit the realization of conflict because the proof of the opponent is not free from prevention is without basis. His statement: (The second aspect...). It is not hidden that its beginning indicates that the discussion is about non-existence, and its end about nothingness, so a harmonization is necessary by saying: what is meant by nothingness is non-existence, or one should add to his statement: 'and if non-existence were distinct' our statement: 'and it has no distinction except by consideration of nothingness, because the vague essence and the relation are shared, so nothingness has a reality.' His statement: (and a quiddity) is an explicative apposition to indicate that what is meant by reality is not the existent quiddity. His statement: (otherwise that thing would have no opposite), because the opposite of a thing is either its removal, like nothingness for existence, or the necessary condition of its removal, like existence for nothingness. On either estimation, from the impossibility of its removal follows the non-actualization of the opposite.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and a multi-part response. The objector argues that if non-existence is not conceived, the judgment that it is not conceived itself requires its conception. The response first offers a counterargument (muʿāraḍa): the objector's proof would conflict with the original proof, leading to a conflict of certain proofs, which is actually one of the author's own arguments against self-evident premises. Then an alternative answer is given using the second proof: the proposition 'a thing is either existent or non-existent' implies that non-existence is distinct from existence, and if distinct, it must have a reality (ḥaqīqa) that the intellect can negate. This negation is what makes existence its opposite. The passage then includes commentary on harmonizing terms and a defense against a counter-objection by Sayf al-Dīn al-Abhari about non-realities.

Key Terms
معارضة
تعارض
قواطع
بديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objection is a valid solution that disproves the original claim.
Correction: The objection leads to a conflict of certain proofs, which is actually a problem for the objector, not a solution.

Checkpoint
What does the muʿāraḍa (counterargument) indicate?
A) That the original proof is false.
B) That the objector's argument conflicts with the original proof, creating a conflict of certain proofs.
C) That non-existence is indeed conceptualized.
D) That the judgment is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The second objection is countered by showing it leads to a conflict of certain proofs, which is itself an argument against self-evident premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief with certainty, and then encounter a equally certain but contradictory belief, does that shake your trust in your own reasoning? How do you handle such a conflict in your own heart without dismissing one side prematurely?
?
This lesson shows that even self-evident truths can be put into question through logical argument. Is there any belief you consider absolutely self-evident that you would be unwilling to examine? Are you ready to let go of certainty if a real conflict arises?
?
The mu'arada (counterargument) is presented not as a solution but as a contradiction. Have you ever used an argument to defend a position that actually revealed a deeper problem you were not ready to face? What would it take for you to admit that your defense creates a conflict rather than a resolution?

The Second Proof: The Disjunction Argument
Second proof using the disjunction to show non-existence has a reality and is opposite to existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that the proposition 'a thing is either existent or non-existent' implies that non-existence has a distinct reality that can be negated, establishing existence as its opposite.

Core Idea
The disjunction 'either existent or non-existent' necessitates a distinct reality for non-existence.

This lesson presents an alternative response to the objection using a second proof. The argument starts from the self-evident proposition 'a thing is either existent or non-existent'. This implies a distinction between non-existence and existence. From this distinction it follows that non-existence has a reality (ḥaqīqa) and quiddity (māhiyya) that the intellect can negate. The ability to negate non-existence is what makes existence its opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the passage starting from 'the second aspect' which states that the disjunction requires distinction.
This is a different approach from the muʿāraḍa.
2
Understand the logical flow
The proof: disjunction → distinction → reality → negation by intellect → opposite existence.
Each step builds on the previous.
3
Identify the key claim
Non-existence must have a reality because otherwise it could not be distinguished from existence.
This reality is not an existent quiddity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
If non-existence had no reality, it could not be negated, and existence would have no opposite.
This establishes the reality of non-existence in a certain sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المبهمة والنسبة مشتركتان فيكون للعدم حقيقة . قوله : ( وماهية) عطف تفسيري للإشارة إلى أنه ليس المراد بالحقيقة الماهية الموجودة . قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن لذلك الشيء مقابل لأن المقابل إما رفع الشيء أو أخص منه . قوله : ( عن المنع الذي سنذكره وهو قوله والجواب أن المقصود إلخ، ولك أن تقول : لو سلم تحقق التعارض فلا نسلم أن مقدمات الحجتين بديهية. قوله : ( لكان له حقيقة قال سيف الدين الأبهري : فيه نظر لأن اللاحقيقة متميزة عن الحقيقة واللاهوية متميزة عن الهوية، مع أنه ليس لهما حقيقة وهوية ورد بأن اللاحقيقة حقيقة نوعية مغايرة للحقائق النوعية الصادق على كلها اسم الحقيقة لها أفراد اعتبارية هي أسلوب الحقائق ولا استحالة فيه. قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن لذلك الشيء مقابل لأن مقابل الشيء إما رفعه كالعدم للوجود، أو ملزوم رفعه كالوجود للعدم وعلى كل تقدير يلزم من عدم إمكان رفعها عدم تحقق المقابل
(Also) if non-existence is conceptualized, then the judgment that it is not conceptualized, as you mentioned, (requires its conceptualization) because if it were not conceptualized at all, this judgment would be absolutely impossible. (We say:) in response to the second (objection), the other is a counterargument, i.e., to the proof that absolute non-existence is not conceptualized (not a solution) for that proof. (And that it) i.e., the counterargument of what you mentioned to what we mentioned (realizes) a conflict of (apodictic) proofs, because both are certain. (And it) i.e., the conflict of apodictic proofs composed of self-evident premises (is one of our arguments) that undermine self-evident truths, as will come. And it may be answered that the realization of conflict only follows if the proof of the opponent is free from the prevention that we will mention in the answer to it. The second (of those four aspects) is that (our statement 'a thing is either existent or non-existent') necessitates the distinction of non-existence from existence, because if it were not distinct from it, the judgment of separation between them would not be possible. (And if non-existence were distinct), it would have a reality and a quiddity by which it is distinguished from existence. (And) the intellect can negate it, i.e., negate that reality and remove it, because everything that has a reality that the intellect points to can be removed; otherwise, that thing would have no opposite. So if the intellect could not remove the reality of non-existence, it would have no opposite which is existence. And this is the meaning of his statement: (And it may be answered...). It is not hidden that the intention of that speaker is to repel this counterargument by saying that if it were correct, it would not harm us. So the statement that we do not admit the realization of conflict because the proof of the opponent is not free from prevention is without basis. His statement: (The second aspect...). It is not hidden that its beginning indicates that the discussion is about non-existence, and its end about nothingness, so a harmonization is necessary by saying: what is meant by nothingness is non-existence, or one should add to his statement: 'and if non-existence were distinct' our statement: 'and it has no distinction except by consideration of nothingness, because the vague essence and the relation are shared, so nothingness has a reality.' His statement: (and a quiddity) is an explicative apposition to indicate that what is meant by reality is not the existent quiddity. His statement: (otherwise that thing would have no opposite), because the opposite of a thing is either its removal, like nothingness for existence, or the necessary condition of its removal, like existence for nothingness. On either estimation, from the impossibility of its removal follows the non-actualization of the opposite.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and a multi-part response. The objector argues that if non-existence is not conceived, the judgment that it is not conceived itself requires its conception. The response first offers a counterargument (muʿāraḍa): the objector's proof would conflict with the original proof, leading to a conflict of certain proofs, which is actually one of the author's own arguments against self-evident premises. Then an alternative answer is given using the second proof: the proposition 'a thing is either existent or non-existent' implies that non-existence is distinct from existence, and if distinct, it must have a reality (ḥaqīqa) that the intellect can negate. This negation is what makes existence its opposite. The passage then includes commentary on harmonizing terms and a defense against a counter-objection by Sayf al-Dīn al-Abhari about non-realities.

Misconception: Some may think non-existence is absolutely nothing and cannot have any reality.
Correction: The proof shows that non-existence has a conceptual reality (ḥaqīqa) that is distinct and can be negated, even if it is not an existent thing.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why must non-existence have a reality?
A) Because it is mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because it is distinct from existence based on the disjunction.
C) Because the intellect cannot conceive of non-existence.
D) Because it is impossible to have opposites without realities.

Key Takeaway
The second proof establishes that non-existence has a conceptual reality that can be negated, thereby making existence its opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If non-existence has a conceptual reality that the intellect can negate, then your own existence is not a mere accident. How does this certainty about existence compel you to be grateful to the One who granted you existence, and what specific action will you take today to express that gratitude?
?
The proof shows that the intellect can negate the reality of non-existence. But do you ever negate the reality of your own mortality? When did you last truly contemplate that you will one day be non-existent, and what change did that contemplation bring?
?
If the disjunction 'either existent or non-existent' is a necessary judgment, then you are either existent by yourself or by another. Are you living as if your existence is self-sufficient, or do you acknowledge your absolute dependence on the Necessary Existent? What concrete habit can you adopt to remind yourself of this dependence?

Harmonization and Defense Against Abharī's Objection
Harmonization of terms and defense against Abharī's objection on non-realities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani harmonizes the terms 'non-existence' and 'nothingness' in the second proof, and how he responds to Sayf al-Dīn al-Abharī's objection that non-realities are distinct without having reality.

Core Idea
Non-existence has a conceptual reality even if it is not an existent entity.

This lesson follows up on the second proof by addressing a subtle issue: the beginning of the proof discusses non-existence (ma'dūm) while the end discusses nothingness ('adam). Al-Jurjani provides a harmonization by interpreting 'nothingness' as 'non-existence'. Then he addresses an objection from Sayf al-Dīn al-Abharī who argues that things without reality (like non-reality) can still be distinct from each other. The response is that non-reality is itself a type of reality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the harmonization issue
Notice that the proof uses both 'ma'dūm' and 'adam'. Al-Jurjani explains that they refer to the same thing.
This shows careful attention to terminology.
2
Read Abharī's objection
Abharī argues that the opposite of a reality can be a non-reality, which is distinct from reality without itself having a reality.
This is a logical objection.
3
Understand the response
The response states that non-reality (lā-ḥaqīqa) is itself a type of reality, a generic reality, and thus can be considered as having a conceptual reality.
This is a conceptual distinction.
4
Reflect on the significance
This defense shows the sophistication of kalam in dealing with abstract concepts.

intermediate

The Logical Necessity of a Reality for the Non-Existent
The author proves that the non-existent must have a reality from the disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the distinction between existence and non-existence implies that the non-existent must have a reality that the mind can negate.

Core Idea
If the non-existent were not distinct, it could not be opposed to existence.

The author presents a powerful logical argument starting from the simple disjunction 'everything either exists or does not exist'. He shows that this disjunction only works if the non-existent is genuinely distinct from existence, which in turn requires that the non-existent has some reality. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step, showing how the mind's ability to negate that reality is essential to the structure of opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the core argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully and its literal translation. Notice that the argument flows from the disjunction to the necessity of a reality for the non-existent.
2
Identify the disjunction and its implication
The author starts with 'everything either exists or does not exist'. Ask yourself: what does this statement presuppose? It presupposes that the two options are distinct. If they were not distinct, we could not judge a separation between them.
This is the key move: distinction is necessary for any 'or' judgment.
3
Follow the deduction to reality and negation
If the non-existent is distinct, it must have a reality (ḥaqīqa) and quiddity (māhiyya) that sets it apart. Moreover, the mind can negate or remove that reality because anything with a reality can be negated; otherwise it would have no opposite.
The mind's ability to negate is crucial for the existence of an opposite.
4
Connect to the conclusion
If the mind could not negate the reality of non-existence, then non-existence would have no opposite (existence), which is absurd. So the argument stands: the non-existent must have a reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الثاني) من تلك الوجوه الأربعة ( أنه ) أي قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون ) يقتضي تميز المعدوم عن الوجود ) إذ لولا تميزه عنه لما أمكن الحكم بالانفصال بينهما ( ولو كان المعدوم ( متميزاً لكان له حقيقة) وماهية بها يمتاز عن الوجود (و) كان للعقل سلبها أي سلب تلك الحقيقة ورفعها فإن كل ما له حقيقة يشير العقل إليها يمكنه ،رفعها وإلا لم يكن لذلك الشيء مقابل فلو لم يكن للعقل رفع حقيقة العدم لم يكن لها مقابل هو الوجود، وهذا معنى

قوله : ( الوجه الثاني إلخ لا يخفى أن أوله يدل على أن الكلام في المعدوم، وآخره على في العدم فلا بد من التطبيق بأن يقال : المراد بالعدم المعدوم أو يضم لقوله، ولو كان المعدوم متميزاً قولنا ولا تميز له إلا باعتبار ،العدم إذ الذات المبهمة والنسبة مشتركتان فيكون للعدم حقيقة .

قوله : ( وماهية) عطف تفسيري للإشارة إلى أنه ليس المراد بالحقيقة الماهية الموجودة .

قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن لذلك الشيء مقابل لأن المقابل إما رفع الشيء أو أخص منه .

قوله : ( لكان له حقيقة قال سيف الدين الأبهري : فيه نظر لأن اللاحقيقة متميزة عن الحقيقة واللاهوية متميزة عن الهوية، مع أنه ليس لهما حقيقة وهوية ورد بأن اللاحقيقة حقيقة نوعية مغايرة للحقائق النوعية الصادق على كلها اسم الحقيقة لها أفراد اعتبارية هي أسلوب الحقائق ولا استحالة فيه.

قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن لذلك الشيء مقابل لأن مقابل الشيء إما رفعه كالعدم للوجود، أو ملزوم رفعه كالوجود للعدم وعلى كل تقدير يلزم من عدم إمكان رفعها عدم تحقق المقابل .

قوله ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن للعقل سلبها ( انتفى الوجود ) وإذا كان للعقل سلبها وسلبها عدم (خاص) لكونه مضافاً إلى حقيقة العدم ) فقسم من العدم المطلق وهو هذا العدم الخاص (قسيم (له لأنه رفعه الذي يقابله ( هذا خلف ) لأن قسم الشيء أخص منه، وقسيمه مباين له فيستحيل صدقهما على شيء واحد .
The second aspect of those four aspects (is that) i.e., our saying 'Everything either exists or does not exist' necessitates the distinction of the non-existent from existence, for if it were not distinct from it, it would not be possible to judge the separation between them. And if the non-existent were distinct, it would have a reality (ḥaqīqa) and a quiddity (māhiyya) by which it is distinguished from existence, and the mind would have the power to negate it, i.e., to deny and remove that reality. For everything that has a reality to which the mind points, it is possible to remove it; otherwise, that thing would have no opposite. So if the mind did not have the power to remove the reality of non-existence, it would not have an opposite which is existence. And this is the meaning.

His saying: (The second aspect, etc.) It is not hidden that its beginning indicates that the discussion is about the non-existent (al-maʿdūm) and its end about non-existence (al-ʿadam), so application must be made by saying: what is meant by non-existence is the non-existent, or one may add to his saying 'if the non-existent were distinct' the statement 'and it has no distinction except by consideration of non-existence', for the vague essence and the relation are shared, so non-existence has a reality.

His saying: (and a quiddity) is an explanatory apposition to indicate that what is meant by 'reality' is not the existent quiddity.

His saying: (otherwise that thing would have no opposite) because the opposite is either the removal of the thing or something more specific than it.

His saying: (it would have a reality) – Saif al-Din al-Abhari said: There is consideration in this, because non-reality is distinct from reality and non-identity is distinct from identity, even though they have no reality and identity. And it was replied that non-reality is a generic reality different from generic realities to which the name 'reality' applies; it has conceptual individuals which are a style of realities, and there is no impossibility in it.

His saying: (otherwise that thing would have no opposite) because the opposite of a thing is either its removal, like non-existence for existence, or something that necessitates its removal, like existence for non-existence. In either case, the lack of possibility of its removal entails the non-occurrence of the opposite.

His saying: (otherwise) i.e., if the mind does not have the power to negate it, (existence would be negated). And if the mind has the power to negate it, its negation is a (particular) non-existence because it is added to the reality of non-existence. So it is a part of absolute non-existence, namely this particular non-existence, and it is a co-ordinate (qasīm) of it because it is its removal which is opposite to it. This is a contradiction (khalaf), because a part of a thing is more specific than it, and its co-ordinate is distinct from it, so it is impossible for both to be true of one thing.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is the second of four aspects (wujūh) that argue against the claim that the non-existent (al-maʿdūm) is inconceivable. The argument begins from the common statement 'Everything either exists or does not exist'. This disjunction presupposes a distinction between the non-existent and existence, because without distinction we could not judge a separation. If the non-existent were distinct, it must have a reality (ḥaqīqa) and a quiddity (māhiyya) by which it is distinguished. Moreover, the mind would then be able to negate or remove that reality, because anything with a reality can be negated; otherwise it would have no opposite. If the mind could not negate the reality of non-existence, then non-existence would have no opposite (existence), which is absurd. The commentator clarifies a subtlety: the beginning of the passage speaks of the non-existent (maʿdūm) and the end speaks of non-existence (ʿadam); these must be harmonized, either by taking 'non-existence' to mean 'non-existent' or by adding that the non-existent has no distinction except by consideration of non-existence – so non-existence has a reality. The commentary then explains that 'quiddity' is an explanatory apposition to stress that 'reality' here does not mean an existent quiddity. The phrase 'otherwise that thing would have no opposite' is explained: the opposite of a thing is either its removal (as non-existence is the opposite of existence) or something that necessitates its removal. If the mind could not negate the reality of non-existence, then non-existence would have no opposite (existence), which is contradictory. The commentator then reports an objection from Saif al-Din al-Abhari: non-reality and non-identity are distinct from reality and identity even though they have no reality/identity, so the argument is questionable. The reply is that non-reality is a generic reality (ḥaqīqa nawʿiyya) different from other generic realities; it has conceptual individuals and there is no impossibility. The final commentary on the second 'إلا' shows the logical structure: if the mind cannot negate the reality of non-existence, existence would be negated; if it can, that negation is a particular non-existence which is part of absolute non-existence yet also its co-ordinate, leading to contradiction because a part and its co-ordinate cannot be identical.

Key Terms
الوجه الثاني (second aspect)
المعدوم (non-existent)
الوجود (existence)
حقيقة (reality)
ماهية (quiddity)
سلب (negation)
رفع (removal)
مقابل (opposite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The non-existent has no reality whatsoever; it is pure nothing.
Correction: The passage argues that if the non-existent were pure nothing without distinction, it could not be opposed to existence. Therefore, it must have some reality—a conceptual or quidditative reality—even if not physically existent.

Checkpoint
What does the disjunction 'Everything either exists or does not exist' presuppose, according to the argument?
A) That existence and non-existence are the same thing.
B) That the non-existent is distinct from existence.
C) That the mind cannot conceive non-existence.
D) That the non-existent has no reality.

Key Takeaway
The simple disjunction between existence and non-existence forces us to grant that the non-existent has a reality, because without distinction opposites cannot exist.

Refining the Concept: Harmonizing Non-Existent and Non-Existence
The commentator harmonizes terminology, explains key concepts, and defends against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator harmonizes the terms 'non-existent' and 'non-existence' and addresses the objection that non-reality is distinct from reality.

Core Idea
The reality of non-existence is a generic reality, not an existent one.

After the core argument, the commentator clarifies a subtle shift in language: the beginning of the passage speaks about 'the non-existent' (maʿdūm) while the end speaks about 'non-existence' (ʿadam). He also explains that 'reality' here does not mean an existent quiddity, and then reports an objection from al-Abhari that non-reality is distinct from reality without having a reality. The response shows how non-reality can be considered a generic reality. This lesson unpacks these refinements and defenses.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Read the Arabic commentary and its translation. Notice that it is a line-by-line explanation (sharh) of the original argument.
The commentary is by the author himself or a later commentator; it clarifies and defends.
2
Understand the harmonization of terms
The commentator notes that the beginning and end of the passage seem to discuss different things: 'non-existent' vs 'non-existence'. He proposes two ways to harmonize them: either interpret 'non-existence' as 'non-existent' or add that the non-existent's distinction comes only through considering non-existence.
3
Examine the explanation of 'reality' and 'quiddity'
The phrase 'and a quiddity' is an explanatory apposition, meaning that 'reality' here does not refer to an actually existent quiddity but to a conceptual one.
4
Follow the objection and reply
Al-Abhari objects: non-reality is distinct from reality even though it has no reality. The reply: non-reality is a generic reality (ḥaqīqa nawʿiyya) different from other realities; it is conceptually real.
5
Grasp the final logical refinement
The commentator explains that if the mind cannot negate the reality of non-existence, existence would be negated; if it can, that negation is a particular non-existence which is both a part of absolute non-existence and its co-ordinate—a contradiction if not properly understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الثاني) من تلك الوجوه الأربعة ( أنه ) أي قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون ) يقتضي تميز المعدوم عن الوجود ) إذ لولا تميزه عنه لما أمكن الحكم بالانفصال بينهما ( ولو كان المعدوم ( متميزاً لكان له حقيقة) وماهية بها يمتاز عن الوجود (و) كان للعقل سلبها أي سلب تلك الحقيقة ورفعها فإن كل ما له حقيقة يشير العقل إليها يمكنه ،رفعها وإلا لم يكن لذلك الشيء مقابل فلو لم يكن للعقل رفع حقيقة العدم لم يكن لها مقابل هو الوجود، وهذا معنى

قوله : ( الوجه الثاني إلخ لا يخفى أن أوله يدل على أن الكلام في المعدوم، وآخره على في العدم فلا بد من التطبيق بأن يقال : المراد بالعدم المعدوم أو يضم لقوله، ولو كان المعدوم متميزاً قولنا ولا تميز له إلا باعتبار ،العدم إذ الذات المبهمة والنسبة مشتركتان فيكون للعدم حقيقة .

قوله : ( وماهية) عطف تفسيري للإشارة إلى أنه ليس المراد بالحقيقة الماهية الموجودة .

قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن لذلك الشيء مقابل لأن المقابل إما رفع الشيء أو أخص منه .

قوله : ( لكان له حقيقة قال سيف الدين الأبهري : فيه نظر لأن اللاحقيقة متميزة عن الحقيقة واللاهوية متميزة عن الهوية، مع أنه ليس لهما حقيقة وهوية ورد بأن اللاحقيقة حقيقة نوعية مغايرة للحقائق النوعية الصادق على كلها اسم الحقيقة لها أفراد اعتبارية هي أسلوب الحقائق ولا استحالة فيه.

قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن لذلك الشيء مقابل لأن مقابل الشيء إما رفعه كالعدم للوجود، أو ملزوم رفعه كالوجود للعدم وعلى كل تقدير يلزم من عدم إمكان رفعها عدم تحقق المقابل .

قوله ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن للعقل سلبها ( انتفى الوجود ) وإذا كان للعقل سلبها وسلبها عدم (خاص) لكونه مضافاً إلى حقيقة العدم ) فقسم من العدم المطلق وهو هذا العدم الخاص (قسيم (له لأنه رفعه الذي يقابله ( هذا خلف ) لأن قسم الشيء أخص منه، وقسيمه مباين له فيستحيل صدقهما على شيء واحد .
The second aspect of those four aspects (is that) i.e., our saying 'Everything either exists or does not exist' necessitates the distinction of the non-existent from existence, for if it were not distinct from it, it would not be possible to judge the separation between them. And if the non-existent were distinct, it would have a reality (ḥaqīqa) and a quiddity (māhiyya) by which it is distinguished from existence, and the mind would have the power to negate it, i.e., to deny and remove that reality. For everything that has a reality to which the mind points, it is possible to remove it; otherwise, that thing would have no opposite. So if the mind did not have the power to remove the reality of non-existence, it would not have an opposite which is existence. And this is the meaning.

His saying: (The second aspect, etc.) It is not hidden that its beginning indicates that the discussion is about the non-existent (al-maʿdūm) and its end about non-existence (al-ʿadam), so application must be made by saying: what is meant by non-existence is the non-existent, or one may add to his saying 'if the non-existent were distinct' the statement 'and it has no distinction except by consideration of non-existence', for the vague essence and the relation are shared, so non-existence has a reality.

His saying: (and a quiddity) is an explanatory apposition to indicate that what is meant by 'reality' is not the existent quiddity.

His saying: (otherwise that thing would have no opposite) because the opposite is either the removal of the thing or something more specific than it.

His saying: (it would have a reality) – Saif al-Din al-Abhari said: There is consideration in this, because non-reality is distinct from reality and non-identity is distinct from identity, even though they have no reality and identity. And it was replied that non-reality is a generic reality different from generic realities to which the name 'reality' applies; it has conceptual individuals which are a style of realities, and there is no impossibility in it.

His saying: (otherwise that thing would have no opposite) because the opposite of a thing is either its removal, like non-existence for existence, or something that necessitates its removal, like existence for non-existence. In either case, the lack of possibility of its removal entails the non-occurrence of the opposite.

His saying: (otherwise) i.e., if the mind does not have the power to negate it, (existence would be negated). And if the mind has the power to negate it, its negation is a (particular) non-existence because it is added to the reality of non-existence. So it is a part of absolute non-existence, namely this particular non-existence, and it is a co-ordinate (qasīm) of it because it is its removal which is opposite to it. This is a contradiction (khalaf), because a part of a thing is more specific than it, and its co-ordinate is distinct from it, so it is impossible for both to be true of one thing.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is the second of four aspects (wujūh) that argue against the claim that the non-existent (al-maʿdūm) is inconceivable. The argument begins from the common statement 'Everything either exists or does not exist'. This disjunction presupposes a distinction between the non-existent and existence, because without distinction we could not judge a separation. If the non-existent were distinct, it must have a reality (ḥaqīqa) and a quiddity (māhiyya) by which it is distinguished. Moreover, the mind would then be able to negate or remove that reality, because anything with a reality can be negated; otherwise it would have no opposite. If the mind could not negate the reality of non-existence, then non-existence would have no opposite (existence), which is absurd. The commentator clarifies a subtlety: the beginning of the passage speaks of the non-existent (maʿdūm) and the end speaks of non-existence (ʿadam); these must be harmonized, either by taking 'non-existence' to mean 'non-existent' or by adding that the non-existent has no distinction except by consideration of non-existence – so non-existence has a reality. The commentary then explains that 'quiddity' is an explanatory apposition to stress that 'reality' here does not mean an existent quiddity. The phrase 'otherwise that thing would have no opposite' is explained: the opposite of a thing is either its removal (as non-existence is the opposite of existence) or something that necessitates its removal. If the mind could not negate the reality of non-existence, then non-existence would have no opposite (existence), which is contradictory. The commentator then reports an objection from Saif al-Din al-Abhari: non-reality and non-identity are distinct from reality and identity even though they have no reality/identity, so the argument is questionable. The reply is that non-reality is a generic reality (ḥaqīqa nawʿiyya) different from other generic realities; it has conceptual individuals and there is no impossibility. The final commentary on the second 'إلا' shows the logical structure: if the mind cannot negate the reality of non-existence, existence would be negated; if it can, that negation is a particular non-existence which is part of absolute non-existence yet also its co-ordinate, leading to contradiction because a part and its co-ordinate cannot be identical.

حقيقة نوعية (generic reality)
اللاحقيقة (non-reality)
اللاهوية (non-identity)
قسم (part)
قسيم (co-ordinate)
خلف (contradiction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-reality (lā-ḥaqīqa) cannot be a reality because it is a negation.
Correction: The commentator explains that non-reality is a generic reality (ḥaqīqa nawʿiyya) that falls under the category of realities in a conceptual sense.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the objection that non-reality is distinct from reality without having a reality?
A) He denies that non-reality is distinct.
B) He says non-reality is a generic reality with conceptual individuals.
C) He changes the definition of reality to exclude non-reality.
D) He argues that the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The commentator shows that technical terms like 'reality' can apply conceptually to negations, and apparent contradictions can be resolved by careful harmonization and understanding of generic realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator works hard to harmonize a logical tension between 'non-existent' and 'non-existence'. Where in your own life have you avoided confronting a similar tension between what you say you believe and how you actually live? Will you this week sit down and write out that inconsistency honestly before God?
?
If 'non-existence' can have a conceptual reality, then the temporary nature of this world is a real truth. Do you act as if your possessions, status, and even your life are real in the same way God's existence is real? What would change if you truly treated their non-existence as a present reality?
?
Al-Abhari's objection was answered by distinguishing a 'generic reality'. Are you dismissing a spiritual truth—like the reality of the Hereafter—because you think it has no 'existent quiddity'? Will you commit to meditating on that truth until it feels as concrete as the ground beneath your feet?

The Disjunction and the Problem of Predication
The author argues that the disjunction 'either exists or does not' is invalid due to the problem of predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the disjunction 'either exists or does not exist' is problematic when taken as a predication about a thing's own existence, and why the first of its two possible meanings is invalid.

Core Idea
Predication requires distinction; identifying existence with essence makes predication empty, making it other, self-contradictory.

The author presents the third of four arguments against the basic existential disjunction. He shows that the disjunction can only be understood in two ways, both of which fail. The first way, treating existence as predicated of the thing itself, leads to a dilemma: either existence is identical to the thing (useless predication) or it is something else (making the thing non-existent in itself). This reveals a fundamental paradox in how we talk about existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument. Notice the structure: first the two meanings are stated, then the first is explained and shown to be invalid.
The Arabic ends abruptly; focus on the part that is present.
2
Identify the two meanings of the disjunction
The disjunction between existence and non-existence can be understood either 'in itself' (e.g., blackness exists or not) or 'for something else' (e.g., a body is black or not). The author says only these two are conceivable.
These are the two possible interpretations.
3
Understand the invalidity of the first meaning
For the 'in itself' disjunction, the affirmation side ('blackness exists') is problematic: existence is either identical to blackness (predication is useless) or something else (then blackness in itself is non-existent). Both options are problematic.
The passage explains why neither side of the dilemma is acceptable.
4
Consider the commentary's clarification
The commentary explains that 'not intelligible' means not conceivable in a sound manner that corresponds to reality, not absolutely inconceivable. It also delves into the requirement of distinction for predication.
This deepens the understanding of the logical issue.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
This argument challenges even everyday predications like 'fire exists' if taken as a disjunction about the thing itself. It shows the difficulty of using existence as a predicate.
Think about how this connects to the overarching theme of the work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الثالث) من تلك الأربعة أن قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون فيه ترديد بين الثبوت والعدم، فنقول : (المردد فيه في قولنا هذا ) ثبوت الشيء وعدمه إما في نفسه ) فيكون ( كقولنا السواد إما موجود أو (لا) أي ليس بموجود ( وإما لغيره ) فيكون ( كقولنا الجسم إما أسود أو (لا ولا يتصور هاهنا معنى سوى هذين المعنيين ( وكلاهما باطل فالأول وهو أن يكون الترديد بين وجود الشيء وعدمه في نفسه كما في قولنا السواد إما موجود أو لا باطل ( لأنه لا يعقل شيء من طرفيه ) أي لا يتصور من شيء منهما معنى صحيح ( أما الثبوت) وهو قولنا السواد موجود (فلان وجود الشيء إما نفسه فلا يفيد حمله عليه ) بل يكون حينئذ قولنا السواد موجود عارياً عن

قوله : ( الوجه الثالث إلخ) لا يخفى عليك أن هذا الوجه يدل على امتناع الحمل مطلقاً، فيكون قادحاً في الأحكام الحسية أيضاً مثل النار موجودة حارة، لعل القادحين في البديهيات لا يعترفون من الحسيات إلا التصورات الحسية دون أحكامها إذ الحاكم فيها العقل ولا شهادة لمتهم .

قوله : ( أي لا يتصور إلخ) أي ليس المراد نفي التعقل مطلقاً، إذ الباطل أيضاً يمكن تعقله بل المراد التعقل على وجه يصح ويمكن مطابقته .

قوله : (إما) نفسه إذا لم يعتبر التغاير بين الشيء ونفسه بوجه من الوجوه لا يمكن الحمل أصلاً، لأن النسبة تقتضي تغاير الطرفين ولو بوجه، وأما إذا اعتبر التغاير بوجه يمكن الحمل لكن يكون عارياً عن الفائدة، فلا بد أن يراد بقوله : إما نفسه نفسه بحسب الذات والماهية ليترتب عليه قوله : فلا يفيد وبقوله وأما غيره بحسب الذات ليترتب عليه قوله : فهو في نفسه معدوم ولا يجوز أن يراد به نفسه من جميع الوجوه وبغيره غيره بوجه من الوجوه لعدم صحة ترتب شيء منهما كما لا يخفى، ولم يتعرض لكونه جزءاً لعدم ذهاب أحد إليه مع أنه يلزمه كلا الأمرين عدم الإفادة، وكونه معدوماً في نفسه أي مع قطع النظر عن ذلك الجزء.

قوله : (فلا يفيد حمله ) أي لا مواطأة ولا اشتقاقاً إذ لا فائدة في قولنا السواد ذو نفسه، وإن باعتبار التغاير الاعتباري والاختلاف في أن الوجود موجود أولاً ليس بمعنى أنه متصف بنفسه أو لا، بل بمعنى أنه متصف بوجود خاص أولا .

صح

قوله : (إما نفسه فلا يفيد حمله عليه ) قد يمنع ذلك بأن النسبة بين الشيء ونفسه اشتقاقاً مما تفيد، ولهذا يحتاج إلى البيان بل يصير مبحثاً للعقلاء يتنازعون فيها نفياً وإثباتاً، فإن النسبة بين الوجود ونفسه اشتقاقاً معركة للآراء، حيث ذهب أكثر المتكلمين إلى أن الوجود موجود،
The third aspect of those four is that our saying 'Everything either exists or does not exist' involves a disjunction between affirmation (thubūt) and non-existence (ʿadam). So we say: That which is disjuncted in this statement is the affirmation of a thing and its non-existence: either in itself, like our saying 'Blackness either exists or not' i.e., is not existent, or for something else, like our saying 'A body is either black or not'. And no meaning is conceivable here besides these two meanings. And both are invalid. The first, namely that the disjunction is between a thing's existence and non-existence in itself, as in our saying 'Blackness either exists or not', is invalid because nothing of its two sides is intelligible – i.e., no correct meaning is conceivable from either of them. As for affirmation (thubūt), which is our saying 'Blackness exists', then the existence of a thing is either [identical to] itself, so predicating it upon itself is useless, or other than itself, so it would be non-existent in itself (and this is the second branch? Actually, the text is cut: 'بل يكون حينئذ قولنا السواد موجود عارياً عن' – then the commentary continues.

His saying: (The third aspect, etc.) It is not hidden to you that this aspect indicates the impossibility of predication (ḥaml) absolutely, so it becomes an objection also against sensory judgments like 'Fire exists and is hot'. Perhaps the objectors against self-evident truths do not acknowledge from sensory things except sensory concepts, not their judgments, because the judge is the intellect and there is no testimony for them.

His saying: (i.e., not conceivable, etc.) What is meant is not the negation of intellectual comprehension absolutely, for the false also can be comprehended; rather, what is meant is comprehension in a manner that is sound and can correspond to reality.

His saying: ('itself') If no distinction is considered between a thing and itself in any respect, predication is impossible at all because the relation requires a distinction between the two sides, even in one respect. And if distinction is considered in a respect, predication is possible but devoid of benefit. So what is meant by his saying 'itself' must be itself with respect to essence and quiddity so that his saying 'so it does not benefit' follows from it; and by 'other than itself' with respect to essence so that his saying 'so it is non-existent in itself' follows. And it is not permissible to mean by 'itself' in all respects and by 'other than itself' other in a single respect, because neither consequence would be valid, as is not hidden. And he did not discuss its being a part because no one holds that, although it would entail both lack of benefit and its being non-existent in itself (i.e., abstracted from that part).

His saying: (so predicating it upon it does not benefit) i.e., neither as equational (muwāṭaʾa) nor derivative (ishtiqāq), for there is no benefit in our saying 'Blackness is itself'. And by considering a relational distinction and the difference in that 'existence is existent' is not in the sense that it is qualified by itself or not, but in the sense that it is qualified by a particular existence or not.

His saying: ('itself' so predicating it upon it does not benefit) – this may be objected by saying that the relation between a thing and itself derivatively (ishtiqāqan) does benefit, and therefore it needs explanation; indeed it becomes a topic of debate among rational beings disputing over its affirmation or negation. For the relation between existence and itself derivatively is a battleground of opinions, as most theologians (mutakallimūn) hold that existence is existent.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents the third of four aspects arguing against the disjunction 'either exists or does not exist'. The author states that this disjunction can be understood in only two ways: either the disjunction is between the thing's existence and non-existence in itself (e.g., 'Blackness either exists or does not'), or it is with respect to something else (e.g., 'A body is either black or not'). Both are invalid. The focus is on the first: if we say 'Blackness exists', the predicate 'exists' either is identical to blackness itself or is something else. If identical, then predication (ḥaml) is useless because the subject and predicate are the same (like saying 'blackness is itself'). If existence is something else, then blackness in itself (apart from that existence) is non-existent, which also is problematic. The commentator expands: this aspect shows the impossibility of predication altogether, and thus it also undermines sensory judgments like 'Fire exists and is hot', unless one denies that such sensory judgments involve intellectual predication. The commentary clarifies that 'not intelligible' does not mean absolutely inconceivable (the false can be conceived), but rather not conceivable in a way that could be soundly matched to reality. The commentator then explains the phrase 'itself' (nafsuh): if no distinction at all is made between a thing and itself, predication is impossible because it requires a distinction between subject and predicate. If a distinction is made, predication is possible but empty. So the intended meaning is that 'itself' refers to the essence and quiddity, leading to useless predication, while 'other than itself' refers to a different essence, leading to the thing being non-existent in itself. The commentator also notes that some might object that a thing can be predicated of itself in a derivative sense (ishtiqāq) and that this is debated; for example, most theologians hold that 'existence is existent'. This passage highlights the deep logical problems that arise from taking the existential disjunction as a basic principle.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying 'Blackness exists' is a simple, unproblematic predication.
Correction: This argument shows that it leads to a dilemma: either the predicate is identical to the subject (useless) or it is something else (making the subject non-existent in itself).

Checkpoint
According to the third aspect, why is the first meaning of the disjunction (between existence and non-existence in itself) invalid?
A) Because the non-existent cannot be conceived.
B) Because its two sides are both unintelligible in a sound sense.
C) Because existence is always predicated of something else.
D) Because the disjunction is self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The first meaning of the existential disjunction fails because the affirmation side leads to an irresolvable dilemma: existence either adds nothing or makes the thing non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that God exists, do you ever stop to consider what you are actually predicating? If existence is identical to God's essence, then saying 'God exists' is redundant; if distinct, then you are implying God's essence is something other than existence. How does this dilemma affect the way you conceptualize your Creator, and what does it demand of your faith?
?
The commentator notes that this argument challenges even sensory judgments like 'fire exists'. Are there things you take for granted as existing simply because you experience them, without considering the logical structure of your judgment? How might this lesson change your awareness of the act of making any existential claim, especially about the unseen?
?
This argument shows that predication requires a real distinction between subject and predicate. In your daily life, when you describe something as 'good' or 'true', are you assuming a separation between the thing and its attribute? What does this imply about the relationship between an attribute and its subject in your own understanding of reality, and how might it lead you to a more careful use of language?
