The Third Aspect: Refuting Existence as Essence
The author refutes the first alternative in the third aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third aspect of the argument against existence being the essence, and why declaring a thing's existence is useless if existence is identical to the thing.

Core Idea
Saying 'black exists' is pointless if existence is black itself.

In this passage, the author presents the third of four aspects in an argument against the claim that existence is the essence of a thing. He shows that the common disjunctive expression 'the thing either exists or does not exist' leads to absurdity if taken in the sense of existence being the essence. Let's trace the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Focus on the structure: disjunction between two alternatives.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents a disjunction: either the existence of a thing is in itself (e.g., black exists) or in relation to something else (e.g., body is black). Notice how each alternative is defined.
The author declares both invalid but only refutes the first in this passage.
3
Understand why the first alternative is invalid
The first alternative (existence in itself) is invalid because neither side is rationally sound. The affirmative side implies existence is the thing itself, making the predication useless.
The commentary clarifies that this is not about conceptualization but sound conceptualization.
4
Reflect on the commentary's nuance
The commentary explains that without any difference between subject and predicate, predication is impossible; with a conceptual difference, predication becomes possible but useless. This reinforces the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الثالث) من تلك الأربعة أن قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون فيه ترديد بين الثبوت والعدم، فنقول : (المردد فيه في قولنا هذا ) ثبوت الشيء وعدمه إما في نفسه ) فيكون ( كقولنا السواد إما موجود أو (لا) أي ليس بموجود ( وإما لغيره ) فيكون ( كقولنا الجسم إما أسود أو (لا ولا يتصور هاهنا معنى سوى هذين المعنيين ( وكلاهما باطل فالأول وهو أن يكون الترديد بين وجود الشيء وعدمه في نفسه كما في قولنا السواد إما موجود أو لا باطل ( لأنه لا يعقل شيء من طرفيه ) أي لا يتصور من شيء منهما معنى صحيح ( أما الثبوت) وهو قولنا السواد موجود (فلان وجود الشيء إما نفسه فلا يفيد حمله عليه ) بل يكون حينئذ قولنا السواد موجود عارياً عن الفائدة. قوله : ( الوجه الثالث إلخ) لا يخفى عليك أن هذا الوجه يدل على امتناع الحمل مطلقاً، فيكون قادحاً في الأحكام الحسية أيضاً مثل النار موجودة حارة، لعل القادحين في البديهيات لا يعترفون من الحسيات إلا التصورات الحسية دون أحكامها إذ الحاكم فيها العقل ولا شهادة لمتهم. قوله : ( أي لا يتصور إلخ) أي ليس المراد نفي التعقل مطلقاً، إذ الباطل أيضاً يمكن تعقله بل المراد التعقل على وجه يصح ويمكن مطابقته. قوله : (إما) نفسه إذا لم يعتبر التغاير بين الشيء ونفسه بوجه من الوجوه لا يمكن الحمل أصلاً، لأن النسبة تقتضي تغاير الطرفين ولو بوجه، وأما إذا اعتبر التغاير بوجه يمكن الحمل لكن يكون عارياً عن الفائدة، فلا بد أن يراد بقوله : إما نفسه نفسه بحسب الذات والماهية ليترتب عليه قوله : فلا يفيد وبقوله وأما غيره بحسب الذات ليترتب عليه قوله : فهو في نفسه معدوم ولا يجوز أن يراد به نفسه من جميع الوجوه وبغيره غيره بوجه من الوجوه لعدم صحة ترتب شيء منهما كما لا يخفى، ولم يتعرض لكونه جزءاً لعدم ذهاب أحد إليه مع أنه يلزمه كلا الأمرين عدم الإفادة، وكونه معدوماً في نفسه أي مع قطع النظر عن ذلك الجزء. قوله : (فلا يفيد حمله ) أي لا مواطأة ولا اشتقاقاً إذ لا فائدة في قولنا السواد ذو نفسه، وإن باعتبار التغاير الاعتباري والاختلاف في أن الوجود موجود أولاً ليس بمعنى أنه متصف بنفسه أو لا، بل بمعنى أنه متصف بوجود خاص أولا.
The third aspect of those four: that our saying 'the thing either is or is not' is a disjunction between affirmation and negation. So we say: the disjunct in our saying 'this' is the affirmation of the thing and its negation, either in itself, like our saying 'black either exists or does not exist', or in relation to something else, like our saying 'the body either is black or is not'. And no meaning other than these two meanings is conceivable. Both are invalid. The first—that the disjunction is between the existence of the thing and its non-existence in itself, as in our saying 'black either exists or does not exist'—is invalid, because neither of its two alternatives is rationally conceivable, i.e., no sound meaning is conceivable from either of them. As for affirmation, which is our saying 'black exists', it is because the existence of the thing is either itself, so predicating it upon it is useless; rather, then our saying 'black exists' would be devoid of benefit. His saying: 'The third aspect, etc.'—it is not hidden from you that this aspect indicates the impossibility of predication absolutely, so it becomes an objection against sensory judgments as well, such as 'fire is existent and hot'. Perhaps the objectors against self-evident truths do not acknowledge from sensory things except sensory concepts, not their judgments, since the judge in them is the intellect, and there is no testimony from them. His saying: 'i.e., not rationally conceivable, etc.'—i.e., the intention is not the negation of conceptualization absolutely, for the false can also be conceptualized; rather, the intention is conceptualization in a manner that is sound and possible to match. His saying: 'itself'—if no difference between a thing and itself is considered in any respect, predication is impossible at all, because the relation requires a difference between the two sides in some respect. But if a difference is considered in some respect, predication is possible but devoid of benefit. So it must be that what is meant by 'itself' in his saying is 'itself' in terms of essence and quiddity so that his saying 'it does not benefit' follows, and by 'other' is meant 'other' in terms of essence so that his saying 'it is in itself non-existent' follows. It is not valid that it be meant as 'itself' in all respects and 'other' as other in any respect, because neither follows correctly, as is not hidden. And he did not mention the case of being a part because no one holds it, although if held it would entail both: lack of benefit and it being non-existent in itself, i.e., disregarding that part. His saying: 'predicating it does not benefit'—i.e., neither by identity nor by derivation, for there is no benefit in our saying 'black is identical to itself'. And if considering the conceptual difference and the disagreement about whether existence is existent first, it is not in the sense that it is qualified by itself or not, but rather in the sense that it is qualified by a special existence.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It presents the third aspect of an argument concerning the disjunction between existence and non-existence. The author first states that in predication, the disjunction is either in itself (e.g., 'black exists or does not') or in relation to something else (e.g., 'the body is black or not'). He then declares both invalid. The first alternative (disjunction in itself) is invalid because neither side yields a coherent meaning: the affirmative side would require that existence is the essence of the thing, making the predication pointless. The commentary clarifies that this argument actually challenges all predication, including sensory judgments, unless one denies that reason judges in sensory matters. It then explains the nuance: 'not rationally conceivable' does not mean not conceptualizable at all, but not soundly so. The commentary also elaborates that if no difference is considered between subject and predicate, predication is impossible; if a difference is considered, predication becomes possible but useless. It stresses that the intended meaning of 'itself' is in terms of essence, to make the argument work. Finally, it explains that the lack of benefit applies both to identity predication (مواطأة) and derivation (اشتقاق).

الثبوت
العدم
الحمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that predication of a thing of itself (e.g., 'black is black') is informative.
Correction: The argument shows that if existence is identical to the thing, saying 'black exists' conveys no new information and is thus useless.

Checkpoint
What is the first alternative in the third aspect argument?
A) That existence is the essence of the thing.
B) That existence is other than the thing.
C) That existence is a part of the thing.
D) That existence is a universal.

Key Takeaway
Declaring a thing's existence is meaningless if existence is taken to be identical to the thing itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists', do you ever stop to consider whether your words are conveying real meaning or just a tautology? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the substance behind your profession of faith?
?
Think of a belief you hold about your own existence or identity. Is there a statement you repeat that might be empty of real information? Will you examine one such statement today and ask yourself what it truly means?
?
This passage shows that even a grammatically correct sentence can be pointless. Where in your daily life do you use language carelessly, assuming that because it sounds right it must be meaningful? What will you do to ensure your words carry real weight?

Refutation of the Second Alternative: Existence as Other than Essence
Sanusi refutes the second alternative with two reasons and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments Sanusi presents to refute the claim that existence is other than the essence.

Core Idea
If existence is separate from essence, the essence is either non-existent or requires an infinite regress.

Sanusi now tackles the second alternative in the debate about existence and essence—that existence is something added to and distinct from the essence. He gives two devastating reasons why this view fails. The first shows that it would make the thing non-existent, and the second shows it leads to an infinite chain of existences. This passage is a classic example of logical refutation in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Let the argument flow before breaking it down.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two reasons
The author gives two reasons for the falsity of the second alternative. Locate where each reason begins in the text.
The first reason is indicated by the phrase 'فهو في نفسه معدوم' and the second by 'وإلا عاد الكلام'.
3
Understand the first reason
The first reason: if existence is other than the essence, then the essence itself would be non-existent, because its existence comes from something else. This is absurd.
Consider the example of blackness: if its existence is separate, blackness in itself would not exist.
4
Understand the second reason and the objection
The second reason: if we avoid the first conclusion by saying the thing exists by itself, then we need a third existence, leading to infinite regress. The objection about 'true particular' is refuted by noting that predication here is general.
The regress is invalid because an infinite chain of existences is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما غيره وهذا أيضاً باطل لوجهين أشار إلى أولهما بقوله ( فهو) أي ذلك الشيء كالسواد مثلا ( في نفسه معدوم) على تقدير مغايرة الوجود إياه ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن معدوماً في نفسه على ذلك التقدير بل كان موجوداً ( عاد الكلام ) فيه إلى ذلك الوجود فيقال : هو إما أن يكون نفس الشيء وهو باطل لما مر أو غيره فالشيء معدوم في نفسه، إذ لو كان موجوداً عاد الكلام إلى الوجود الثالث فإما أن يثبت المدعي أو تتسلسل الوجودات إلى غير النهاية والتسلسل باطل فتعين. قوله : ( بل كان موجوداً) ولو بالتبع لكونه مقابلاً للمعدوم في نفسه، فيتناول الحال أيضاً، ويعود الكلام إلى ذلك الوجود الذي هو بالتبع إما نفسه أو غيره إلخ، فيثبت المدعي وكونه معدوماً في نفسه أو تتسلسل الوجودات، وما قيل : إنه يجوز أن ينتهي إلى وجود خاص هو عينه، وهو جزئي حقيقي، فيمتنع حمله على الشيء كما حققه الشارح قدس سره في كتبه، ولا يكون الشيء معدوماً في نفسه، لأنه موجود بوجود هو نفسه، فمندفع بأن الترديد في قولنا فلان وجود الشيء إما نفسه، أو غيره في الوجود المحمول في قولنا السواد موجود الذي به صار الشيء موجوداً لكونه في مقابلة المعدوم على أن الجزئي الحقيقي إنما يمتنع حمله مواطأة لا اشتقاقاً والمراد بالحمل هاهنا أعم كما مر
And the second [alternative] is other than it, and this is also invalid for two reasons. He indicated the first of them with his saying: 'it'—i.e., that thing, like black for example—'is in itself non-existent' on the assumption that existence is other than it. 'Otherwise'—i.e., if it were not non-existent in itself on that assumption but rather was existent—'the discussion would return' to that existence, and it would be said: it is either the thing itself—which is invalid as before—or other than it, so the thing is non-existent in itself; for if it were existent, the discussion would return to the third existence, so either the claimant's position is established or there is a regress of existences ad infinitum, and regress is invalid, so it is determined. His saying: 'rather, it was existent'—even if by following, because it is opposite to non-existent in itself. So it includes the state as well, and the discussion returns to that existence which is by following, either itself or other, etc. So either the claimant's position is established and it is non-existent in itself, or there is a regress of existences. And what is said—that it is possible to terminate at a special existence that is identical to it, a true particular, so that predicating it upon the thing is impossible, as the commentator (may God sanctify his secret) verified in his books, and the thing would not be non-existent in itself because it exists by an existence that is itself—is refuted by the fact that the disjunction in our saying 'so-and-so, the existence of the thing is either itself or other' concerns the existence predicated in our saying 'black exists', by which the thing becomes existent because it is opposite to the non-existent. Moreover, a true particular is impossible to predicate only by identity, not by derivation, and the intended meaning of predication here is more general, as has passed.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage refutes the second alternative, i.e., that existence is other than the essence. The author gives two reasons. The first reason: if existence is other than the thing, then the thing would be non-existent in itself because its existence is added to it. The second reason: if we suppose the thing is existent without that added existence, then we need another existence, leading to an infinite regress of existences, which is invalid. The commentator addresses an objection that there might be a special existence identical to the thing (a true particular) that cannot be predicated, so the thing would not be non-existent. He refutes this by noting that the disjunction concerns only the predicated existence, and that a true particular is only impossible in identity predication, not in derivative predication, while the meaning of predication here is general (including both). Thus, the refutation stands.

Key Terms
الوجود
العدم الخاص
التسلسل
الجزئي الحقيقي
الحمل
الاشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence can be other than the essence without any problems, as long as the thing is still considered existent.
Correction: This is false because if existence is truly other, then the essence itself must be non-existent (first reason), and if we try to avoid that by saying the essence exists by itself, we fall into an infinite regress of existences (second reason). Both consequences are impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given for the falsity of the second alternative (existence as other than essence)?
A) The thing would be non-existent in itself.
B) The predication would be useless.
C) The thing would be composed.
D) The existence would be a universal.

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be other than essence because that would either render the essence non-existent or lead to an impossible infinite regress of existences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your existence is not separate from your essence, do you truly feel that you are nothing without God's continuous creation of you? In what area of your life do you act as if your existence is self-sustained?
?
The refutation of infinite regress shows that existence must be identical to essence. How does this truth affect your understanding of your own mortality and your need for God?
?
Are there moments when you forget that your existence is a constant act of God? What habits remind you of this reality, and when is the last time you acted on that reminder?

Objection and Response: Can Self-Predication Be Informative?
Objection and response regarding self-predication's informativeness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that predication of a thing of itself by derivation can be informative, and how the kalam response defends the original argument that such predication lacks benefit.

Core Idea
Predication of a thing of itself by derivation may seem informative, but the difference is apparent.

This lesson presents a nuanced objection to the earlier claim that predicating existence of itself yields no benefit. The objector argues that even self-predication can carry meaning, illustrated by the philosophical debate over whether existence is existent. The response clarifies that such predications actually reveal a difference, thereby undermining the objection and reinforcing the original argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to how the objection is introduced with 'قوله: (إما نفسه...) قد يمنع ذلك بأن...'.
The objection claims that the relation between a thing and itself by derivation can be informative.
2
Examine the example: existence and itself
Focus on the example of existence (الوجود). The objector says that the debate over whether existence is existent shows that even self-predication can be meaningful and debatable.
Most theologians (more المتكلمين) hold that existence is existent.
3
Analyze the response and defense
The response argues that in such predications (like 'black is black') the difference is apparent, so the original argument stands. If the objector insists on no difference, then the claim of self-evident difference would be contradictory.
The commentator clarifies that 'appearance of difference' means common understanding, not necessarily self-evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إما نفسه فلا يفيد حمله عليه ) قد يمنع ذلك بأن النسبة بين الشيء ونفسه اشتقاقاً مما تفيد، ولهذا يحتاج إلى البيان بل يصير مبحثاً للعقلاء يتنازعون فيها نفياً وإثباتاً، فإن النسبة بين الوجود ونفسه اشتقاقاً معركة للآراء، حيث ذهب أكثر المتكلمين إلى أن الوجود موجود، الفائدة ) كقولك السواد سواد والموجود (موجود لكن التفاوت ظاهر، فبطل كون وجود الشيء نفسه، وقد يقال : لحن للتزم عدم التفاوت فإن ادعيت حكم البديهة بالتفاوت فقد ناقضت مطلوبك ) . قوله : ( وقد يقال : نحن نلتزم إلخ قيل : يمكن أن يقال : أن المراد بظهور التفاوت اتفاق الفاهمين عليه سواء كان ببديهية العقل أو لم يكن .
His saying: '(itself does not benefit predicating it upon it)'—it may be objected that the relation between a thing and itself by derivation can be informative, and for this reason it needs explanation; indeed, it becomes a topic of debate among rational thinkers disputing it negatively and affirmatively. For the relation between existence and itself by derivation is a battlefield of opinions, where most theologians hold that existence is existent. 'Benefit'—like your saying 'black is black' and 'existent is existent', but the difference is apparent, so the idea that existence is the thing itself is invalid. And it may be said: let us [i.e., the objector] commit to the absence of difference; if you claim that the intuition of difference is self-evident, then you contradict your own demand. His saying: '(And it may be said: we commit, etc.)'—it is said: it can be said that what is meant by 'the appearance of difference' is the agreement of those who understand it, whether by intellectual intuition or not.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response. The objector argues that the predication of a thing of itself, especially by derivation (اشتقاق), can be informative, as evidenced by the ongoing debate about whether existence is existent. The objector suggests that the lack of benefit is not a strong argument because the predication might still have some meaning. The response appears to be that in such predications like 'black is black' or 'existent is existent', the difference is apparent, so the predication is not truly of the thing itself. If the objector insists on no difference, then arguing by self-evident difference would be contradictory. The commentator then clarifies that the appearance of difference is based on common understanding, not necessarily self-evidence. The passage thus defends the original argument that predication of existence as essence is useless.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a predication is not informative, it is impossible or meaningless. The objection argues that it can be merely non-informative yet still meaningful.
Correction: The objector's argument shows that lack of benefit does not entail impossibility; the response counters that even if it seems informative, the difference is apparent.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the objection against the argument that predication of existence of itself lacks benefit?
A) That such predication can be informative by derivation.
B) That the argument is not logical.
C) That the objector does not understand the point.
D) That the predication is necessary.

Key Takeaway
Self-predication by derivation can appear informative, but the difference is perceptible, defending the original argument that existence is not a distinct attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that even experts debate self-evident matters. When have you mistaken familiarity for self-evidence, thus avoiding real reflection on what you claim to know?
?
The response insists on apparent difference. In what area of your faith do you lazily assume no difference exists between what you say and what you believe, avoiding the hard work of distinguishing between comfortable words and lived conviction? What will you do today to close that gap?

Existence and Essence: The Tautology Argument
The author argues that if existence were identical to the thing, the statement 'the thing exists' would be a useless tautology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Muslim theologians argued that existence cannot be identical to the essence of a thing, and how the problem of tautology demonstrates this.

Core Idea
Existence is not the same as the thing; otherwise all existence statements would be empty tautologies.

Before we delve into the proof, consider what it means to say 'the book exists.' If existence were just another name for the book itself, that statement would convey no new information—it would be like saying 'the book is the book.' The author uses this insight to argue against the identity of existence and essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm. Then read the full English translation to understand the argument.
2
Understand the tautology argument
Focus on the example 'blackness is blackness' and how the author uses it to show that if existence were identical to the thing, 'the existent is existent' would be a useless tautology.
3
Consider the objection and response
The passage includes an objection from a heretic who denies any difference in tautologies. Notice how the author responds by pointing out the contradiction if one appeals to intuition.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this argument is foundational for Islamic theology: if existence and essence were identical, then God's existence and His essence would be the same, which has implications for divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة) كقولك السواد سواد والموجود موجود لكن التفاوت ظاهر، فبطل كون وجود الشيء نفسه، وقد يقال: لحن للتزم عدم التفاوت فإن ادعيت حكم البديهة بالتفاوت فقد ناقضت مطلوبك)
The benefit: like your saying 'blackness is blackness' and 'the existent is existent' but the difference is apparent, so it is invalid that existence is the thing itself. And it may be said: a heretic? is committed to the lack of difference; for if you claim the judgment of intuition of difference, then you have contradicted your claim.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a proof that existence cannot be identical to the essence. The author gives an example: saying 'blackness is blackness' is a tautology where the subject and predicate are the same, yet there is apparent difference between them (since we perceive blackness as one thing and existence as another). If existence were identical to the thing, then the statement 'the thing exists' would be equivalent to 'the thing is the thing', which provides no new information and is therefore useless. An objection is raised: someone might claim that there is no difference in such tautologies. The author responds: if you claim that intuition (badīha) perceives a difference, then you have contradicted your own claim that there is no difference. Thus, the argument stands that existence cannot be the same as the thing.

Key Terms
وجود
شيء
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is identical to the essence of a thing.
Correction: If that were true, saying 'the thing exists' would be a tautology with no informative value, which contradicts our intuitive understanding that existence adds information.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider it invalid to say existence is identical to the thing?
A) Because it leads to an infinite regress of existences.
B) Because it would be a tautology like 'blackness is blackness' with no informative value.
C) Because it would imply the thing exists twice.
D) Because it contradicts the evidence of the senses.

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be identical to the essence of a thing, because that would make statements of existence empty tautologies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I exist', do you truly feel the weight of that statement, or have you become numb to the miracle of your own being, as if it were just a tautology?
?
Consider the difference between the thing and its existence. Is your relationship with your Creator based on the reality of His existence or just a concept in your mind?
?
If existence were identical to essence, then nothing would be contingent; everything would be necessary. Does your heart tremble at the thought that your existence is not necessary but granted?

Why Existence Cannot Be Other: The Infinite Regress Argument
The author presents the first proof that existence cannot be other because it leads to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that if existence were other than the thing, an infinite regress of existences would follow, proving that existence must be identical to the thing.

Core Idea
If existence is other, the thing is non-existent; to exist it needs another existence, ad infinitum.

This lesson presents the core of the first proof against the view that existence is separate from the essence. The author uses a simple conditional: if existence is other, then the thing is in itself non-existent. If we try to say it exists, we must posit another existence, leading either to a regress or a circular proof. This logical structure is foundational for the subsequent discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional statement
Read the passage focusing on the conditional: 'if existence is other, then the thing is non-existent in itself'. Notice the two possibilities: either the thing is non-existent or it exists.
2
Trace the logical consequences
If the thing is non-existent, the argument is done. If it exists, then that existence itself must be either identical to the thing or other, repeating the same reasoning, leading to a regress.
3
Recognize the conclusion
The author concludes that the only way to avoid regress is to affirm that existence is identical to the thing, thus proving the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما غيره وهذا أيضاً باطل لوجهين أشار إلى أولهما بقوله ( فهو) أي ذلك الشيء كالسواد مثلا ( في نفسه معدوم) على تقدير مغايرة الوجود إياه ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن معدوماً في نفسه على ذلك التقدير بل كان موجوداً ( عاد الكلام ) فيه إلى ذلك الوجود فيقال : هو إما أن يكون نفس الشيء وهو باطل لما مر أو غيره فالشيء معدوم في نفسه، إذ لو كان موجوداً عاد الكلام إلى الوجود الثالث فإما أن يثبت المدعي أو تتسلسل الوجودات إلى غير النهاية والتسلسل باطل فتعين قوله : ( بل كان موجوداً) ولو بالتبع لكونه مقابلاً للمعدوم في نفسه، فيتناول الحال أيضاً، ويعود الكلام إلى ذلك الوجود الذي هو بالتبع إما نفسه أو غيره إلخ، فيثبت المدعي وكونه معدوماً في نفسه أو تتسلسل الوجودات، وما قيل : إنه يجوز أن ينتهي إلى وجود خاص هو عينه، وهو جزئي حقيقي، فيمتنع حمله على الشيء كما حققه الشارح قدس سره في كتبه، ولا يكون الشيء معدوماً الوجود على السواد اشت، والحق أن الوجود إذا كان نفس السواد يكون معنى قولنا السواد موجود هذا الذات وهذا الذات والمشار إليه واحد وعدم الفائدة في هذا الحمل على تقدير صحته بديهي والمنازع مكابر والنزاع في وجود الوجود إنما في اتصاف الوجود المطلق بوجود خاص مغاير له وأما الاتصاف بمطلقه في ضمنه فاعتباري. قوله : ( وقد يقال : نحن نلتزم إلخ قيل : يمكن أن يقال : أن المراد بظهور التفاوت اتفاق الفاهمين عليه سواء كان ببديهية العقل أو لم يكن . هو قوله : ( وأما غيره) لم يذكر الجزئية وفسادها، لأن هذا الترديد جار في الأشياء البسيطة ولا احتمال للجزئية فيها على أنه يجوز أن يريد بالنفس فيها ما لا يكون غيره، فتندرج الجزئية في النفسية ويلائمه التعليل، إذ لا فائدة في قولنا : الحيوان الناطق حيوان إلا أنه إنما يظهر عند تصور السواد بالكنه فتأمل. قوله : (بل) كان موجوداً إشارة إلى أن ترتب عود الكلامن على انتفاء And as for other [than identity], this is also invalid for two reasons, the first of which he indicates by his saying: (It) i.e. that thing like blackness (is non-existent in itself) on the supposition that existence is other than it. (Otherwise) i.e. if it is not non-existent in itself on that supposition but rather exists, (the discourse returns) regarding it to that existence, and it is said: it is either the thing itself—which is invalid as previously mentioned—or other, then the thing is non-existent in itself, for if it existed, the discourse would return to the third existence. So either the claimant is proven or the existences regress infinitely, and regress is invalid, so it is settled. His saying: (Rather it existed) even as a concomitant because it is opposed to the non-existent in itself, so it includes the state as well, and the discourse returns to that existence which is concomitant, either itself or other etc., so the claimant is proven and it is non-existent in itself or the existences regress. And what is said: that it is possible for it to terminate at a particular existence which is its very self, and it is a true particular, so it is impossible to predicate it of the thing as the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) has verified in his books, and the thing is not non-existent in itself because it exists by an existence that is its self, is repelled by [the fact] that the disjunction in our saying: the existence of a thing is either itself or other concerns the predicated existence in our saying 'blackness exists' by which the thing becomes existent because it is opposite to the non-existent, and that the true particular is only impossible to predicate idem per idem, not derivationally, and the intended meaning here of predication is general as has passed. Likewise some philosophers, and most philosophers, hold that it is among the secondary intelligibles. Yes, predicating the thing itself idem per idem is not useful, but our discussion is about predicating existence of blackness. The truth is that if existence were the very blackness, the meaning of our saying 'blackness exists' would be 'this essence and this essence' and the referent is one, and the lack of benefit in this predication, supposing its correctness, is intuitive, and the opponent is obstinate. The dispute about the existence of existence is only concerning the attribution of absolute existence with a particular existence other than it; as for its attribution with its absolute [existence] within it, it is mental. His saying: (And it may be said: we commit ourselves etc.) It is said: it may be said that the intended meaning of the appearance of difference is the agreement of understanders upon it, whether by rational intuition or not. He said: (As for other) he did not mention the part and its corruption because this disjunction applies to simple things and there is no possibility of part in them, except that it is permissible for him to intend by 'itself' in them what is not other, so the part is included in the self, and the reasoning is consistent, for there is no benefit in our saying 'the rational animal is an animal' except that it appears when blackness is conceived by its essence, so reflect. His saying: (Rather) existed indicates that the arrangement of the return of discourse upon the negation of non-existence is due to its implication of existence, because blackness, for example, is among the essences and no one has said that it is a state in it. His saying: (Or the existences regress etc.) There is a discussion in it because it is permissible for that thing to exist by an existence.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage contains the first proof that existence cannot be other than the thing, followed by extensive commentary. The author states that the claim that existence is other than the thing is invalid for two reasons. The first reason is indicated by the quotation: if existence is other than the thing, then the thing (e.g., blackness) is in itself non-existent on the supposition that existence is other. If it were not non-existent but existed, we would refer back to that existence and ask whether it itself is identical to the thing or other. If identical, it is invalid as before; if other, then the thing is non-existent in itself, and we would need a third existence, leading to an infinite regress or a circular proof. The commentary then addresses objections: some might say that a particular existence which is identical to the thing could be the endpoint, but the commentator replies that such a particular cannot be predicated of the thing in the same way; the disjunction concerns predicated existence, not constitutive existence. The commentary also discusses the nature of predication, the concept of 'state' (ḥāl), and the distinction between absolute and particular existence. The passage is a dense dialectical argument ensuring that the first proof is sound against various objections.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that if existence is other than the thing, it leads to an infinite regress, so existence must be identical to the thing.

Responding to the Objection: The True Particular and Predication
The author addresses the objection of a true particular and clarifies the type of predication involved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that a particular existence identical to the thing avoids regress is refuted by the distinction between idem per idem and derivational predication.

Core Idea
A particular existence that is the thing itself cannot be predicated idem per idem, but the predication in question is derivational, so the regress stands.

This lesson addresses a sophisticated objection: perhaps the regress can stop at a particular existence that is exactly the thing itself, making predication impossible and thus avoiding the problem. The commentator's response hinges on the type of predication involved. By distinguishing between predication by identity (muwāṭaʼa) and derivational predication (ishtiqāq), he shows that the objection fails and the original proof remains valid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector says the regress could terminate at a true particular existence that is the thing's very self, so predication is impossible and the thing exists without needing another existence.
2
Identify the key distinction
The response points out that the disjunction in the argument concerns 'predicated existence' (al-wujūd al-maḥmūl), not constitutive existence. The true particular is only impossible to predicate idem per idem (muwāṭaʼa), not derivationally (ishtiqāq).
3
Apply the distinction
In our statement 'blackness exists', the predication is derivational, meaning existence is attributed to blackness in a different way than identity. Therefore, the true particular objection is irrelevant.
4
Conclude the debate
The correct view is that existence is identical to the thing, and the opponent's alternative leads to regress. The discussion about absolute existence and particular existence is a separate philosophical issue.

The Second Proof: The Thing Would Exist Twice
Sanusi presents the first branch of the second proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core argument of the second proof: if existence were other than the thing, the thing would either exist twice or lead to a contradiction.

Core Idea
If existence is separate, the thing would need two existences—an absurdity.

Sanusi now presents a second proof against the Ash'ari view that existence is other than the thing. The argument is built on a dilemma: either the thing is non-existent in itself or it is not. Both branches lead to an absurdity. This lesson introduces the first branch and the initial logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Understand the supposition
Assume existence is other than the thing. Consider two possibilities: the thing is not non-existent in itself, or it is. The proof shows both lead to contradictions.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: 'if...then...' and the conclusion 'this is a contradiction'.
The parentheses indicate quotations from the original text.
3
Identify the two branches
The passage presents two branches: if the thing is not non-existent → two existences; if it is non-existent → existence must be existent, otherwise contradictories combine. Focus on the first branch for this lesson.
The second branch will be explored in the next lesson.
4
Reflect on the absurdity
Why is having two existences absurd? Because one entity cannot have two distinct existences; that would entail two different things.
This connects to the fundamental principle that a thing is identical to its existence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

موجوداً (اجتمع النقيضان) على تقدير كونه معدوماً ) أو وجد الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، إذا لم يكن موجوداً ولا معدوماً ( وفيهما ) أي في اجتماع النقيضين ووجود الواسطة (المطلوب) وهو بطلان قولنا : السواد إما موجود أو معدوم، إذ على الأول يبطل منع الجمع في هذه المنفصلة، وعلى الثاني يبطل منع الخلو فيها (فيلزم) مما ذكر من كون السواد معدوماً في نفسه، وكون الوجود موجوداً قيام الموجود) الذي هو الوجود ( بالمعدوم) الذي هو السواد مثلاً على تقدير صحة قولنا السواد موجود (فيلزم جواز في الحركات والألوان بأن يقال : هذه أمور موجودة بشهادة الحس، وقائمة بالمعدومات ( ويحصل المراد وهو بطلان حكم البداهة لأنها تحكم بأن هذه الحركات والألوان لا يجوز قيامها إلا بأمور موجودة، وأشار إلى ثانيهما بقوله (وأيضاً) قوله : ( وكان موجوداً بوجودين) بناء على أن الترديد المذكور في الوجود الذي صار به قوله: (اجتمع النقيضان إلخ) وأما لزوم المثلين على تقدير كونه موجوداً فلا يضر المستدل لأنه حينئذ يلزم تعارض القواطع وهو إحدى حججه ، وإنما تعرض لاجتماع النقيضين لأن فيه ثبوت المدعي . هو عينه، ولا يكون محمولاً عليه فإن المحمول هو الوجود المطلق، وأما الوجود الخاص الذي هو جزئي حقيقي فلا يحمل على الحقيقة كما سبق. قوله : ( لوجد ذلك الشيء مرتين فيه بحث لأن الواجب تعالى موجود بوجودين خاص هو عينه عندهم ومطلق زائد والاستحالة إنما تلزم إذا ثبت وجود ذلك الشيء بوجودين خاصين، وأما إذا كان المحمول مطلقاً، والآخر خاصاً فلا فتأمل. قوله : (اجتمع النقيضان فإن قلت : إذا كان موجوداً يلزم اجتماع المثلين فلم يجوز هذا، قلت : التجويز في نفس الأمر ممنوع وإنما المقصود الإلزام ولو سلم، فلزوم اجتماع المثلين ممنوع لجواز أن يكون موجوداً بوجود هو نفسه لا بوجود زائد، ولو سلم فقد جوز المعتزلة ذلك بخلاف اجتماع النقيضين، فليس ذلك مثله في مرتبة الاستحالة . قوله : (إذا لم يكن موجوداً ولا معدوماً لا يخفي أن فيه أيضاً اجماع النقيضين لأن الوجود إذا لم يكن موجوداً صدق سلب الوجود فاجتمع هو وسلبه سواء قيل: بالواسطة أو بالعدم بل إطلاق النقيض على العدم علي القول بالحال باعتبار استلزامه لذلك السلب، وإنما لم يتعرض له لحصول الغنية بدونه .
The claim: And also if the thing were not non-existent in itself on that supposition, that thing would be found twice and would exist by two existences—this is a contradiction. So when it is established that the thing is non-existent in itself, and existence is existent—otherwise if existence were not existent, then the two contradictories would combine on the supposition that it is non-existent, or the intermediary between existent and non-existent would be found if it is neither existent nor non-existent. And in both—i.e., in the combination of the two contradictories and the existence of the intermediary—(lies the desired result) which is the invalidity of our saying: blackness is either existent or non-existent, because on the first [alternative] the prevention of conjunction in this disjunction is invalidated, and on the second the prevention of absence in it is invalidated. So it follows from what has been mentioned—that blackness is non-existent in itself and existence is existent—that the existent (which is existence) subsists in the non-existent (which is blackness, for example) on the supposition of the correctness of our saying 'blackness exists'. So it follows that the permissibility arises in motions and colors, such that it could be said: these things are existent by sensory testimony and subsist in non-existents. And the intended goal is achieved: the invalidity of the judgment of intuition, because it judges that these motions and colors cannot subsist except in existent things. And he indicated the second of them by his saying (Also). His saying: (And it would be found by two existences) based on the fact that the aforementioned disjunction is concerning the existence by which it becomes... His saying: (The two contradictories combine etc.) As for the occurrence of two similars on the supposition that it is existent, that does not harm the arguer because then the contradictories would be in conflict, which is one of his proofs. He only mentioned the combination of contradictories because in it lies the establishment of the claim. It is identical and is not predicated of it, for the predicate is absolute existence; as for the particular existence which is a true particular, it is not predicated of the essence as previously. His saying: (That thing would be found twice) has a discussion because the Necessary Exalted exists by two existences—a particular that is His essence according to them and an absolute that is additional—and the impossibility only follows if it is established that that thing exists by two particular existences. But if the predicate is absolute and the other is particular, then not, so reflect. His saying: (The two contradictories combine) for if you say: if it is existent, then two similars would combine, so why did he permit this? I say: the permission in reality is denied; the intent is only to refute. And if conceded, the occurrence of two similars is denied because it is possible for it to exist by an existence that is itself, not by an additional existence. And if conceded, the Mu'tazila have permitted that, unlike the combination of contradictories, so it is not equivalent in rank of impossibility. His saying: (If it is neither existent nor non-existent) it is not hidden that in this there is also a combination of contradictories, because if existence is not existent, then the negation of existence is true, so it and its negation combine, whether one says by intermediary or by non-existence—rather, the application of 'contradictory' to non-existence on the view of the state is due to its implying that negation. And he did not discuss it because the need is satisfied without it.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage contains the second proof that existence cannot be other than the thing, along with detailed commentary. The argument runs: if existence is other than the thing, then on the supposition that the thing is not non-existent in itself, the thing would be found twice (once as essence, once with existence) and would have two existences, which is a contradiction. If we instead accept that the thing is non-existent in itself, then existence itself must be existent; otherwise, if existence were not existent, we would have a combination of contradictories (existence would be both existent and non-existent). This leads to the conclusion that the disjunction 'blackness is either existent or non-existent' is invalid. From this, it would follow that existent accidents (like motions and colors) could subsist in non-existent subjects, which contradicts the intuitive judgment (badīha) that they only subsist in existent subjects. The commentary then addresses several objections: the problem of two similars (if the thing is existent, why doesn't the arguer also face the problem of two similar existences?) is dismissed because the arguer's aim is to show the absurdity of the opponent's position; the Mu'tazilites might accept two similars but not the combination of contradictories. The commentary also discusses the case of the Necessary Being (God) who exists by both essential and absolute existence, but that is a special case that does not affect the argument. The passage concludes that the intuitive judgment against accidents subsisting in non-existents is preserved.

Key Terms
خلف
وجودين
معدوم
تقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a thing can have multiple existences, like an essence and an accident.
Correction: A thing's existence is either identical to it or a separate attribute. The proof shows that a separate existence would double the thing, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence if the thing is not non-existent in itself?
A) The thing would be impossible to conceive.
B) The thing would exist twice.
C) Existence would be identical to non-existence.
D) The thing would have no existence at all.

Key Takeaway
The first branch of the second proof shows that if existence were separate, a thing that is not non-existent would have to exist twice—an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that a thing cannot have two existences. Yet you may be living as if you have two lives—one for Allah and one for yourself. Where in your daily routine are you trying to maintain this contradiction? What step will you take today to unify your existence under one Lord?
?
If your existence is entirely from Allah and not separate from His act of creation, then why do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient? The argument exposes the absurdity of claiming independence. How will you consciously acknowledge your dependence on Him in your next action?
?
The logic of tawhid demands unity; a thing cannot have two distinct existences. In your heart, are you serving two masters—your desires and your Creator? Name one specific desire that you are allowing to compete with Allah, and commit to replacing it with obedience today.

The Absurd Consequence: Accidents in Non-Existent Subjects
Sanusi derives the absurd consequence that accidents could subsist in non-existent subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof leads to the conclusion that if existence were other than the thing, then accidents could subsist in non-existent subjects, contradicting intuitive judgment.

Core Idea
If existence is separate, then existent accidents could inhere in non-existent substances—an absurdity.

After establishing that existence must be existent (otherwise contradictories combine), Sanusi derives a startling consequence: the disjunction 'blackness is either existent or non-existent' fails, and worse, accidents like motion could subsist in non-existent bodies. This contradicts our immediate intuition (badīha) that accidents only inhere in existent subjects. This lesson unpacks that consequence.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two branches
From the previous lesson, the proof had two branches. Now we follow the second branch: the thing is non-existent in itself, and existence is existent.
2
Read the passage on the invalid disjunction
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the combination of contradictories and the intermediary are used to invalidate the disjunction 'blackness is either existent or non-existent'.
This is a technical move in modal logic.
3
Understand the consequence for accidents
The passage then states that if blackness is non-existent in itself but existence is existent, then existence (which is existent) subsists in blackness (which is non-existent). This would allow accidents like motion to exist while inhering in non-existent subjects.
This is absurd because accidents need a existing substance.
4
Connect to intuitive judgment
The conclusion is that the intuitive judgment (badīha) is false, because we intuitively know that motions and colors only subsist in existent things. This shows the absurdity of the opponent's position.

Commentary
This passage contains the second proof that existence cannot be other than the thing, along with detailed commentary. The argument runs: if existence is other than the thing, then on the supposition that the thing is not non-existent in itself, the thing would be found twice (once as essence, once with existence) and would have two existences, which is a contradiction. If we instead accept that the thing is non-existent in itself, then existence itself must be existent; otherwise, if existence were not existent, we would have a combination of contradictories (existence would be both existent and non-existent). This leads to the conclusion that the disjunction 'blackness is either existent or non-existent' is invalid. The commentary also discusses the case of the Necessary Being (God) who exists by both essential and absolute existence, but that is a special case that does not affect the argument. Handling Objections: Two Similars and the Necessary Being
Sanusi defends the second proof against objections about two similars and the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the second proof against objections, particularly the problem of two similars and the special case of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The objection of two similars is dismissed; the Necessary Being's existence is a unique case.

After presenting the second proof, the commentary anticipates two objections. First, if the thing is existent, why doesn't the arguer also face the problem of two similar existences (two similars)? Second, what about the Necessary Being (God) who is described as having both an essential and an absolute existence? Sanusi addresses these objections, showing that the proof remains sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
The objector says: if the thing is existent (not non-existent), then why wouldn't the arguer also have two similars? The arguer's response is that his aim is to refute the opponent, not to accept the situation.
The arguer uses the opponent's own assumptions to show inconsistency.
2
Understand the response to two similars
The commentary argues that even if two similars occurred, the opponent (Mu'tazila) might accept it, but they cannot accept the combination of contradictories. So the arguer's argument is stronger.
3
Address the case of the Necessary Being
Some might object that the Necessary Being (God) exists by two existences: one essential and one absolute. But the commentary clarifies that absolute existence is not a particular existence, so it does not lead to two particular existences.
4
Reflect on the strategy
Notice how the arguer uses the opponent's own principles to force an absurd consequence. This is a common dialectical technique in kalam.

هو الوجود المطلق، وأما الوجود الخاص الذي هو جزئي حقيقي فلا يحمل على الحقيقة كما سبق. قوله : ( لوجد ذلك الشيء مرتين فيه بحث لأن الواجب تعالى موجود بوجودين خاص هو عينه عندهم ومطلق زائد والاستحالة إنما تلزم إذا ثبت وجود ذلك الشيء بوجودين خاصين، وأما إذا كان المحمول مطلقاً، والآخر خاصاً فلا فتأمل. قوله : (اجتمع النقيضان فإن قلت : إذا كان موجوداً يلزم اجتماع المثلين فلم يجوز هذا، قلت : التجويز في نفس الأمر ممنوع وإنما المقصود الإلزام ولو سلم، فلزوم اجتماع المثلين ممنوع لجواز أن يكون موجوداً بوجود هو نفسه لا بوجود زائد، ولو سلم فقد جوز المعتزلة ذلك بخلاف اجتماع النقيضين، فليس ذلك مثله في مرتبة الاستحالة . قوله : (إذا لم يكن موجوداً ولا معدوماً لا يخفي أن فيه أيضاً اجماع النقيضين لأن الوجود إذا لم يكن موجوداً صدق سلب الوجود فاجتمع هو وسلبه سواء قيل: بالواسطة أو بالعدم بل إطلاق النقيض على العدم علي القول بالحال باعتبار استلزامه لذلك السلب، وإنما لم يتعرض له لحصول الغنية بدونه .
The claim: And also if the thing were not non-existent in itself on that supposition, that thing would be found twice and would exist by two existences—this is a contradiction. So when it is established that the thing is non-existent in itself, and existence is existent—otherwise if existence were not existent, then the two contradictories would combine on the supposition that it is non-existent, or the intermediary between existent and non-existent would be found if it is neither existent nor non-existent. And in both—i.e., in the combination of the two contradictories and the existence of the intermediary—(lies the desired result) which is the invalidity of our saying: blackness is either existent or non-existent, because on the first [alternative] the prevention of conjunction in this disjunction is invalidated, and on the second the prevention of absence in it is invalidated. So it follows from what has been mentioned—that blackness is non-existent in itself and existence is existent—that the existent (which is existence) subsists in the non-existent (which is blackness, for example) on the supposition of the correctness of our saying 'blackness exists'. So it follows that the permissibility arises in motions and colors, such that it could be said: these things are existent by sensory testimony and subsist in non-existents. And the intended goal is achieved: the invalidity of the judgment of intuition, because it judges that these motions and colors cannot subsist except in existent things. And he indicated the second of them by his saying (Also). His saying: (And it would be found by two existences) based on the fact that the aforementioned disjunction is concerning the existence by which it becomes... His saying: (The two contradictories combine etc.) As for the occurrence of two similars on the supposition that it is existent, that does not harm the arguer because then the contradictories would be in conflict, which is one of his proofs. He only mentioned the combination of contradictories because in it lies the establishment of the claim. It is identical and is not predicated of it, for the predicate is absolute existence; as for the particular existence which is a true particular, it is not predicated of the essence as previously. His saying: (That thing would be found twice) has a discussion because the Necessary Exalted exists by two existences—a particular that is His essence according to them and an absolute that is additional—and the impossibility only follows if it is established that that thing exists by two particular existences. But if the predicate is absolute and the other is particular, then not, so reflect. His saying: (The two contradictories combine) for if you say: if it is existent, then two similars would combine, so why did he permit this? I say: the permission in reality is denied; the intent is only to refute. And if conceded, the occurrence of two similars is denied because it is possible for it to exist by an existence that is itself, not by an additional existence. And if conceded, the Mu'tazila have permitted that, unlike the combination of contradictories, so it is not equivalent in rank of impossibility. His saying: (If it is neither existent nor non-existent) it is not hidden that in this there is also a combination of contradictories, because if existence is not existent, then the negation of existence is true, so it and its negation combine, whether one says by intermediary or by non-existence—rather, the application of 'contradictory' to non-existence on the view of the state is due to its implying that negation. And he did not discuss it because the need is satisfied without it.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage contains the second proof that existence cannot be other than the thing, along with detailed commentary. The argument runs: if existence is other than the thing, then on the supposition that the thing is not non-existent in itself, the thing would be found twice (once as essence, once with existence) and would have two existences, which is a contradiction. If we instead accept that the thing is non-existent in itself, then existence itself must be existent; otherwise, if existence were not existent, we would have a combination of contradictories (existence would be both existent and non-existent). This leads to the conclusion that the disjunction 'blackness is either existent or non-existent' is invalid. From this, it would follow that existent accidents (like motions and colors) could subsist in non-existent subjects, which contradicts the intuitive judgment (badīha) that they only subsist in existent subjects. The commentary then addresses several objections: the problem of two similars (if the thing is existent, why doesn't the arguer also face the problem of two similar existences?) is dismissed because the arguer's aim is to show the absurdity of the opponent's position; the Mu'tazilites might accept two similars but not the combination of contradictories. The commentary also discusses the case of the Necessary Being (God) who exists by both essential and absolute existence, but that is a special case that does not affect the argument. The passage concludes that the intuitive judgment against accidents subsisting in non-existents is preserved.

Key Terms
مثلين
معتزلة
واجب تعالى
محمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof applies even to God, making His existence impossible.
Correction: The proof only applies to contingent things; God's existence is unique and not subject to the same reasoning because absolute existence is not a particular.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection of two similars not harm the arguer?
A) Because the arguer accepts two similars.
B) Because the arguer's aim is to show an absurdity the opponent must deny, and the opponent may accept two similars but not the combination of contradictories.
C) Because two similars are impossible.
D) Because the arguer's proof does not involve existence.

Key Takeaway
The objections about two similars and God's existence do not refute the second proof; the proof remains sound for contingent things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an intellectual objection to your faith, do you immediately feel shaken, or do you pause to examine the logic like Sanusi does? What does your reaction reveal about the strength of your conviction? Resolve to study one objection deeply this week.
?
This lesson shows that even seemingly strong objections can be dismissed once the proper distinctions are made. Are there areas in your life where you conflate things that should be distinguished—like absolute truths and particular experiences? Spend time in self-reflection to identify one such area.
?
The Mu'tazila accepted the possibility of two similars but rejected the combination of contradictories. Are you selective in what you accept from Islamic teachings, perhaps allowing some difficulties while rejecting others? Ask Allah to grant you a heart that submits fully to revealed truths, even when your intellect struggles.

Proof That Existence Must Be Existent
Al-Iji argues that existence must be existent via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji proves that existence itself must be an existent attribute through a reductio argument that avoids logical absurdities.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be non-existent without collapsing logic.

This passage from al-Iji's matn presents a sophisticated argument that establishes the necessary existence of existence itself. Al-Iji uses a reductio to show that if existence were not existent, we would face either a combination of contradictories or a middle state between existence and non-existence. Read closely to follow the steps of this proof and see how it defends our intuitive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: assumption, reductio, conclusion.
The text is dense; take it slowly.
2
Identify the key assumption and the two problematic outcomes
The argument assumes the opposite of the claim and derives two impossible consequences: either contradictories combine or a middle exists. Find these in the text.
This is the heart of the reductio.
3
Understand the conclusion and its implication
The passage concludes that existence must be existent, and that denying this would falsify our intuitive judgment that qualities inhere only in existent things.
This links the technical argument to everyday knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المدعى (و) أيضاً لو لم يكن الشيء معدوماً في نفسه على ذلك التقدير لوجد ذلك الشيء مرتين وكان موجوداً بوجودين هذا خلف فإذا ثبت أن الشيء معدوم في نفسه والوجود موجود وإلا أي وإن لم يكن الوجود موجوداً اجتمع النقيضان على تقدير كونه معدوماً أو وجد الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم إذا لم يكن موجوداً ولا معدوماً وفيهما أي في اجتماع النقيضين ووجود الواسطة المطلوب وهو بطلان قولنا السواد إما موجود أو معدوم إذ على الأول يبطل منع الجمع في هذه المنفصلة وعلى الثاني يبطل منع الخلو فيها فيلزم مما ذكر من كون السواد معدوماً في نفسه وكون الوجود موجوداً قيام الموجود الذي هو الوجود بالمعدوم الذي هو السواد مثلاً على تقدير صحة قولنا السواد موجود فيلزم جواز في الحركات والألوان بأن يقال هذه أمور موجودة بشهادة الحس وقائمة بالمعدومات ويحصل المراد وهو بطلان حكم البداهة لأنها تحكم بأن هذه الحركات والألوان لا يجوز قيامها إلا بأمور موجودة وأشار إلى ثانيهما بقوله (وأيضاً مثله موجدواً)
The claim: and also if the thing were not non-existent in itself on that assumption, that thing would be found twice and would exist with two existences, which is absurd. So if it is established that the thing is non-existent in itself, and existence is existent, otherwise—i.e., if existence were not existent—then the two contradictories would be combined on the assumption of it being non-existent, or the middle between existent and non-existent would be found if it is neither existent nor non-existent. And in both—i.e., the combination of the two contradictories and the existence of the middle—lies the desired conclusion, which is the falsity of our statement 'blackness is either existent or non-existent'. For on the first assumption, the prevention of combination in this disjunction is invalidated, and on the second, the prevention of exclusion is invalidated. So it follows from what has been mentioned—that blackness is non-existent in itself and that existence is existent—that the existent (which is existence) subsists in the non-existent (which is, for example, blackness) on the assumption of the correctness of our statement 'blackness is existent'. Thus it follows that it is permissible for motions and colors to be described as existent by the testimony of sense and subsisting in non-existents. And the goal is achieved, namely the falsification of the judgment of intuition, because it judges that these motions and colors cannot subsist except in existent things. And he indicated the second of them by his saying: 'and also similarly existent'.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a proof from al-Iji's text (matn) within the fourth observatory on proving necessary sciences. The argument is structured as a reductio ad absurdum. It begins by assuming that a thing (such as blackness) is not non-existent in itself on a certain estimation. If that were the case, then the thing would be found twice, i.e., it would have two existences, which is impossible (خلف). Therefore, it must be that the thing is non-existent in itself, and that existence itself is existent. If existence were not existent, then either two contradictories would combine (the thing would be both existent and non-existent at once) or there would be a middle state between existent and non-existent. Both are impossible, thus proving that existence must be existent. The consequence is that our statement 'blackness is existent' would mean that existence (which is existent) subsists in blackness (which is non-existent), leading to the absurdity that sensible qualities like motions and colors can inhere in non-existent things—contradicting our intuitive knowledge that they only inhere in existent substances. The passage ends by noting the second argument (indicated by 'and also similarly existent'), though the text of that second argument is not fully given here.

Key Terms
وجود
معدوم
نقيضان
واسطة
بداهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is about the physical existence of things in the world.
Correction: The argument is about the ontological status of existence itself, not about physical objects. It is a logical proof that existence must be an existent attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if existence were not existent?
A) The thing would be non-existent
B) Two contradictories would combine or a middle state would exist
C) The thing would be found twice
D) Intuitive judgment would remain valid

Key Takeaway
Al-Iji proves that existence must be existent through a reductio that exposes the absurdity of the contrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence itself must be existent, then you cannot claim that God's existence is a mere concept—it is the most real thing. Do you live as though your own existence is borrowed from a necessary existent, or do you behave as if you are self-sufficient?
?
This proof challenges your comfort by showing that what you take for granted (like your perception that colors exist in objects) may be logically flawed. What is one belief you hold that you have never subjected to rigorous logical scrutiny? Are you willing to risk the discomfort of being wrong?
?
Al-Iji's argument forces you to accept that existence is not a neutral label but a reality that must belong to something. If you truly internalized that your own existence points to a necessary existent, would you still fear death, or would you trust that your existence is sustained by the One who is existence itself?

Two Existences: Special and Absolute
Commentary on the possibility of two existences for a single thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between special existence (identical to essence) and absolute existence (predicated as an additional attribute) and why the objection of 'two existences' does not apply to God.

Core Idea
Special existence is the essence itself; absolute existence is an additional attribute.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif tackles a subtle objection against the Ash'ari view on divine attributes. Critics argue that if God's attributes are additional existences, then He would exist with multiple existences, leading to absurdities. The commentator clarifies by distinguishing between special existence (the concrete existence of a thing) and absolute existence (the universal concept predicated of many), showing that the objection only holds for two special existences.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Do not try to analyze it yet; just get a feel for the argumentative flow.
The passage is a commentary on al-Iji's text, so it refers to earlier discussions.
2
Identify the two types of existence
Look for where the commentator distinguishes 'special existence' (al-wujud al-khas) and 'absolute existence' (al-wujud al-mutlaq). Understand that special existence is particular and not predicated, while absolute existence is universal and predicated.
3
Follow the impossibility argument
Trace why the commentator says impossibility only follows if both existences are special. The necessary being has one special existence (identical to essence) and one absolute existence (additional), so no contradiction arises.
4
Examine the objection about similars vs contradictories
The opponent shifts to an argument about combining two similars. The commentator responds by noting the intent is to compel, and that even if similars could combine, it's not as absurd as combining contradictories.
This part shows the dialectical nature of kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وكان موجوداً بوجودين) بناء على أن الترديد المذكور في الوجود الذي صار به هو عينه ولا يكون محمولاً عليه فإن المحمول هو الوجود المطلق وأما الوجود الخاص الذي هو جزئي حقيقي فلا يحمل على الحقيقة كما سبق. قوله: (لوجد ذلك الشيء مرتين) فيه بحث لأن الواجب تعالى موجود بوجودين خاص هو عينه عندهم ومطلق زائد والاستحالة إنما تلزم إذا ثبت وجود ذلك الشيء بوجودين خاصين وأما إذا كان المحمول مطلقاً والآخر خاصاً فلا فتأمل. قوله: (اجتمع النقيضان) فإن قلت: إذا كان موجوداً يلزم اجتماع المثلين فلم يجوز هذا قلت: التجويز في نفس الأمر ممنوع وإنما المقصود الإلزام ولو سلم فلزوم اجتماع المثلين ممنوع لجواز أن يكون موجوداً بوجود هو نفسه لا بوجود زائد ولو سلم فقد جوز المعتزلة ذلك بخلاف اجتماع النقيضين فليس ذلك مثله في مرتبة الاستحالة. قوله: (إذا لم يكن موجوداً ولا معدوماً) لا يخفي أن فيه أيضاً اجتماع النقيضين لأن الوجود إذا لم يكن موجوداً صدق سلب الوجود فاجتمع هو وسلبه سواء قيل بالواسطة أو بالعدم بل إطلاق النقيض على العدم على القول بالحال باعتبار استلزامه لذلك السلب وإنما لم يتعرض له لحصول الغنية بدونه.
His saying: 'and was existent with two existences'—based on the fact that the disjunction mentioned regarding existence by which it becomes is identical to it and is not predicated upon it; for the predicate is absolute existence. As for special existence, which is a real particular, it is not predicated upon the essence, as has preceded. His saying: 'that thing would be found twice'—there is investigation in it, because the Necessary Exalted is existent with two existences: a special one that is identical to Him according to them, and an absolute one that is additional. Impossibility only follows if it is established that that thing exists with two special existences. But if the predicate is absolute and the other is special, then it is not impossible, so reflect. His saying: 'the two contradictories would combine'—for if you say: if it is existent, then the concurrence of two similars follows, so why allow this? I say: permission in reality is denied; the intent is only to compel. And even if it is granted, the occurrence of two similars is denied because it is possible that it exists with an existence that is itself, not with an additional existence. And even if it is granted, the Mu'tazila have allowed that, unlike the combination of two contradictories, so that is not like it in the rank of impossibility. His saying: 'if it is neither existent nor non-existent'—it is not hidden that in this there is also combination of two contradictories, because if existence is not existent, the negation of existence is true, so it and its negation combine, whether one says by the middle or by non-existence. Rather, the application of contradictory to non-existence is according to the view of states (al-hal) in consideration of its entailment of that negation. He only did not address it because sufficiency is obtained without it.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on specific lines of al-Iji's matn. It discusses the issue of a thing being 'existent with two existences'. The commentator explains that the disjunction in the text refers to absolute existence which is predicated (like a universal), while special existence (the individual existence of a thing) is not predicated upon the essence. Then he addresses the impossibility of a thing being found twice: it is argued that the Necessary (God) is existent with two existences—one special (identical to His essence) and one absolute (additional)—so impossibility only arises if both existences are special. If one is absolute and the other special, it is not impossible. Next, the commentator handles the objection about the combination of two similars (مثلين) versus two contradictories (نقيضين). He states that the intent of the argument is to compel the opponent, not to assert real permission. Even if two similars were possible, the Mu'tazila allow it, but no one allows two contradictories, so the two cases are not equivalent in impossibility. Finally, he explains that the case of a thing being neither existent nor non-existent actually involves a combination of contradictories, because if existence is not existent, the negation of existence is true, so existence and its negation coincide. The author did not elaborate further because the point is already sufficiently made.

Key Terms
وجود مطلق
وجود خاص
مثلين
نقيضين
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that God cannot have an additional attribute because it would mean He exists with two existences.
Correction: The impossibility only applies to two special existences. God's special existence is identical to His essence, and His absolute existence is an additional attribute, so no impossibility occurs.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, when does the impossibility of a thing existing with two existences apply?
A) When both existences are absolute
B) When both existences are special
C) When one existence is special and the other absolute
D) When the existence is not predicated

Key Takeaway
The distinction between special and absolute existence is crucial in Ash'ari theology to defend the reality of divine attributes without implying multiple essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own special existence—your unique self—and the attributes you possess, do you ever confuse your essence with your actions? How does the distinction between special and absolute existence in God help you see that your attributes are distinct from your core self, and yet in God they are one? Will you meditate on this to better understand tawhid?
?
The commentator argues that the combination of two similars is allowed even by the Mu'tazila, but two contradictories are impossible. Is there a belief or practice in your life where you have treated a 'similar' (a minor difference) as if it were a 'contradictory' (a major opposition), causing unnecessary division? Will you commit to re-examining such areas with the precision of this theological distinction?
?
God's existence is both special (identical to His essence) and absolute (an additional attribute). Do you truly treat God's attributes as real and distinct yet not separate from His essence? How might this nuanced understanding transform your worship—from casual invocation to profound reverence for the reality of His attributes? Will you implement this awareness in your next prayer or supplication?
