The Predication Problem: Why 'Blackness Exists' Implies Unity or Regress
Al-Iji argues that predicating existence to an essence on the assumption of difference leads to either unity of two or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Iji's argument that if existence is distinct from essence, then the statement 'essence exists' either posits a unity of two distinct things or leads to an infinite regress of descriptions.

Core Idea
Calling something 'existent' cannot be a middle ground: either existence is the thing itself, or you end up in a regress.

Al-Iji tackles a foundational problem in kalam: how can we say a thing 'exists' if existence is not identical to its essence? In this passage, he considers the alternative—that existence is distinct—and shows that it leads to absurdity. By examining the case of 'blackness exists,' he forces the reader to see that the only consistent view is the identity of existence and essence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Recognize the issue: if existence is other than the essence (e.g., blackness), what does it mean to predicate 'exists' of that essence? The passage assumes this difference and draws consequences.
The term 'blackness' is a concrete example; the argument applies to any essence.
2
Follow the objection
An objector might say 'blackness exists' does not mean blackness is identical to existence, but rather that blackness is described by existence. Note this move.
This objection tries to avoid the unity problem by introducing a third notion: 'being described'.
3
Analyze 'being-described-by-existence'
Al-Iji transfers the discussion to this concept. He considers two possibilities: either the concept of being-described is identical to blackness itself, or it is something else. If identical, it's trivial; if different, the problem reappears.
4
See the regress
To avoid the second possibility, one might say 'blackness is described by the description-of-existence'. But then that second description requires another description, leading to an infinite regress.
Infinite regress (تسلسل) is deemed false in kalam.
5
Conclude: identity is necessary
Since the regress is false, the only remaining possibility is that being-described must be removed, forcing the conclusion that the original judgment posits unity of two distinct things. Therefore, the initial assumption of difference must be rejected.
Thus existence must be identical to essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه أي حمل الوجود على السواد على تقدير المغايرة حكم بوحدة الاثنين وهما السواد والوجود وأنه باطل لا يقال ليس المراد بقولنا السواد موجود هو أن السواد عين الوجود حتى يلزم ما ذكرتم بل المراد أن السواد موصوف بالوجود ولا إشكال فيه لأنا ننقل الكلام إلى الموصوفية بالوجود فإن مفهوم الموصوفية بالوجود إما نفس السواد فلا يفيد الحمل وقد أبطلناه وإما غيره فيكون قولنا السواد موصوف بالوجود حكماً بوحدة الاثنين إلا أن يراد به أن السواد موصوف بموصوفية الوجود وحينئذ يعود التقسيم إلى الموصوفية الثانية ويلزم التسلسل وهو باطل فوجب رفع الموصوفية عن البين ويلزم الحكم بوحدة الاثنين
For it—i.e., predicating existence of blackness on the assumption of difference—is a judgment of the unity of two things, namely blackness and existence, and it is false. It cannot be said: the meaning of our statement 'blackness is existent' is not that blackness is identical to existence so that what you mentioned follows; rather the meaning is that blackness is described by existence, and there is no problem in that. For we transfer the discussion to being-described-by-existence. For the concept of being-described-by-existence is either identical to blackness, so it does not benefit predication and we have already refuted it, or it is other than blackness, so our statement 'blackness is described by existence' becomes a judgment of the unity of two things, unless it is meant that blackness is described by the description-of-existence, and then the division returns to the second description-of-existence and an infinite regress follows, which is false. Therefore being-described must be removed from the picture, and the judgment of the unity of two things follows.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained argument from the matn on page 164. It addresses the problem of how we can say 'blackness exists' if existence is distinct from blackness. The author argues that if existence is other than blackness (مغايرة), then the statement 'blackness exists' judges a unity between two distinct realities (blackness and existence), which is false. An objector might respond that the statement means blackness is described by existence, not that blackness is identical to existence. The author counters by analyzing 'being-described-by-existence' (الموصوفية بالوجود). If this concept of being-described is the same as blackness itself, then it is not a real predication (it is just saying blackness is blackness). If it is something else, then saying 'blackness is described by existence' again posits a unity of two things. To avoid this, one might say that blackness is described by the 'description-of-existence' (موصوفية الوجود), but then that description-of-existence itself needs a description, leading to an infinite regress. Since infinite regress is false, the only way out is to remove the intermediary descriptions, which forces us back to the conclusion that the original judgment is a unity of two distinct things, which is absurd. Thus, the only correct interpretation is that existence is identical to the essence.

Key Terms
حمل
مغايرة
موصوفية
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument rejects the idea of description altogether, so we cannot say an essence is described by existence.
Correction: The argument does not reject description entirely; it shows that if existence is distinct, then any attempt to describe essence by existence leads either to triviality or regress. The only consistent view is that existence is identical to essence, so description in the sense of attribution is not possible.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of saying 'blackness is described by the description-of-existence'?
A) It avoids the problem of unity
B) It leads to an infinite regress
C) It proves the Mu'tazila correct
D) It is the same as saying blackness exists

Key Takeaway
When existence is distinct from essence, predicating existence to essence forces either an impossible unity or an infinite regress—so existence must be identical to essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just saw that any description of God's attributes as separate from His essence leads to absurdity. Do you ever think about God's attributes like mercy or justice as if they were things added to Him? How does this argument force you to re-examine the way you speak about God? Will you commit to weeding out any language that implies separation between God and His attributes?
?
Al-Iji argues that infinite regress is impossible. But in your own life, do you act as if there is no end to excuses, delays, or worldly pursuits? What is one area where you are stuck in a 'regress' of procrastination or distraction, and when will you cut the chain?
?
The argument shows that the only consistent position is to affirm that existence is identical to essence. When you say 'Allah exists', do you truly mean that His existence is His very being, or do you silently treat existence as an extra quality? How can you bring the tawheed of Allah's existence from your mind into your heart?

Infinite Regress in Mental Matters: Objection and Rebuttal
Objection that infinite regress is permissible in mental matters, with the commentator's rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between permissible and impossible infinite regress in mental considerations, and why an infinite regress in necessary concepts invalidates a judgment.

Core Idea
Not all mental regress is harmless; regress required for a judgment's truth makes the judgment false.

This lesson presents an objection against the impossibility of infinite regress in mental matters, and the commentator's critical response. The objector argues that the proof against regress only applies to external realities, not mental ones. The reply shows that while some mental regress is permissible (like numbers), regress that is essential for the truth of a concrete judgment leads to absurdity. The example of 'blackness is existent' illustrates how an infinite chain of descriptions renders the judgment invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Begin by reading the objection which claims that infinite regress is not impossible in mental matters because the proof only applies to external things.
Note the objector's distinction between external and mental realms.
2
Understand the response's distinction
The response distinguishes two cases: regress originating from arbitrary intellectual consideration (permissible) vs. regress on which the truth of a judgment depends (impossible).
The key is whether the regress is merely conventional or affects truth.
3
Analyze the example of 'blackness is existent'
Examine why this judgment leads to an infinite regress: the description (mawsufiyya) is either identical or other, requiring further descriptions ad infinitum, making the judgment false.
This shows that some mental regress is impossible because it stems from the nature of the judgment itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل لا التسلسل في الأمور الذهنية لأن البرهان إنما قام على بطلانه في الأمور الخارجية قوله : ( فإن قيل : لا يمتنع إلخ ) نقل عن الشارح قدس سره ولقائل أن يقول : ما يقال من أن التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية جائز حق فيما إذا كان منشأ تلك السلسلة مجرد اعتبار العقل لانقطاعه بانقطاع الاعتبار إذ لا سبيل للعقل إلى أن يعتبر ما لا نهاية له فلا تسلسل في الحقيقة في هذا الموضع وأما إذا كانت صحة الحكم موقوفة على تعقلات لا تتناهى كما في قولنا السواد موجود كان هذا الحكم باطلاً قطعاً لتوقفها حينئذ على تعقلات غير متناهية وإنما قلنا : السواد موجود من هذا القبيل لاحتياجنا هاهنا إلى اعتبار الموصوفية فيرجع الترديد المذكور في الموصوفية بأنها إما عين السواد فلا يكون مفيداً لكونه حمل الشيء على نفسه أو غيره فيكون حكما بوحدة الاثنين فنحتاج إلى موصوفية ثالثة ورابعة وهلم جرا فكان قولنا السواد موجود باطلاً قطعاً انتهى يعني أن الحكم بجواز التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية ليس بصحيح على إطلاقه وإنما ذلك فيما إذا كان منشأ وجود آحاد السلسلة مجرد اعتبار العقل وإن كان ذلك الاعتبار مطابقاً لنفس الأمر كما في مراتب الأعداد فإن منشأها الوحدة وتكرارها واللزوم والوحدة والوجوب والإمكان والأعراض النسبية فإن وحدة الوحدة وإمكان الإمكان وغير ذلك مما تكرر نوعه بمجرد اعتبار العقل مثلاً إذا لاحظ الوحدة من حيث إنها وصف للواحد لم يعتبر
For it is said: infinite regress is not impossible in mental things because the demonstration was only established against it in external things. His saying: 'for it is said: it is not impossible etc.'—narrated from the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) and it is possible for a speaker to say: what is said that infinite regress in conventional matters is permissible is true in the case where the origin of that series is merely the consideration of the intellect, because it is cut off when the consideration is cut off, since the intellect cannot consider something infinite, so there is no true regress in that case. But when the truth of a judgment depends on an infinite number of concepts—as in our statement 'blackness is existent'—then this judgment is absolutely false, because it would depend on infinite concepts. And we only say: 'blackness is existent' falls under this category, because we need here to consider the description (al-mawsufiyya), so the mentioned disjunction returns in the description: either it is identical to blackness, so it is not beneficial because it is a predication of a thing of itself, or it is other, so it becomes a judgment of unity of two, and we need a third, fourth description ad infinitum; thus our statement 'blackness is existent' is absolutely false. (End). That is, the judgment that regress is permissible in conventional matters is not absolutely correct; it is only so when the origin of the individuals of the series is merely the intellect's consideration, even if that consideration corresponds to reality, as in the levels of numbers—their origin is unity and its repetition—and the necessary and the possible and relative attributes. For the unity of unity, and the possibility of possibility, etc., are types repeated merely by the intellect's consideration. For example, when it observes unity insofar as it is a description of the one, it does not consider...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response. The objection claims that infinite regress is not impossible in mental (ذهني) or conventional (اعتباري) matters, because the proof of impossibility only applies to external realities. The commentator cites a response: regress is allowed only when the series originates solely from the intellect's arbitrary consideration, so that the series ends when the consideration stops. However, when a judgment's truth depends on an infinite number of mental concepts—as in 'blackness is existent' where we must analyze the description of existence—then the judgment becomes false because it requires an infinite chain of descriptions. In the case of 'blackness is existent', we need to consider the description (الموصوفية). This description is either identical to blackness (trivial) or other (requiring a further description), leading to an infinite regress. Hence the judgment is false. The commentator further explains that regress in purely conventional matters like numbers and their relations is allowed because those series are based on the intellect's capacity to posit them. But when the truth of a proposition hinges on an infinite mental regress, the proposition is invalid. The argument is cut off here, but the core point is established: the infinite regress objection fails because the regress in this case is not purely conventional but affects the truth of a real judgment.

Key Terms
تسلسل
ذهني
اعتباري
موصوفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response, when is infinite regress in mental things actually impossible?
A) When the series originates from the intellect's arbitrary consideration
B) When the truth of a judgment depends on an infinite number of mental concepts
C) When the series corresponds to external reality
D) When the regress involves numbers

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress is permissible only when it originates from arbitrary intellectual consideration; but when a judgment's truth depends on infinite mental steps, the judgment is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any belief you hold that, upon reflection, depends on an endless series of justifications? If such a belief were shown to require an infinite regress, would you reject it or find a foundation?
?
You often think abstractly about concepts like 'existence' and 'unity'. When you say 'something exists', do you ever consider whether your own understanding of that statement itself falls into an infinite regress? What would you do if it did?
?
The passage teaches that not all mental chains are harmless. Can you identify a thought pattern in your own life—perhaps a fear or a doubt—that relies on an infinite regress of worries? How can you cut that chain and base your judgment on something finite and true?

Mawsufiyya as a Mental Concept and Permissibility of Infinite Regress
Jurjani defines mawsufiyya as a mental concept and discusses the permissibility of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mawsufiyya is considered a mental posited concept and how infinite regress in such mental considerations is permissible.

Core Idea
In mental concepts, infinite regress is cut off by the mind's cessation, not by logical impossibility.

This passage introduces mawsufiyya as a mental posited notion and explains the conditions under which infinite regress can occur without contradiction. The author argues that when the mind considers a relation like mawsufiyya, the chain of considerations can be interrupted by the mind's own cessation, not by an external logical impossibility. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion about the nature of predication and the reality of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the core argument about mawsufiyya and infinite regress.
Notice how the author defines mawsufiyya as a mental concept.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two cases: when the chain originates from mental consideration vs. when it originates from real existence. Observe how each leads to a different ruling on infinite regress.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Examine the dilemma regarding the judgment 'blackness exists'. Consider why the author concludes that if infinite regress were required, the judgment would be false.
This dilemma is crucial for understanding the limitations of mental concepts.

صورتها فممنوع، إذ لا فساد فيه كما في قيام صورة الجوهر بالذهن، وهذا أقرب مما نقله الشارح بقوله وقد يقال : إلخ .
And mawsufiyya (being described) is among mental posited concepts. (We said: mawsufiyya is a relation between two things having unity, and when one observes it in itself and that it is a concept among concepts, one posits for it unity, and analogize to that. And we said: the permissibility of infinite regress in it because then the chain is cut off due to the cessation of the mind's consideration, for the mind is not capable of considering infinite things in detail, and it is not obligated to intentional observation in every rank. And even if the soul is eternal, the individuals do not exist such that application (tatbiq) could occur, so there is no infinite regress. And on the supposition of its assumption, the absurdity does not follow from the necessity of finitude of what is infinite, or the deficient being like the excessive, because there is nothing actually infinite nor anything excessive in it, but merely by assumption. But if the cause of existence of that chain is something other than the mind's consideration, then infinite regress in it is invalid, otherwise the existence of infinite things in actual fact would be necessary, and application would occur in it according to us and the philosophers if there is order and coexistence in that existence, and it is not then cut off by the cessation of the mind's consideration, since the mind's consideration has no entry into its existence. Therefore they ruled the invalidity of infinite regress on the supposition of the theory of the whole, because it necessitates the existence of infinite things in the mind, since it is not cut off by the cessation of consideration. This, but there remains for me an investigation whether what we are dealing with is of this kind, because the correctness of the judgment in our saying 'blackness exists' based on otherness depends on observing mawsufiyya as a relation between the two extremes, and the instrument of observation is the state of one of them relative to the other. Then it is not possible for the mind to attribute it to blackness at all. Then if it observes it intentionally and considers that it is a concept that must be obtained for both extremes, otherwise one of them would not be obtained for the other, it considers a second mawsufiyya as an instrument for observing the state of the first mawsufiyya relative to blackness. And this observation is not necessary for the mind always, so the chain of mawsufiyyas is cut off by the cessation of its consideration. As for the theologians' allowing the non-finiteness of the relations of knowledge to acts, even though they are posited matters and not mere mental consideration, it is because these relations are not in the external world nor in the mind, so application does not occur in them. Rather they are in His knowledge, and they are finite from His perspective because He encompasses them. So ponder, for it is a matter on which feet have slipped. It is existing of this kind because we need here to consider mawsufiyya, so the earlier dilemma returns regarding mawsufiyya: either it is identical to blackness, then it is not useful because it would be predication of a thing of itself; or other, then it would be a judgment of the unity of two things, needing a second and third mawsufiyya and so on. Thus our saying 'blackness exists' would be definitively false. This, and it is apparent to me that what he mentioned about the falsehood of that saying, i.e., our saying 'blackness exists' on the supposition of its need for infinite intellections, is true without doubt. But as for the invalidity of infinite regress in mental posited matters related to actual reality (al-umur al-i'tibariyya al-nafs al-amriyya) absolutely, no. As for the philosophers, because they require order in the running of the proof, and there is no order among those things according to external existence, and that is apparent. And as for order according to the mind, it depends on their detailed conceptualization, and the soul is not capable of that by their admission. As for the theologians, because they argued for the posited nature of relative accidents (al-a'rad al-nisabiyya) by saying: if they existed, their loci would be qualified by them, so they have a relation to them of locativity, and the discussion returns to them, so infinite regress would follow in existing things. And you are aware that these relations are not merely assumed posited matters, but real things by which their loci are qualified in actual reality. Therefore their loci have a relation to them of locativity in actual reality, and the described and the attribute return, so they subsist by both, which is the mind, because it is impossible for the relation to subsist without the two relata. And if it does not subsist in the mind, it is not a mental thing but rather external. And it may be said: the meaning of its being mental is that it is not an external existent, but exists in the mind subsisting by the two relata. While the mind's judgment that blackness is described by existence in the external is either corresponding to the external, then there is an external mawsufiyya, and the objection we mentioned first returns; or not corresponding to it, so it is disregarded because it is a false judgment. And it may be answered that the mind's judgment must correspond to actual reality, so that... His saying: 'the meaning of its being etc.' That does not contradict its subsistence by the two relata. And to this extent the answer is complete, except that he added to it his saying: 'rather it exists etc.' to repel the objection that if it is not an external existent, it would exist in the mind, necessitating the existence of the relation without the two relata. Meaning that it exists in the mind subsisting by them, not without them. And the meaning of that is that when the mind observes the two relata in a specific manner, it extracts the qualification of one by the other. His saying: 'and it may be answered etc.' This answer chooses the second alternative and denies the necessity that it be a false judgment, for the false is what does not correspond to actual reality, not what does not correspond to the external. And it is based on making the external a locus for existence, not for the described. And the second answer chooses the first alternative and denies the necessity that it be external in the sense of existent in the external, and is based on considering it a locus for the described. His saying: 'for the apparent difference etc.' For the existent in the external is that for which the external is a locus for its existence, not that for which it is a locus for itself. Do you not see that our saying 'Zayd exists in the external' necessitates the existence of Zayd in it, not the existence of his existence. The discussion in it would lead to infinite regress, but they do not prevent it. Also, they are those who say that the relations of God's knowledge to acts are non-finite, and they do not care about the necessity of infinite regress in those relations, even though they do not require order in its invalidity, apart from other cases. And it is supported by the agreement of the two schools on the requirement of existence in the application of the proof of correspondence (burhan al-tatbiq). Their disagreement is only about requiring coexistence in existence and order, and the application of that proof or other proofs for nullifying infinite regress in those matters absolutely is not apparent. Then what he mentioned: that the mind cannot consider what has no end, rather its consideration must break off at a rank that does not stop at a limit, so infinite regress is not realized in actual reality. This is a statement that the commentator mentioned in his glosses on al-Matali' as well, and it is a place of investigation and difficulty, because the soul is eternal by agreement, so why can it not consider infinite things in infinite future times? If you say: the actual considerations are finite, since another consideration can occur after them, I say: this is a mistake of the imagination, because it does not consider the infinite as it is, otherwise nothing is conceivable after the infinite in infinite future times. So contemplate. His saying: 'because it is impossible for the relation to subsist without the two relata.' It is said: If he intended the impossibility of the relation itself subsisting, it is conceded but does not help; if he intended the impossibility of its form subsisting, it is denied, for there is no corruption in it, as in the subsistence of the form of substance in the mind. And this is closer than what the commentator transmitted in his saying 'and it may be said etc.'
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the nature of mawsufiyya (the property of being described) and the issue of infinite regress (tasalsul) in mental considerations. The author begins by stating that mawsufiyya is among mental posited concepts. He explains that when the mind observes mawsufiyya as a relation between two things and considers it in itself, it posits unity for it. He then argues that infinite regress in such mental considerations is permissible because the chain of considerations is cut off when the mind stops considering; the mind cannot consider infinite things in detail, nor is it obligated to intentional observation at every rank. Even if the soul is eternal, the individuals (individual considerations) do not exist in actuality such that the proof of correspondence (tatbiq) could apply, so no actual infinite regress occurs. However, if the chain's existence originates from something other than mental consideration (i.e., real external existence), then infinite regress is invalid because it would entail actual infinite things, and the proof of correspondence would apply since there is order and coexistence in that existence. The author then qualifies his discussion by noting that what he is dealing with (the judgment 'blackness exists') may not fall under the same category. He explains that the correctness of this judgment, if based on otherness (ghayriyya) between subject and predicate, depends on observing mawsufiyya as a relation between the two extremes. But then the mind cannot directly predicate mawsufiyya of blackness; it must observe it intentionally, and this observation is not always necessary, so the chain of mawsufiyyas is cut off. He contrasts this with the theologians' allowing infinite relations of God's knowledge to acts, because those relations are not in the external world nor in the mind, so the proof of correspondence does not apply. The passage then discusses a dilemma: either mawsufiyya is the same as blackness (making the predication trivial) or different (requiring a second mawsufiyya and so on, leading to infinite regress). The author concludes that if that infinite regress is required, the judgment 'blackness exists' would be false. He then clarifies that the falsehood of that judgment under that supposition is correct, but the absolute invalidity of infinite regress in mental posited matters (al-umur al-i'tibariyya al-nafs al-amriyya) is not established. He explains the positions of philosophers and theologians: philosophers require order in the proof, and there is no order among such things externally; the soul cannot conceptualize them in detail. Theologians argue that relative accidents are mental, otherwise they would lead to infinite regress in existing things, but the author notes that these relations are not merely assumed but real. The discussion continues with objections and answers about whether mental judgment must correspond to external reality, and whether the relation subsists in the mind or externally. The passage ends with a critical comment on the claim that the mind cannot consider infinite things, arguing that since the soul is eternal, it could potentially consider infinite things in infinite future times, and the objection that actual considerations are finite is dismissed as a mistake of the imagination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That infinite regress is always impossible in any context.
Correction: In mental considerations, infinite regress is permissible because the chain is cut off by the mind's cessation, not by a logical contradiction. Actual infinity is not required.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does infinite regress not occur in a chain of mental considerations?
A) Because the soul is eternal and can consider infinite things.
B) Because the chain is cut off when the mind stops considering.
C) Because the proof of correspondence shows they are finite.
D) Because the theologians have established their impossibility.

Key Takeaway
Mawsufiyya is a mental concept; infinite regress in mental considerations is permissible because the mind can stop its consideration, preventing actual infinity. This understanding is crucial for the analysis of predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I exist' or 'God exists', do you ever pause to consider that this judgment relies on a mental concept of existence that is not itself the thing? How does this awareness affect the sincerity of your testimony?
?
The author says the mind is not capable of considering infinite things in detail. Reflect on a time you tried to think deeply about eternity or God's attributes. Did your mind eventually 'stop'? What does that limitation tell you about your need for revelation?
?
If the judgment 'blackness exists' can only be sound if infinite regress is cut off by the mind's cessation, how much more careful should you be when making judgments about Allah—whose attributes are not mere mental concepts? Are you quick to affirm statements about Him without realizing the subtle pitfalls?

Rational Judgments
advanced
14 min
Invalidity of Infinite Regress in Real Existence and Views of Philosophers and Theologians
Jurjani argues that infinite regress is invalid in real existence and discusses philosophers' and theologians' positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite regress is invalid in real existence and how philosophers and theologians differ on the conditions for applying the proof of correspondence.

Core Idea
In real existence, infinite regress leads to actual infinities, making it absurd; mental posited matters are exempt.

This passage builds on the previous discussion by examining the absolute invalidity of infinite regress in real existence. The author contrasts the positions of philosophers and theologians regarding the proof of correspondence (burhan al-tatbiq) and the role of order and coexistence. He also addresses a potential objection about the mind's ability to consider infinite things given the soul's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about absolute invalidity
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the statement about the invalidity of infinite regress in real existence.
Note that the author denies the absolute invalidity in mental posited matters.
2
Compare the two schools
Understand the philosophers' requirement of order in the proof and the theologians' argument from relative accidents.
3
Examine the objection about the mind's ability
Consider the objection that the soul is eternal and could consider infinite things in infinite future times, and the author's response.
This ties back to the permissibility of regress in mental concepts.

والموصوفية من المفهومات الاعتبارية الذهنية (قلنا : الموصوفية نسبة بين لها وحدة، وإذا لاحظها من حيث ذاتها وأنها مفهوم من المفهومات اعتبر لها وحدة وقس على ذلك، وإنما قلنا : بجواز التسلسل فيها لأنه حينئذ تنقطع السلسلة بسبب انقطاع اعتبار العقل، إذ العقل لا يقدر على اعتبار الأمور الغير المتناهية مفصلا، ولا يجب عليه الملاحظة القصدية في كل مرتبة، وإن كان النفس أبدياً فلا تكون الآحاد موجودة حتى يجري التطبيق فلا تسلسل، وعلى تقدير فرضه لا يلزم المحال من لزوم تناهي ما لا يتناهى أو كون الناقص كالزائد، إذ لا غير متناه في نفس الأمر ولا زائد فيه، بل بمجرد الفرض وأما إذا كان منشأ وجود تلك السلسلة أمراً غير اعتبار العقل، فالتسلسل فيها باطل وإلا لزم وجود الأمور الغير المتناهية في نفس الأمر ويجري فيها التطبيق عندنا وعند الحكماء إذا كان ترتب واجتماع في ذلك الوجود ولا ينقطع حينئذ بانقطاع اعتبار العقل، إذ لا مدخل لاعتبار العقل في وجودها، ولذا حكموا ببطلان التسلسل على تقدير نظرية الكل لاستلزامه وجود أمور غير متناهية في الذهن لعدم انقطاعه بانقطاع الاعتبار هذا، لكن بقي لي بحث في كون ما نحن فيه من هذا القبيل، لأن صحة الحكم في قولنا : السواد موجود بناء على الغيرية موقوفة على ملاحظة الموصوفية من حيث أنها نسبة بين الطرفين وآلة الملاحظة حال أحدهما بالقياس إلى الآخر، وحينئذ لا يمكن للعقل أن يحملها على السواد أصلاً، ثم إذا لاحظها قصداً واعتبر أنها مفهوم لا بد من حصوله للطرفين، وإلا لم يكن أحدهما حاصلاً للآخر، اعتبر موصوفية ثانية آلة لملاحظة حال الموصوفية الأولى بالقياس إلى السواد وهذه الملاحظة ليست لازمة للعقل دائماً فتنقطع سلسلة الموصوفيات بانقطاع اعتباره، وأما تجويز المتكلمين عدم تناهي تعلقات العلم بالفعل مع أنها أمور اعتبارية، وليست بمجرد اعتبار العقل، فلأن هذه التعلقات ليست في الخارج ولا في الذهن، فلا يجري التطبيق فيها وإنما هي في علمه تعالى وهي بالنظر إليه متناهية لإحاطته بها فتدبر فإنه مما زل فيه الاقدام . هي موجود من هذا القبيل لاحتياجنا هاهنا إلى اعتبار الموصوفية، فيرجع الترديد المذكور في الموصوفية، بأنها إما عين السواد فلا يكون مفيداً لكونه حمل الشيء على نفسه، أو غيره. فيكون حكماً بوحدة الاثنين، فيحتاج إلى موصوفية ثانية وثالثة وهلم جرا، فكان قولنا : السواد موجود باطلاً قطعاً هذا والظاهر عندي أن ما ذكره من بطلان القول المذكور، أعني قولنا : السواد موجود على تقدير احتياجه إلى تعقلات لا تتناهى حق بلا مرية وأما بطلان التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية النفس الأمرية مطلقاً، فلا أما عند الفلاسفة فلأنهم يشترطون الترتب في جريان البرهان، ولا ترتب بين تلك الأمور بحسب الخارج وهو ظاهر وأما الترتب بحسب الذهن فيتوقف على تصورها مفصلاً، والنفس لا تقدر عليه باعترافهم، وأما عند المتكلمين فلانهم استدلوا على اعتبارية الأعراض النسبية بأنها لو وجدت لاتصفت محالها بها فلها نسبة إليها بالمحلية ويعود الكلام فيها فيلزم التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة، وأنت خبير بأن هذه النسب ليست باعتبارية فرضية، بل حقيقة تتصف بها محالها في نفس الأمر فلمحالها إليها نسبة بالمحلية في نفس الأمر، ويعود الموصوف والصفة فتقوم بهما وهو (الذهن) لاستحالة قيام النسبة بغير المنتسبين، وإذا لم تقم بالذهن لم تكن أمراً ذهنياً بل خارجياً، وقد يقال: معنى كونها ذهنية أنها ليست موجوداً خارجياً، بل توجد في الذهن قائمة بالمنتسبين ( مع أن حكم الذهن) بأن السواد موصوف بالوجود في الخارج ( إما مطابق للخارج) فيكون هناك موصوفية خارجية ) ويعود (الإلزام الذي ذكرناه ( أولاً ) يكون مطابقاً له ( فلا عبرة به ) لكونه حكماً باطلاً، وقد يجاب بأن حكم الذهن يجب أن يكون مطابقاً لنفس الأمر حتى قوله : معنى كونها إلخ) وذلك لا ينافي قيامها بالطرفين، وبهذا القدر تم الجواب إلا أنه زاد عليه قوله : بل توجد إلخ لدفع ما يرد من أنها إذا لم تكن موجودة خارجية كانت موجودة في الذهن، فيلزم وجود النسبة بدون الطرفين يعني أنها توجد في الذهن قائمة بهما لا بدونهما، ومعنى ذلك أنه إذا لاحظ العقل الطرفين على نحو مخصوص انتزع اتصاف أحدهما بالآخر. قوله : ( وقد يجاب إلخ) هذا الجواب اختيار للشق الثاني ومنع للزوم كونه حكماً باطلاً فإن الباطل ما لا يطلق نفس الأمر لا ما لا يطابق الخارج، ومبني على أن يكون في الخارج ظرفاً للوجود لا الموصوف والجواب الثاني باختيار الشق الأول ومنع لزوم كونها خارجيا بمعنى الموجود في الخارج، ومبني على تقدير كونه ظرفاً للموصوف . . قوله : ( للفرق الظاهر إلخ) فإن الموجود في الخارج ما يكون الخارج ظرفاً لوجوده، لا ما يكون ظرفاً لنفسه ألا يرى أن قولنا زيد موجود في الخارج يقتضي وجود زيد فيه لا وجود وجوده . الكلام فيها فيتسلسل لكنهم لا يمنعونه، وأيضاً فهم قائلون بعدم تناهي تعلقات علم الله تعالى بالفعل، ولا يبالون بلزوم التسلسل في التعلقات مع أنهم لا يشترطون الترتب في بطلانه إلى غير ذلك من المواضع ويؤيده اتفاق الفريقين على اشتراط الوجود في جريان برهان التطبيق إنما اختلافهم في اشتراط الاجتماع في الوجود والترتب وجريان ذلك البرهان أو غيره من براهين إبطال التسلسل في تلك الأمور مطلقا غير ظاهر ثم ما ذكره من أن العقل لا يمكنه أن يعتبر ما لا نهاية له بل لا بد أن ينقطع اعتباره في مرتبة من المراتب التي لا تقف عند حد، فلا يتحقق التسلسل في نفس الأمر كلام ذكره الشارح في حواشي المطالع أيضاً، وهو محل بحث وإشكال لأن النفس أبدية بالاتفاق، فلم لا يمكن لها اعتبار أمور غير متناهية في أزمنة مستقبلة غير متناهية، فإن قلت : الاعتبارات المتحققة متناهية إذ يمكن بعدها اعتبار آخر قلت هذا من خطأ الوهم حيث : لا يلاحظ غير المتناهي كما هو وإلا لا يعقل بعد غير المتناهي في الأزمنة المستقبلة الغير المتناهية شيء فتأمل. قوله : ( لاستحالة قيام النسبة بغير المنتسبين قيل : إن أراد استحالة قيام النسبة نفسها ،فمسلم ولا يفيد وإن أراد استحالة قيام صورتها فممنوع، إذ لا فساد فيه كما في قيام صورة الجوهر بالذهن، وهذا أقرب مما نقله الشارح بقوله وقد يقال : إلخ .
And mawsufiyya (being described) is among mental posited concepts. (We said: mawsufiyya is a relation between two things having unity, and when one observes it in itself and that it is a concept among concepts, one posits for it unity, and analogize to that. And we said: the permissibility of infinite regress in it because then the chain is cut off due to the cessation of the mind's consideration, for the mind is not capable of considering infinite things in detail, and it is not obligated to intentional observation in every rank. And even if the soul is eternal, the individuals do not exist such that application (tatbiq) could occur, so there is no infinite regress. And on the supposition of its assumption, the absurdity does not follow from the necessity of finitude of what is infinite, or the deficient being like the excessive, because there is nothing actually infinite nor anything excessive in it, but merely by assumption. But if the cause of existence of that chain is something other than the mind's consideration, then infinite regress in it is invalid, otherwise the existence of infinite things in actual fact would be necessary, and application would occur in it according to us and the philosophers if there is order and coexistence in that existence, and it is not then cut off by the cessation of the mind's consideration, since the mind's consideration has no entry into its existence. Therefore they ruled the invalidity of infinite regress on the supposition of the theory of the whole, because it necessitates the existence of infinite things in the mind, since it is not cut off by the cessation of consideration. This, but there remains for me an investigation whether what we are dealing with is of this kind, because the correctness of the judgment in our saying 'blackness exists' based on otherness depends on observing mawsufiyya as a relation between the two extremes, and the instrument of observation is the state of one of them relative to the other. Then it is not possible for the mind to attribute it to blackness at all. Then if it observes it intentionally and considers that it is a concept that must be obtained for both extremes, otherwise one of them would not be obtained for the other, it considers a second mawsufiyya as an instrument for observing the state of the first mawsufiyya relative to blackness. And this observation is not necessary for the mind always, so the chain of mawsufiyyas is cut off by the cessation of its consideration. As for the theologians' allowing the non-finiteness of the relations of knowledge to acts, even though they are posited matters and not mere mental consideration, it is because these relations are not in the external world nor in the mind, so application does not occur in them. Rather they are in His knowledge, and they are finite from His perspective because He encompasses them. So ponder, for it is a matter on which feet have slipped. It is existing of this kind because we need here to consider mawsufiyya, so the earlier dilemma returns regarding mawsufiyya: either it is identical to blackness, then it is not useful because it would be predication of a thing of itself; or other, then it would be a judgment of the unity of two things, needing a second and third mawsufiyya and so on. Thus our saying 'blackness exists' would be definitively false. This, and it is apparent to me that what he mentioned about the falsehood of that saying, i.e., our saying 'blackness exists' on the supposition of its need for infinite intellections, is true without doubt. But as for the invalidity of infinite regress in mental posited matters related to actual reality (al-umur al-i'tibariyya al-nafs al-amriyya) absolutely, no. As for the philosophers, because they require order in the running of the proof, and there is no order among those things according to external existence, and that is apparent. And as for order according to the mind, it depends on their detailed conceptualization, and the soul is not capable of that by their admission. As for the theologians, because they argued for the posited nature of relative accidents (al-a'rad al-nisabiyya) by saying: if they existed, their loci would be qualified by them, so they have a relation to them of locativity, and the discussion returns to them, so infinite regress would follow in existing things. And you are aware that these relations are not merely assumed posited matters, but real things by which their loci are qualified in actual reality. Therefore their loci have a relation to them of locativity in actual reality, and the described and the attribute return, so they subsist by both, which is the mind, because it is impossible for the relation to subsist without the two relata. And if it does not subsist in the mind, it is not a mental thing but rather external. And it may be said: the meaning of its being mental is that it is not an external existent, but exists in the mind subsisting by the two relata. While the mind's judgment that blackness is described by existence in the external is either corresponding to the external, then there is an external mawsufiyya, and the objection we mentioned first returns; or not corresponding to it, so it is disregarded because it is a false judgment. And it may be answered that the mind's judgment must correspond to actual reality, so that... His saying: 'the meaning of its being etc.' That does not contradict its subsistence by the two relata. And to this extent the answer is complete, except that he added to it his saying: 'rather it exists etc.' to repel the objection that if it is not an external existent, it would exist in the mind, necessitating the existence of the relation without the two relata. Meaning that it exists in the mind subsisting by them, not without them. And the meaning of that is that when the mind observes the two relata in a specific manner, it extracts the qualification of one by the other. His saying: 'and it may be answered etc.' This answer chooses the second alternative and denies the necessity that it be a false judgment, for the false is what does not correspond to actual reality, not what does not correspond to the external. And it is based on making the external a locus for existence, not for the described. And the second answer chooses the first alternative and denies the necessity that it be external in the sense of existent in the external, and is based on considering it a locus for the described. His saying: 'for the apparent difference etc.' For the existent in the external is that for which the external is a locus for its existence, not that for which it is a locus for itself. Do you not see that our saying 'Zayd exists in the external' necessitates the existence of Zayd in it, not the existence of his existence. The discussion in it would lead to infinite regress, but they do not prevent it. Also, they are those who say that the relations of God's knowledge to acts are non-finite, and they do not care about the necessity of infinite regress in those relations, even though they do not require order in its invalidity, apart from other cases. And it is supported by the agreement of the two schools on the requirement of existence in the application of the proof of correspondence (burhan al-tatbiq). Their disagreement is only about requiring coexistence in existence and order, and the application of that proof or other proofs for nullifying infinite regress in those matters absolutely is not apparent. Then what he mentioned: that the mind cannot consider what has no end, rather its consideration must break off at a rank that does not stop at a limit, so infinite regress is not realized in actual reality. This is a statement that the commentator mentioned in his glosses on al-Matali' as well, and it is a place of investigation and difficulty, because the soul is eternal by agreement, so why can it not consider infinite things in infinite future times? If you say: the actual considerations are finite, since another consideration can occur after them, I say: this is a mistake of the imagination, because it does not consider the infinite as it is, otherwise nothing is conceivable after the infinite in infinite future times. So contemplate. His saying: 'because it is impossible for the relation to subsist without the two relata.' It is said: If he intended the impossibility of the relation itself subsisting, it is conceded but does not help; if he intended the impossibility of its form subsisting, it is denied, for there is no corruption in it, as in the subsistence of the form of substance in the mind. And this is closer than what the commentator transmitted in his saying 'and it may be said etc.'
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the nature of mawsufiyya (the property of being described) and the issue of infinite regress (tasalsul) in mental considerations. The author begins by stating that mawsufiyya is among mental posited concepts. He explains that when the mind observes mawsufiyya as a relation between two things and considers it in itself, it posits unity for it. He then argues that infinite regress in such mental considerations is permissible because the chain of considerations is cut off when the mind stops considering; the mind cannot consider infinite things in detail, nor is it obligated to intentional observation at every rank. Even if the soul is eternal, the individuals (individual considerations) do not exist in actuality such that the proof of correspondence (tatbiq) could apply, so no actual infinite regress occurs. However, if the chain's existence originates from something other than mental consideration (i.e., real external existence), then infinite regress is invalid because it would entail actual infinite things, and the proof of correspondence would apply since there is order and coexistence in that existence. The author then qualifies his discussion by noting that what he is dealing with (the judgment 'blackness exists') may not fall under the same category. He explains that the correctness of this judgment, if based on otherness (ghayriyya) between subject and predicate, depends on observing mawsufiyya as a relation between the two extremes. But then the mind cannot directly predicate mawsufiyya of blackness; it must observe it intentionally, and this observation is not always necessary, so the chain of mawsufiyyas is cut off. He contrasts this with the theologians' allowing infinite relations of God's knowledge to acts, because those relations are not in the external world nor in the mind, so the proof of correspondence does not apply. The passage then discusses a dilemma: either mawsufiyya is the same as blackness (making the predication trivial) or different (requiring a second mawsufiyya and so on, leading to infinite regress). The author concludes that if that infinite regress is required, the judgment 'blackness exists' would be false. He then clarifies that the falsehood of that judgment under that supposition is correct, but the absolute invalidity of infinite regress in mental posited matters (al-umur al-i'tibariyya al-nafs al-amriyya) is not established. He explains the positions of philosophers and theologians: philosophers require order in the proof, and there is no order among such things externally; the soul cannot conceptualize them in detail. Theologians argue that relative accidents are mental, otherwise they would lead to infinite regress in existing things, but the author notes that these relations are not merely assumed but real. The discussion continues with objections and answers about whether mental judgment must correspond to external reality, and whether the relation subsists in the mind or externally. The passage ends with a critical comment on the claim that the mind cannot consider infinite things, arguing that since the soul is eternal, it could potentially consider infinite things in infinite future times, and the objection that actual considerations are finite is dismissed as a mistake of the imagination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That infinite regress is always invalid in all contexts, including mental posited matters.
Correction: In mental posited matters, infinite regress is not absolutely invalid; philosophers and theologians have differing conditions for its invalidity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do philosophers require for the proof of correspondence to apply?
A) Existence and order
B) Only existence
C) Only order
D) Neither existence nor order

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress is not absolutely invalid in mental posited matters; philosophers require order, and theologians have their own arguments, but the author questions the universal applicability of the proof of correspondence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological puzzle that seems to lead to an infinite regress, do you quickly assume it is impossible, or do you pause to consider whether it pertains to your mind's finite capacity or to a deeper reality that you cannot fully conceptualize? How might this lesson change your impulse in those moments?
?
The passage says the soul is eternal and could potentially consider infinite things. Reflect on a time you felt overwhelmed by the infinite nature of a concept (e.g., God's knowledge, the afterlife). Did you trust that your inability to grasp it does not negate its truth? What would it mean for you to live with that trust today?
?
The author challenges the assumption that the mind can only consider finite things. If your reasoning faculties are not the ultimate measure of truth, how does that affect your confidence in your own theological conclusions? Are you willing to hold your beliefs with humility, knowing that your mind might not be capable of fully grasping the arguments?

Objections and Answers on the Subsistence of Relations
Jurjani responds to an objection about the subsistence of relations and prefers a specific answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections regarding the subsistence of relations and the different answers proposed.

Core Idea
The subsistence of relations in the mind avoids the need for external infinite regress.

This final passage addresses a specific objection about the impossibility of a relation subsisting without its two relata. The author presents a precise distinction between the relation itself and its form in the mind, and evaluates the different answers transmitted by the commentator. This technical discussion refines the understanding of mental concepts and their relationship to reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the short Arabic passage and translation carefully to understand the distinction made.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Notice the two possible meanings of 'impossible for the relation to subsist without the two relata': either the relation itself or its form.
3
Evaluate the author's preference
The author favors the second interpretation (form) and explains why it is more plausible.

And mawsufiyya (being described) is among mental posited concepts. (We said: mawsufiyya is a relation between two things having unity, and when one observes it in itself and that it is a concept among concepts, one posits for it unity, and analogize to that. And we said: the permissibility of infinite regress in it because then the chain is cut off due to the cessation of the mind's consideration, for the mind is not capable of considering infinite things in detail, and it is not obligated to intentional observation in every rank. And even if the soul is eternal, the individuals do not exist such that application (tatbiq) could occur, so there is no infinite regress. And on the supposition of its assumption, the absurdity does not follow from the necessity of finitude of what is infinite, or the deficient being like the excessive, because there is nothing actually infinite nor anything excessive in it, but merely by assumption. But if the cause of existence of that chain is something other than the mind's consideration, then infinite regress in it is invalid, otherwise the existence of infinite things in actual fact would be necessary, and application would occur in it according to us and the philosophers if there is order and coexistence in that existence, and it is not then cut off by the cessation of the mind's consideration, since the mind's consideration has no entry into its existence. Therefore they ruled the invalidity of infinite regress on the supposition of the theory of the whole, because it necessitates the existence of infinite things in the mind, since it is not cut off by the cessation of consideration. This, but there remains for me an investigation whether what we are dealing with is of this kind, because the correctness of the judgment in our saying 'blackness exists' based on otherness depends on observing mawsufiyya as a relation between the two extremes, and the instrument of observation is the state of one of them relative to the other. Then it is not possible for the mind to attribute it to blackness at all. Then if it observes it intentionally and considers that it is a concept that must be obtained for both extremes, otherwise one of them would not be obtained for the other, it considers a second mawsufiyya as an instrument for observing the state of the first mawsufiyya relative to blackness. And this observation is not necessary for the mind always, so the chain of mawsufiyyas is cut off by the cessation of its consideration. As for the theologians' allowing the non-finiteness of the relations of knowledge to acts, even though they are posited matters and not mere mental consideration, it is because these relations are not in the external world nor in the mind, so application does not occur in them. Rather they are in His knowledge, and they are finite from His perspective because He encompasses them. So ponder, for it is a matter on which feet have slipped. It is existing of this kind because we need here to consider mawsufiyya, so the earlier dilemma returns regarding mawsufiyya: either it is identical to blackness, then it is not useful because it would be predication of a thing of itself; or other, then it would be a judgment of the unity of two things, needing a second and third mawsufiyya and so on. Thus our saying 'blackness exists' would be definitively false. This, and it is apparent to me that what he mentioned about the falsehood of that saying, i.e., our saying 'blackness exists' on the supposition of its need for infinite intellections, is true without doubt. But as for the invalidity of infinite regress in mental posited matters related to actual reality (al-umur al-i'tibariyya al-nafs al-amriyya) absolutely, no. As for the philosophers, because they require order in the running of the proof, and there is no order among those things according to external existence, and that is apparent. And as for order according to the mind, it depends on their detailed conceptualization, and the soul is not capable of that by their admission. As for the theologians, because they argued for the posited nature of relative accidents (al-a'rad al-nisabiyya) by saying: if they existed, their loci would be qualified by them, so they have a relation to them of locativity, and the discussion returns to them, so infinite regress would follow in existing things. And you are aware that these relations are not merely assumed posited matters, but real things by which their loci are qualified in actual reality. Therefore their loci have a relation to them of locativity in actual reality, and the described and the attribute return, so they subsist by both, which is the mind, because it is impossible for the relation to subsist without the two relata. And if it does not subsist in the mind, it is not a mental thing but rather external. And it may be said: the meaning of its being mental is that it is not an external existent, but exists in the mind subsisting by the two relata. While the mind's judgment that blackness is described by existence in the external is either corresponding to the external, then there is an external mawsufiyya, and the objection we mentioned first returns; or not corresponding to it, so it is disregarded because it is a false judgment. And it may be answered that the mind's judgment must correspond to actual reality, so that... His saying: 'the meaning of its being etc.' That does not contradict its subsistence by the two relata. And to this extent the answer is complete, except that he added to it his saying: 'rather it exists etc.' to repel the objection that if it is not an external existent, it would exist in the mind, necessitating the existence of the relation without the two relata. Meaning that it exists in the mind subsisting by them, not without them. And the meaning of that is that when the mind observes the two relata in a specific manner, it extracts the qualification of one by the other. His saying: 'and it may be answered etc.' This answer chooses the second alternative and denies the necessity that it be a false judgment, for the false is what does not correspond to actual reality, not what does not correspond to the external. And it is based on making the external a locus for existence, not for the described. And the second answer chooses the first alternative and denies the necessity that it be external in the sense of existent in the external, and is based on considering it a locus for the described. His saying: 'for the apparent difference etc.' For the existent in the external is that for which the external is a locus for its existence, not that for which it is a locus for itself. Do you not see that our saying 'Zayd exists in the external' necessitates the existence of Zayd in it, not the existence of his existence. The discussion in it would lead to infinite regress, but they do not prevent it. Also, they are those who say that the relations of God's knowledge to acts are non-finite, and they do not care about the necessity of infinite regress in those relations, even though they do not require order in its invalidity, apart from other cases. And it is supported by the agreement of the two schools on the requirement of existence in the application of the proof of correspondence (burhan al-tatbiq). Their disagreement is only about requiring coexistence in existence and order, and the application of that proof or other proofs for nullifying infinite regress in those matters absolutely is not apparent. Then what he mentioned: that the mind cannot consider what has no end, rather its consideration must break off at a rank that does not stop at a limit, so infinite regress is not realized in actual reality. This is a statement that the commentator mentioned in his glosses on al-Matali' as well, and it is a place of investigation and difficulty, because the soul is eternal by agreement, so why can it not consider infinite things in infinite future times? If you say: the actual considerations are finite, since another consideration can occur after them, I say: this is a mistake of the imagination, because it does not consider the infinite as it is, otherwise nothing is conceivable after the infinite in infinite future times. So contemplate. His saying: 'because it is impossible for the relation to subsist without the two relata.' It is said: If he intended the impossibility of the relation itself subsisting, it is conceded but does not help; if he intended the impossibility of its form subsisting, it is denied, for there is no corruption in it, as in the subsistence of the form of substance in the mind. And this is closer than what the commentator transmitted in his saying 'and it may be said etc.'
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the nature of mawsufiyya (the property of being described) and the issue of infinite regress (tasalsul) in mental considerations. The author begins by stating that mawsufiyya is among mental posited concepts. He explains that when the mind observes mawsufiyya as a relation between two things and considers it in itself, it posits unity for it. He then argues that infinite regress in such mental considerations is permissible because the chain of considerations is cut off when the mind stops considering; the mind cannot consider infinite things in detail, nor is it obligated to intentional observation at every rank. Even if the soul is eternal, the individuals (individual considerations) do not exist in actuality such that the proof of correspondence (tatbiq) could apply, so no actual infinite regress occurs. However, if the chain's existence originates from something other than mental consideration (i.e., real external existence), then infinite regress is invalid because it would entail actual infinite things, and the proof of correspondence would apply since there is order and coexistence in that existence. The author then qualifies his discussion by noting that what he is dealing with (the judgment 'blackness exists') may not fall under the same category. He explains that the correctness of this judgment, if based on otherness (ghayriyya) between subject and predicate, depends on observing mawsufiyya as a relation between the two extremes. But then the mind cannot directly predicate mawsufiyya of blackness; it must observe it intentionally, and this observation is not always necessary, so the chain of mawsufiyyas is cut off. He contrasts this with the theologians' allowing infinite relations of God's knowledge to acts, because those relations are not in the external world nor in the mind, so the proof of correspondence does not apply. The passage then discusses a dilemma: either mawsufiyya is the same as blackness (making the predication trivial) or different (requiring a second mawsufiyya and so on, leading to infinite regress). The author concludes that if that infinite regress is required, the judgment 'blackness exists' would be false. He then clarifies that the falsehood of that judgment under that supposition is correct, but the absolute invalidity of infinite regress in mental posited matters (al-umur al-i'tibariyya al-nafs al-amriyya) is not established. He explains the positions of philosophers and theologians: philosophers require order in the proof, and there is no order among such things externally; the soul cannot conceptualize them in detail. Theologians argue that relative accidents are mental, otherwise they would lead to infinite regress in existing things, but the author notes that these relations are not merely assumed but real. The discussion continues with objections and answers about whether mental judgment must correspond to external reality, and whether the relation subsists in the mind or externally. The passage ends with a critical comment on the claim that the mind cannot consider infinite things, arguing that since the soul is eternal, it could potentially consider infinite things in infinite future times, and the objection that actual considerations are finite is dismissed as a mistake of the imagination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a mental relation cannot exist in the mind without its actual relata being present.
Correction: The form of a relation can subsist in the mind independently, just as the form of a substance can.

Checkpoint
What does the author concede if the statement is interpreted as referring to the relation itself?
A) It is false
B) It is true but does not help
C) It is impossible
D) It is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The form of a relation can subsist in the mind without its relata, making mental concepts possible without entailing external infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between the relation itself and its mental form. In your own understanding, are God's attributes (like His mercy) merely mental forms or realities beyond your mind? How does this distinction affect your worship?
?
Think of a relationship you have that you cannot currently experience (e.g., with a distant friend). Does that relationship exist in your mind? How does this lesson help you see that mental realities are not nothing?
?
If the form of a relation can subsist in the mind without its relata, what does that say about the power of your intellect to hold truths that are not physically present? Will you trust your mind's ability to grasp unseen realities like the afterlife?

The Subsistence of the Relation in the Mind and the Two Answers
Explains why relation is mental and gives two responses to objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the relation (nisba) between a subject and its attribute must subsist in the mind, and how two key answers defend this view against an objection.

Core Idea
The relation between a thing and its attribute exists only in the mind, not outside.

In this passage, al-Iji explains that the relation between a described thing and its attribute cannot exist outside the mind because it requires both relata together. An objection arises: if our mental judgment about blackness being in the external is true, doesn't that imply the relation is external? The author provides two refined answers, distinguishing between truth as correspondence to the real state of affairs versus external existence. Let's dive in.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the argument structure: claim, objection, two answers.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage states that the relation (nisba) must subsist in the mind because it cannot subsist without its two relata (the described and the attribute). The mind is where both relata can be present together.
This is key to understanding why the relation is mental, not external.
3
Understand the objection
An objector argues that if the mind's judgment (e.g., 'blackness exists externally') is true, then the relation of 'being-described' would exist externally, contradicting the claim that the relation is mental.
The objection is based on the assumption that true judgments about external things imply external relations.
4
Examine the two answers
The first answer says truth requires correspondence to the real state of affairs (nafs al-amr), not necessarily to external reality. The second answer distinguishes between 'in the external' as a container for the relation itself vs. for its existence.
These answers show that the relation remains mental even when judgments about external things are true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموصوف والصفة فتقوم بهما وهو (الذهن) لاستحالة قيام النسبة بغير المنتسبين، وإذا لم تقم بالذهن لم تكن أمراً ذهنياً بل خارجياً، وقد يقال: معنى كونها ذهنية أنها ليست موجوداً خارجياً، بل توجد في الذهن قائمة بالمنتسبين ( مع أن حكم الذهن) بأن السواد موصوف بالوجود في الخارج ( إما مطابق للخارج) فيكون هناك موصوفية خارجية ) ويعود (الإلزام الذي ذكرناه ( أولاً ) يكون مطابقاً له ( فلا عبرة به ) لكونه حكماً باطلاً، وقد يجاب بأن حكم الذهن يجب أن يكون مطابقاً لنفس الأمر حتى يكون صادقاً لا للخارج، فإنه أخص منها وأيضاً إذا صدق أن هذا موصوف بكذا في الخارج، لم يلزم وجود الموصوفية في الخارج للفرق الظاهر بين أن يكون قولنا في الخارج ظرفاً لنفس الموصوفية، وبين أن يكون ظرفاً لوجودها. قوله : معنى كونها إلخ) وذلك لا ينافي قيامها بالطرفين، وبهذا القدر تم الجواب إلا أنه زاد عليه قوله : بل توجد إلخ لدفع ما يرد من أنها إذا لم تكن موجودة خارجية كانت موجودة في الذهن، فيلزم وجود النسبة بدون الطرفين يعني أنها توجد في الذهن قائمة بهما لا بدونهما، ومعنى ذلك أنه إذا لاحظ العقل الطرفين على نحو مخصوص انتزع اتصاف أحدهما بالآخر. قوله : ( وقد يجاب إلخ) هذا الجواب اختيار للشق الثاني ومنع للزوم كونه حكماً باطلاً فإن الباطل ما لا يطلق نفس الأمر لا ما لا يطابق الخارج، ومبني على أن يكون في الخارج ظرفاً للوجود لا الموصوف والجواب الثاني باختيار الشق الأول ومنع لزوم كونها خارجيا بمعنى الموجود في الخارج، ومبني على تقدير كونه ظرفاً للموصوف .
The described and the attribute, so it subsists by them, and it is the mind, due to the impossibility of the relation subsisting without the two relata. And if it does not subsist in the mind, it would not be a mental thing but an external one. And it may be said: the meaning of its being mental is that it is not an external existent, rather it exists in the mind subsisting in the two relata, while the mind's judgment that blackness is described by existence in the external either corresponds to the external, so there is an external being-described, and the implication we mentioned earlier returns and corresponds to it, so it is disregarded because it is a false judgment. And it may be answered that the mind's judgment must correspond to the real state of affairs itself to be truthful, not to the external, for it is more specific than it. Also, if it is true that this is described as such in the external, the existence of the being-described in the external does not necessarily follow, due to the apparent difference between our saying 'in the external' being a container for the being-described itself or a container for its existence. His saying: 'the meaning of its being mental etc.' — that does not negate its subsistence by the two relata, and with this much the answer is completed, except that he added his saying 'rather it exists etc.' to repel what is objected that if it is not an external existent, it would exist in the mind, necessitating the existence of the relation without the two relata, meaning it exists in the mind subsisting by them, not without them. And the meaning of that is that when the intellect observes the two relata in a specific manner, it extracts the attribution of one to the other. His saying: 'and it may be answered etc.' — this answer is a choice of the second side and a prevention of the necessity of it being a false judgment, for the false is that which does not correspond to the real state of affairs, not that which does not correspond to the external. And it is based on considering the external as a container for existence, not for the described. And the second answer is by choosing the first side and preventing the necessity of it being external in the sense of existent in the external, and it is based on considering it as a container for the described.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of the relation (nisba) between a subject and a predicate in mental judgments. The author of the main text (likely al-Iji) states that the relation subsists in the mind because it cannot subsist without its two relata (the described and the attribute). If it were not mental, it would be external, but that leads to problems. An objector says that mental judgments about external things would then imply that the relation exists externally. The author offers two answers. First, the mind's judgment must correspond to the real state of affairs (nafs al-amr), not necessarily to external reality. Second, even if the judgment is true, it does not require the external existence of the relation itself because 'in the external' can be a container for the existence of the relation or for the relation itself. The commentator (al-Sharif al-Jurjani) clarifies that the first answer uses the distinction between the thing and its existence, and the two choices (shiq) correspond to whether the external is a container for existence or for the thing. The argument is a key part of the discussion on necessary sciences (al-‘ulum al-daruriyya) and the nature of mental existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a judgment about external existence is true, the relation must exist externally as well.
Correction: Truth of a judgment is about correspondence to the real state of affairs (nafs al-amr), not about the existence of the relation itself. The relation remains mental even when the judgment is true.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the relation (nisba) between subject and attribute subsist in the mind?
A) Because it cannot subsist without the two relata, and the mind is the only place where both relata can coexist.
B) Because external existence is impossible for any relation.
C) Because the mind creates the relation by its own power.
D) Because the relation is identical to the subject itself.

Key Takeaway
The relation is mental; truth of judgments does not imply external relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that Allah is All-Hearing. Does that attribute exist in your mind only, or does it correspond to something real beyond your concept? If the relation is mental, does it weaken your conviction that Allah truly hears? Examine your heart: do you sometimes reduce God's attributes to mere ideas in your mind, forgetting that they correspond to a greater reality? Resolve today to affirm His attributes as real in the way that pleases Him, not merely as concepts.
?
This lesson teaches that truth is correspondence to the real state of affairs, not necessarily to external appearances. Think about your faith: have you ever evaluated your iman based on what is outwardly visible—your actions, your words—rather than its inner reality before Allah? How often do you worry about how your faith appears to others rather than its truth in your soul? Commit to seeking the reality of your relationship with Allah, not just its external form.
?
When you say 'I am a believer,' your mind judges that faith is an attribute of you. Is this relation mental or external? If mental, does that make your faith less real? The passage shows that mental relations can still be true. But do you live as if your faith is merely a mental concept, or do you let it transform your life? Ask yourself: is my faith merely a judgment in my mind, or does it correspond to a deeper reality that changes how I live? Make a concrete change today to align your external actions with the inner truth of your belief.

Objection on the Relation Without Relata
Objection and answer on the impossibility of the relation subsisting without the relata.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's distinction between the real relation and its mental image, and why the mental image can subsist without the relata.

Core Idea
The mental image of a relation can subsist without the relata, unlike the real relation.

This passage presents a sharp objection to the claim that a relation cannot subsist without its two relata. The objector carefully distinguishes two meanings of 'relation' and shows that only one of them is impossible. This distinction is crucial for understanding how mental concepts can exist independently of external realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: 'if he intends... then...; if he intends... then...'.
The passage is a single objection with two conditional branches.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The objector considers two possible meanings of 'relation': the relation itself (real) and its image (mental). Understand why the first is impossible but the second is possible.
The analogy of a substance's image in the mind supports the possibility of mental images.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the example of a substance's image subsisting in the mind without the actual substance. How does this illustrate the objector's point about the relation's image?
4
Summarize the objection
In your own words, restate the objector's position: what is granted, what is denied, and why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لاستحالة قيام النسبة بغير المنتسبين قيل : إن أراد استحالة قيام النسبة نفسها ،فمسلم ولا يفيد وإن أراد استحالة قيام صورتها فممنوع، إذ لا فساد فيه كما في قيام صورة الجوهر بالذهن، وهذا أقرب مما نقله الشارح بقوله وقد يقال : إلخ .
His saying: 'due to the impossibility of the relation subsisting without the two relata' — it is said: if he intends the impossibility of the subsistence of the relation itself, then it is granted but it does not benefit; and if he intends the impossibility of the subsistence of its image, then it is denied, for there is no corruption in that, as in the subsistence of the image of a substance in the mind. And this is more likely than what the commentator transmitted with his saying 'and it may be said etc.'
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the main argument that the relation cannot subsist without its relata. The objector (qila) makes a distinction: if 'relation' means the real relation itself, then it is indeed impossible for it to subsist without the two relata, but this does not help the opponent because the real relation is not what is in question. If it means the mental image or form (sura) of the relation, then it is possible for that image to subsist in the mind without the actual relata, just as the image of a substance can subsist in the mind without the substance itself. The commentator (al-Sharif) considers this objection stronger than the one he previously transmitted ('and it may be said...').

Key Terms

صورة
جوهر
ذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a relation cannot subsist without its relata, then nothing related to it can exist without them.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only the real relation itself is impossible to subsist without relata; the mental image of the relation can subsist independently, just as a substance's image can exist in the mind without the substance.

Checkpoint
