A) The relation cannot subsist without the relata in any sense.
B) The relation itself cannot subsist without the relata, but its image can.
C) The relation is identical to the relata.
D) The mind creates the relation from nothing.

Key Takeaway
The objection distinguishes between the real relation (impossible to lack relata) and its mental image (possible to subsist alone).

The Apparent Difference: Container for the Thing vs. Its Existence
Explains the apparent difference between being a container for something and for its existence, with implications for infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subtle distinction between 'container for the thing' and 'container for its existence' and how it relates to debates about infinite regress in theology.

Core Idea
The phrase 'in the external' can mean either container for the thing itself or for its existence—a crucial distinction for arguments on infinite regress.

This lesson unpacks a key distinction made by the commentator to resolve an objection. When we say something exists 'in the external,' does that mean the external contains the thing itself or its existence? The answer leads into a discussion about infinite regress (tasalsul) in divine knowledge and the nature of the soul's contemplation. We'll read the passage step by step and clarify its logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the opening phrase 'للفرق الظاهر' and the example of Zayd.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the distinction
Find where the commentator explains the difference between being a container for the thing itself and being a container for its existence. Note how the example of 'Zayd exists in the external' illustrates this.
The key phrase is 'الموجود في الخارج ما يكون الخارج ظرفاً لوجوده، لا ما يكون ظرفاً لنفسه'.
3
Analyze the infinite regress discussion
The passage then moves to infinite regress (تسلسل). Notice how the commentator connects the distinction to the issue of relations and divine knowledge. Also note the reference to 'برهان التطبيق' (proof of application).
Consider why the commentator mentions the agreement of the two groups on this proof.
4
Reflect on the soul's consideration of the infinite
The final part critiques the idea that the intellect cannot consider an infinite series. The commentator argues that the soul is eternal and can consider infinite future things. Ponder the final caution 'فتأمل'.
This part is complex; think about the distinction between actual and potential considerations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( للفرق الظاهر إلخ) فإن الموجود في الخارج ما يكون الخارج ظرفاً لوجوده، لا ما يكون ظرفاً لنفسه ألا يرى أن قولنا زيد موجود في الخارج يقتضي وجود زيد فيه لا وجود وجوده . الكلام فيها فيتسلسل لكنهم لا يمنعونه، وأيضاً فهم قائلون بعدم تناهي تعلقات علم الله تعالى بالفعل، ولا يبالون بلزوم التسلسل في التعلقات مع أنهم لا يشترطون الترتب في بطلانه إلى غير ذلك من المواضع ويؤيده اتفاق الفريقين على اشتراط الوجود في جريان برهان التطبيق إنما اختلافهم في اشتراط الاجتماع في الوجود والترتب وجريان ذلك البرهان أو غيره من براهين إبطال التسلسل في تلك الأمور مطلقا غير ظاهر ثم ما ذكره من أن العقل لا يمكنه أن يعتبر ما لا نهاية له بل لا بد أن ينقطع اعتباره في مرتبة من المراتب التي لا تقف عند حد، فلا يتحقق التسلسل في نفس الأمر كلام ذكره الشارح في حواشي المطالع أيضاً، وهو محل بحث وإشكال لأن النفس أبدية بالاتفاق، فلم لا يمكن لها اعتبار أمور غير متناهية في أزمنة مستقبلة غير متناهية، فإن قلت : الاعتبارات المتحققة متناهية إذ يمكن بعدها اعتبار آخر قلت هذا من خطأ الوهم حيث : لا يلاحظ غير المتناهي كما هو وإلا لا يعقل بعد غير المتناهي في الأزمنة المستقبلة الغير المتناهية شيء فتأمل.
His saying: 'for the apparent difference etc.' For the existent in the external is that for which the external is a container for its existence, not a container for itself. Do you not see that our saying 'Zayd exists in the external' necessitates the existence of Zayd in it, not the existence of his existence. The discussion about it leads to infinite regress, but they do not prevent it. Also, they are those who say that the attachments of God's knowledge to actual things are infinite, and they do not care about the necessity of infinite regress in those attachments, even though they do not require succession in its invalidity, and other similar places. This is supported by the agreement of the two groups on the condition of existence for running the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq). Their difference is only about the condition of simultaneity in existence and succession, and the running of that proof or other proofs of refuting infinite regress in those matters absolutely is not apparent. Then what he mentioned that the intellect cannot consider what is infinite, but rather its consideration must terminate at some rank of ranks that does not stop at a limit, so infinite regress does not obtain in reality—this is something that the commentator mentioned in his glosses on al-Matali‘ as well, and it is a place of investigation and difficulty, because the soul is eternal by agreement, so why can it not consider infinite things in infinite future times? If you say: the actual considerations are finite, since after them another consideration is possible, I say: this is an error of estimation, for it does not observe the infinite as it is; otherwise, after the infinite in the infinite future times nothing is conceivable. So ponder.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the phrase 'the apparent difference' from the previous argument. The commentator clarifies the distinction between saying 'in the external' as a container for the thing itself versus a container for its existence. For example, 'Zayd exists in the external' means the external contains Zayd's existence, not Zayd himself in a different sense. This leads to a discussion about infinite regress (tasalsul). The commentator notes that some thinkers (like philosophers) allow infinite regress in certain contexts, such as the infinite attachments of God's knowledge. They do not require succession for invalidity. The commentator then mentions that the agreement of the two groups (Ash‘aris and philosophers) on the condition of existence for running the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) shows that the difference is only about simultaneity and succession, not about the proof itself. Finally, the commentator cites a point from the commentator's own glosses on al-Matali‘: the intellect cannot consider an infinite series because its consideration must terminate at some limit. However, this is problematic because the soul is eternal and can consider infinite future things. The passage ends with a caution to ponder.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'in the external' always means the same thing, either container for the thing or for its existence without distinction.
Correction: The passage explicitly distinguishes: the external is a container for the existence of the thing, not for the thing itself. This distinction resolves apparent difficulties about relations existing in the external.

C) The difference between a necessary and a contingent being.
D) The difference between the subject and the predicate in a judgment.

Key Takeaway
The apparent difference between being a container for the thing itself and for its existence is crucial for resolving objections about the external existence of relations and opens a deeper debate on the nature of infinite regress in divine attributes.

Negative Propositions Imply Subject Existence
Proves that negative propositions imply subject existence and refutes an objection about time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why negative propositions like 'blackness does not exist' are self-contradictory or impossible, and how this argument establishes the necessity of existence.

Core Idea
Even negation presupposes existence.

This lesson examines a foundational argument in Islamic theology: when we say something does not exist, we must already have some conception of that thing. The author demonstrates that such negative statements either involve a logical contradiction (if existence is identical to essence) or lead to an infinite regress (if existence is other than essence). This argument ultimately points to the necessity of existence itself, a key concept in proving God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the two possible relationships between existence and essence that are considered.
Pay attention to the terms 'nafy' and 'salb'.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage considers two scenarios: (1) when the existence of a thing is identical to its essence, and (2) when existence is other than essence. Understand the implication for negation in each.
In the first case, negation is a direct contradiction; in the second, it leads to an impossible condition.
3
Analyze the objection about time
The objection from al-Abhari suggests that contradiction only follows if the time of negation and affirmation are the same. See how the author refutes this by noting that the self-contradiction is inherent.
The refutation shows that the objection is weak; the contradiction is in the meaning itself, not dependent on time.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument about negative propositions is used to argue for the necessity of existence, a key step in proving the existence of a necessary being.
This connects to the concept of 'necessary existence' (wajib al-wujud).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النفي وهو قولنا : السواد ليس بموجود فلان وجوده إما نفسه فنفيه عنه أي سلب الوجود عن السواد حينئذ تناقض لأنه سلب الشيء عن نفسه أو غيره وهو باطل لوجهين. الأول قوله : فيتوقف نفيه عنه على تصوره أي يتوقف نفي الوجود عن السواد على تصور السواد المحكوم عليه بذلك النفي وهو أي تصور السواد يستدعي تميزه وثبوته لما عرفت في الوجه الأول من الوجوه الأربعة، فيكون حصول الوجود للسواد شرطاً في نفس الوجود عنه وهو محال وليس ثبوت السواد في الذهن حتى يقال : قوله : لأنه سلب الشيء عن نفسه بناء على أن مفهوم قولنا السواد ليس بموجود سلب الوجود عن السواد والوجود نفسه، فيكون سلب الشيء عن نفسه فاندفع ما توهم من أن المراد ينفي وجود السواد عند من يقول : إن وجوده عينه نفي نفس السواد لا إثبات النفي له، فلا يلزم التناقض وإنما كان سلب الشيء عن نفسه تناقضاً، لأن ثبوت الشيء لنفسه دائم، وإطلاق السلب يناقضه فاندفع ما توهم من أنه إنما يلزم التناقض لو اتحد زمان السلب والإيجاب وهو ممنوع. قوله : وهو محال لاستلزامه اجتماع النقيضين وقد قلتم : إن الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون . قوله : وليس ثبوت السواد إلخ لا يخفى على الفطن أن ثبوت السواد في الذهن لا دخل له في التفريع المذكور، بقوله : حتى يقال إلخ لأنه مبني على عدم عموم نفي الثبوت، حتى لو كان النفي مختصاً بالثبوت الخارجي لم يكن ثبوت السواد في الذهن منافياً له، وإن نفيه غير صحيح في نفسه لكون ثبوته في الذهن لازماً مما ذكر، فالواجب أن يقال: وليس نفسه في الخارج والصواب أن يقال: أن قوله وليس في الذهن جملة حالية والمعنى أن تصور السواد قوله : لا للخارج فإنه أخص منها فيه بحث لأن نفس الأمر وإن كان أعم من الخارج إلا أن الحكم المذكور هاهنا هو أن السواد موصوف بالوجود في الخارج، على أن في الخارج متعلق بلموصوف لا بالوجود كما يدل عليه قوله، وأيضاً إذا صدق أن هذا موصوف بكذا في الخارج إلخ، ولا يخفى أن صدقه إنما هو بمطابقته للخارج فالجواب الحق هو الذي ذكره بقوله وأيضاً فتدبر. قوله : فنفيه عنه تناقض قال الأبهري: لقائل أن يقول : إنما يلزم التناقض أن لو اتحد زمان الإيجاب والسلب وهو ممنوع وضعفه غير خفي للفطن، نعم يمكن أن يجاب بأن المراد ينفي وجود السواد عند من يقول : بأن وجوده عينه نفي نفس السواد لا إثبات النفي له فلا يلزم التناقض .
As for negation, which is our saying 'blackness does not exist' — because its existence is either itself, so negating it from it, i.e., the negation of existence from blackness, is then a contradiction, because it is the negation of a thing from itself; or it is other, and it is invalid for two reasons. The first is his saying: its negation from it depends on its conception, i.e., the negation of existence from blackness depends on the conception of blackness which is the subject of that negation, and that, i.e., the conception of blackness, requires its distinction and its existence, as you knew in the first of the four aspects. So the occurrence of existence for blackness is a condition for the very negation from it, and that is impossible. And the existence of blackness is not in the mind such that it could be said... His saying: 'because it is the negation of a thing from itself' based on the understanding that the concept of our saying 'blackness does not exist' is the negation of existence from blackness, and existence itself, so it is the negation of a thing from itself. Thus what was imagined is repelled, that the intended meaning is to deny the existence of blackness according to the one who says that its existence is identical to it, negating blackness itself, not affirming negation for it, so contradiction does not follow. The negation of a thing from itself is a contradiction, because a thing's establishment for itself is perpetual, and the utterance of negation contradicts it. So what was imagined is repelled, that contradiction follows only if the time of negation and affirmation are one, and that is denied. His saying: 'and it is impossible because it necessitates the conjunction of two contradictories, and you have said that a thing either is or is not.' His saying: 'and the existence of blackness is not in the mind etc.' — it is not hidden from the intelligent that the existence of blackness in the mind has no bearing on the mentioned inference, because it is based on the non-generality of the negation of existence, such that even if the negation is specific to external existence, the existence of blackness in the mind does not contradict it, although its negation is not correct in itself because its existence in the mind is necessary from what was mentioned. So it is necessary to say: and it is not itself in the external. And the correct thing is to say: that his saying 'and not in the mind' is a circumstantial clause, and the meaning is that the conception of blackness... His saying: 'not to the external, for it is more specific than it' — there is investigation in this, because the real state of affairs (nafs al-amr), although it is more general than the external, the judgment mentioned here is that blackness is described by existence in the external, with the understanding that 'in the external' is attached to the described, not to existence, as his saying indicates. Also, if it is true that this is described as such in the external, etc., and it is not hidden that its truth is only by its correspondence to the external, so the correct answer is what he mentioned with his saying 'and also', so ponder. His saying: 'so negating it from him is a contradiction' — al-Abhari said: it is possible for someone to say that contradiction follows only if the time of affirmation and negation are one, and that is denied, and its weakness is not hidden from the intelligent. Yes, one can answer that the intended meaning is to negate the existence of blackness according to the one who says that its existence is identical to it, negating blackness itself, not affirming negation for it, so contradiction does not follow.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about negative propositions (nafy), specifically the statement 'blackness does not exist'. The author argues that such a statement is self-contradictory if existence is identical to the essence (as some hold) because it would be negating a thing from itself. Alternatively, if existence is other than essence, the negation requires the conception of the subject, which itself requires the subject's existence (either mentally or externally) — thus the negation presupposes the existence of the subject, leading to a paradox. The commentator then discusses an objection by al-Abhari that the contradiction only follows if the time of negation and affirmation are the same, but the author dismisses this. The correct view is that the negation of existence from a thing entails the thing's existence in some sense, otherwise the proposition is meaningless. The passage also clarifies that the phrase 'in the external' in the original argument about the relation is attached to the described, not to the existence. The overall argument is a standard kalam proof for the necessity of existence for meaningful propositions.

Key Terms

سلب
تناقض
تصور
وجود
نفس الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negative propositions like 'X does not exist' have no ontological implications.
Correction: This passage shows that even negation requires the subject to have some form of existence (mental or external) to be meaningful, otherwise the proposition is either self-contradictory or impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why is the statement 'blackness does not exist' problematic?
A) Because blackness necessarily exists in the external.
B) Because it requires the conception of blackness, which presupposes its existence, leading to a contradiction or regress.
C) Because the word 'blackness' is meaningless.
D) Because existence is an attribute of God only.

Key Takeaway
Negative propositions, when analyzed, reveal that existence is logically prior to negation, supporting the necessity of existence in theological arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever denied God's existence while assuming your own existence as unquestionable? How does this lesson's truth—that even negation requires existence—challenge that denial?
?
When you consider the concept of 'God'—is it a mere mental fiction or does it correspond to a reality that demands your response? The passage shows that every conception requires some form of existence. Are you accountable to the reality behind the concept?
?
The lesson reveals that contradiction arises when a thing is denied of itself. In your life, are there areas where you affirm something in practice but deny it in speech or belief? How will you resolve that internal contradiction today?

The Two Cases of the Negation Proposition
Commentator divides the negation proposition into two cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possibilities for the existence of 'blackness' in the proposition 'blackness is not existent' and why the case of identity leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Negating existence from a thing when existence is identical to it is self-negation, which is contradictory.

The commentator begins by analyzing the negative proposition 'blackness is not existent.' He considers two possibilities: either existence is identical to blackness itself or it is something other than blackness. In this lesson, we explore the first case and see why it leads to an immediate logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the first part of the Arabic passage dealing with the two cases. Notice how the commentator sets up the division: existence is either the essence itself or something else.
The key phrase is 'إما نفسه' and 'أو غيره'.
2
Identify the contradiction
Understand why if existence is identical to blackness, then the negation 'blackness is not existent' becomes a negation of blackness from itself, which is a contradiction.
The commentator says: 'لأنه سلب الشيء عن نفسه.'
3
Ask why this matters
Reflect on why this logical analysis is important for theological arguments about existence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وأما النفي) وهو قولنا : السواد ليس بموجود (فلان) وجوده إما نفسه فنفيه عنه ) أي سلب الوجود عن السواد حينئذ تناقض ) لأنه سلب الشيء عن نفسه ( أو غيره ) وهو باطل لوجهين. الأول قوله : ( فيتوقف نفيه عنه على تصوره ) أي يتوقف نفي الوجود عن السواد على تصور السواد المحكوم عليه بذلك النفي (وهو) أي تصور السواد ( يستدعي تميزه وثبوته ) لما عرفت في الوجه الأول من الوجوه الأربعة، فيكون حصول الوجود للسواد شرطاً في نفس الوجود عنه وهو محال (وليس) ثبوت السواد ( في الذهن) حتى يقال : قوله : ( لأنه سلب الشيء عن نفسه) بناء على أن مفهوم قولنا السواد ليس بموجود سلب الوجود عن السواد والوجود نفسه، فيكون سلب الشيء عن نفسه فاندفع ما توهم من أن المراد ينفي وجود السواد عند من يقول : إن وجوده عينه نفي نفس السواد لا إثبات النفي له، فلا يلزم التناقض وإنما كان سلب الشيء عن نفسه تناقضاً، لأن ثبوت الشيء لنفسه دائم، وإطلاق السلب يناقضه فاندفع ما توهم من أنه إنما يلزم التناقض لو اتحد زمان السلب والإيجاب وهو ممنوع.
(As for negation) it is our statement: blackness is not existent. (For) its existence is either itself, so its negation from it — that is, negating existence from blackness — would then be a contradiction, because it is the negation of a thing from itself; (or other than it) and that is false for two reasons. The first is his statement: (its negation from it depends on its conception) meaning the negation of existence from blackness depends on conceiving the blackness that is judged by that negation. (And it) i.e., the conception of blackness (requires its distinction and its existence) as you knew in the first of the four aspects. So obtaining existence for blackness is a condition for the very negation of existence from it, which is impossible. (And it is not) the existence of blackness (in the mind) such that it could be said: His statement: (because it is the negation of a thing from itself) is based on the fact that the concept of our statement 'blackness is not existent' is the negation of existence from blackness, and existence is itself, so it is the negation of a thing from itself. So what was imagined is refuted — that the intended meaning is the negation of the existence of blackness according to those who say that its existence is identical with it, meaning the negation of blackness itself, not the affirmation of negation to it, so contradiction does not follow. Rather, the negation of a thing from itself is a contradiction because the existence of a thing for itself is perpetual, and the assertion of negation contradicts it. So what was imagined is refuted — that contradiction only follows if the time of negation and affirmation were the same, which is denied.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the third possibility in a logical argument about negative judgments, specifically the proposition 'blackness is not existent.' The commentator explains two cases: either existence is identical to blackness or it is other. If identical, negating existence is negating blackness itself, which is a contradiction. If other, the commentator gives the first reason for its falsehood: negating existence from blackness requires conceiving blackness, and conceiving requires its distinction and existence (in the mind). Thus, existence becomes a condition for its own negation, which is impossible. The commentator then addresses a potential misunderstanding: some might think the contradiction only arises from the time of negation and affirmation being the same. He refutes this by stating that the contradiction is inherent, regardless of time, because a thing's existence for itself is perpetual.

الوجود
السواد
التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what happens if existence is identical to blackness?
A) Negation becomes a contradiction.
B) Negation is possible.
C) Existence is other.
D) No contradiction.

Key Takeaway
When existence is identical to an essence, negating existence is self-negation, which is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a fundamental truth about your own nature—something that defines who you are as a believer. When have you denied that truth in word or action, and how did that denial create a contradiction in your life?
?
Is there an aspect of your faith that you treat as optional, even though it is as essential to your identity as existence is to blackness? What would it mean to truly accept that it cannot be negated without losing yourself?

The First Reason for Falsity: Conception Requires Existence
Commentator gives the first reason for falsity and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the negation 'blackness is not existent' is false when existence is other than blackness, based on the requirement that conceiving blackness presupposes its existence.

Core Idea
To negate existence from a thing, you must first conceive it, but conception requires its existence — so existence becomes a condition for its own negation.

Having introduced the two possibilities, the commentator now gives the first reason why the negation is false when existence is other than blackness. He argues that negating existence from blackness depends on conceiving blackness, and conceiving blackness requires its distinction and existence (in the mind). Thus, existence is a prerequisite for the very act of negating it, which is impossible. He then refutes a potential objection about temporal coincidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first reason and the refutation. Notice the logical flow: negation depends on conception → conception requires existence → contradiction.
Key phrase: 'فيتوقف نفيه عنه على تصوره' and 'يستدعي تمييزه وثبوته'.
2
Understand the argument
Trace the argument: To negate existence from blackness, you must have an idea of blackness. Having an idea means blackness is distinguished and exists (in the mind). So existence is already present before you negate it. Therefore, negating existence is impossible because it would require existence to be both present and absent simultaneously.
3
Examine the refutation
The commentator addresses a potential misunderstanding: some might think the contradiction only follows if negation and affirmation occur at the same time. He argues that the contradiction is inherent because a thing's existence for itself is perpetual, so any negation contradicts it regardless of time.
The phrase 'لأن ثبوت الشيء لنفسه دائم' is key.
4
Summarize
Summarize the lesson in your own words: The first reason shows that negating existence leads to a vicious circle, and the time objection is invalid.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(As for negation) it is our statement: blackness is not existent. So what was imagined is refuted — that the intended meaning is the negation of the existence of blackness according to those who say that its existence is identical with it, meaning the negation of blackness itself, not the affirmation of negation to it, so contradiction does not follow. Rather, the negation of a thing from itself is a contradiction because the existence of a thing for itself is perpetual, and the assertion of negation contradicts it. Al-Abhari's Objection and the Role of Mental Existence
Objection and response regarding time uniformity and mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves apparent contradictions when negating existence from things that have mental existence.

Core Idea
Mental existence conditions the judgment of negation, not the negation itself.

In this dense passage, the commentator tackles a subtle objection from al-Abhari about the conditions for contradiction, then clarifies a crucial point: how something with mental existence can still be described as 'non-existent' in an absolute sense. Understanding this resolution is key to grasping how kalam handles negation and existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the flow. Notice the objection and the two responses.
The passage shifts from one argument to another mid-way.
2
Identify the objection and its dismissal
Find al-Abhari's objection that contradiction only follows if time of affirmation and negation are the same. See why the commentator dismisses it as weak.
The dismissal is brief – the real answer comes next.
3
Understand the proper answer
The commentator gives a better answer: the negation is of existence as identical to essence, not of a separate attribute. This avoids contradiction.
This relies on the view that existence and essence are identical.
4
Analyze the new problem: mental existence
The text raises a question: if blackness exists in the mind, how can we say it is absolutely non-existent? The commentator explains that mental existence is a condition for the judgment of negation, not a condition for the negation itself.
The negation is of absolute existence, which includes both external and mental. The judgment is that blackness is not absolutely existent, but rather exists mentally.
5
See the final resolution
No contradiction arises because we do not judge blackness as absolutely non-existent; we leave it undetermined between non-existence and mental existence.
This preserves consistency in the discussion about absolute negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فنفيه عنه تناقض قال الأبهري: لقائل أن يقول : إنما يلزم التناقض أن لو اتحد زمان الإيجاب والسلب وهو ممنوع وضعفه غير خفي للفطن، نعم يمكن أن يجاب بأن المراد ينفي وجود السواد عند من يقول : بأن وجوده عينه نفي نفس السواد لا إثبات النفي له فلا يلزم التناقض . هذا الثبوت شرط لنفي الثبوت الخارجي عنه، ولا محذور فيه لما مر من أن الكلام في النفي المطلق المقابل للثبوت الذي هو أعم من الخارجي، والذهني فلو كان السواد ثابتاً في الذهن لم يصح نفي الثبوت عنه مطلقاً، وجوابه أن ثبوت السواد في الذهن شرط للحكم بانتفاء الثبوت المطلق عنه لا لانتفائه عنه، ولم نحكم على السواد الثابت في الذهن أنه معدوم مطلقاً، بل رددناه بينه وبين الموجود في الجملة فلا محذور أصلا.
His statement: (So its negation from it is a contradiction). Al-Abhari said: One may say that contradiction only follows if the time of affirmation and negation were the same, which is denied, but its weakness is not hidden to the intelligent. Yes, it can be answered that the intention is the negation of the existence of blackness according to those who say that its existence is identical with it — the negation of blackness itself, not the affirmation of negation to it — so contradiction does not follow. This existence is a condition for the negation of external existence from it, and there is no problem in that, because as has passed, the discussion is about absolute negation which is opposed to absolute existence, which is more general than external and mental. So if blackness were existent in the mind, the negation of existence from it absolutely would not be valid. The answer is that the existence of blackness in the mind is a condition for the judgment of the negation of absolute existence from it, not for the negation itself. And we do not judge blackness that is existent in the mind as absolutely non-existent; rather, we consider it between that and being existent in some sense, so there is no problem at all.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection attributed to al-Abhari: someone might claim that the contradiction in 'blackness is not existent' only follows if the time of the affirmation and negation are the same, and that this is not necessarily the case. The commentator dismisses this as obviously weak. Instead, he provides a proper answer: the intended negation is not of existence as a separate attribute, but of the thing itself, for those who hold that existence is identical to essence. Then he shifts to a new issue: the role of mental existence. He states that the existence of blackness in the mind is a condition for negating external existence from it, not a problem, because the negation in question is absolute (shamil) negation, which includes both external and mental. If blackness existed in the mind, one could not simply deny its existence absolutely. The solution is that the mental existence of blackness is a condition for the judgment that absolute existence is negated from it, but not for the negation itself. Moreover, the judgment is not that blackness is absolutely non-existent, but rather that it is undetermined between being absolutely non-existent and being existent in some sense (i.e., mental existence). Thus no contradiction arises.

Key Terms
النفي المطلق
الثبوت الخارجي
الثبوت الذهني
الوجود
التناقض
الذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something exists in the mind, it cannot be said to be non-existent at all, making absolute negation impossible.
Correction: Mental existence does not prevent absolute negation; it only conditions the judgment of negation. The negation is of absolute existence, and the judgment is that the thing is not absolutely existent, leaving room for mental existence.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the apparent problem that if blackness exists in the mind, negating its existence absolutely would be false?
A) He argues that mental existence is not real existence.
B) He says that the negation is only of external existence, not absolute existence.
C) He states that the mental existence is a condition for the judgment of negation, not for the negation itself, and the judgment is not absolute non-existence.
D) He claims that blackness cannot exist in the mind.

Key Takeaway
Mental existence conditions the judgment of absolute negation; the negation itself remains valid because we do not judge the thing as absolutely non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you deny something in your heart – a doubt, a fear, a desire – do you ever confuse the mental image of that thing with its external reality? How might this lesson on mental existence as a condition for judgment help you see the difference between denying a thought and denying a fact, and how does this precision affect your accountability before Allah?
?
This lesson shows that a condition for judgment is not the same as the judgment itself. ?
The passage warns against assuming that differing times remove the obligation of consistency. When you set standards for yourself today that you did not hold yesterday, do you deceive yourself that contradiction is avoided simply because circumstances changed? What truth about your own soul is this lesson forcing you to confront right now – and what concrete step will you take to align your judgments with what is truly absolute?

Answering the Objection on Mental Existence for Absolute Negation
The commentator resolves an objection about mental existence as a condition for absolute negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the condition of mental existence applies only to the judgment of absolute negation, not to the negation itself, and how the dichotomy of the thing between existence and non-existence avoids contradiction.

Core Idea
The condition applies to the judgment, not to the negation itself.

This passage addresses a subtle objection about absolute negation: if blackness exists in the mind, how can we negate its existence absolutely? The commentator clarifies that mental existence is a condition only for the judgment of absolute negation, not for the negation itself. Moreover, we do not actually judge the mental blackness as absolutely non-existent; we dichotomize it. This lesson unpacks this key distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the objection and the beginning of the answer.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the objection states that if blackness exists in the mind, absolute negation is invalid. Understand why this seems problematic.
The objection assumes that negating existence absolutely requires the thing to have no existence at all, even mental.
3
Grasp the answer's key distinction
The answer distinguishes between the condition for the judgment of absolute negation and the condition for the negation itself. The mental existence is a condition for the judgment, not for the negation.
This is the core insight: the condition is epistemological, not ontological.
4
Understand the dichotomy solution
The commentator explains that they do not judge the mental blackness as absolutely non-existent. Instead, they dichotomize between absolute non-existence and existence in some respect.
The dichotomy avoids contradiction by not placing the mental thing under absolute negation.
5
Address the potential misunderstanding
Consider the final part about pronouns. Some might think that 'its conception' refers to the negation, which would imply the non-existent is conceived and thus exists mentally. The commentator notes this has been refuted.
This shows the importance of precise reference of pronouns in logical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الثبوت شرط لنفي الثبوت الخارجي عنه، ولا محذور فيه لما مر من أن الكلام في النفي المطلق المقابل للثبوت الذي هو أعم من الخارجي، والذهني فلو كان السواد ثابتاً في الذهن لم يصح نفي الثبوت عنه مطلقاً، وجوابه أن ثبوت السواد في الذهن شرط للحكم بانتفاء الثبوت المطلق عنه لا لانتفائه عنه، ولم نحكم على السواد الثابت في الذهن أنه معدوم مطلقاً، بل رددناه بينه وبين الموجود في الجملة فلا محذور أصلا، وقد يتوهم أن الضمائر في تصوره وتميزه وثبوته راجعة إلى نفي الوجود عن السواد، وتصور هذا النفي هو تصور المعدوم فيلزم تميزه وثبوته وقد تبين بطلانه.
This existence (in the mind) is a condition for negating external existence from it, and there is no problem in it because, as previously mentioned, the discussion concerns absolute negation corresponding to existence which is more general than external and mental existence. So if blackness were established in the mind, negating existence from it absolutely would not be valid. The answer is that the existence of blackness in the mind is a condition for the judgment of the negation of absolute existence from it, not for the negation itself. And we do not judge blackness established in the mind to be absolutely non-existent; rather we dichotomize it between that and something that exists in some respect, so there is no problem at all. And it might be wrongly imagined that the pronouns in 'its conception', 'its distinction', and 'its existence' refer to the negation of existence from blackness, and the conception of this negation is the conception of the non-existent, necessitating its distinction and existence, and its falsity has been shown.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the logical conditions for absolute negation. The objection states that if blackness (sawād) is established in the mind, then it cannot be said to be absolutely non-existent; but the author clarifies that the condition applies only to the judgment of absolute non-existence, not to the non-existence itself. Furthermore, they do not actually pronounce a judgment of absolute non-existence; instead, they dichotomize the thing between absolute non-existence and existence in some respect, avoiding contradiction. Then a possible misunderstanding is addressed: one might think that pronouns in phrases like 'its conception' refer back to the negation of existence, which would require that the non-existent (ma'dūm) itself be conceived and thus have mental existence—but this has already been refuted. The commentator explains that this is indeed a misunderstanding because the negation here is a judgment, not what is being conceived.

Key Terms
نفي مطلق (absolute negation)
ثبوت (existence)
سواد (blackness)
معدوم مطلق (absolutely non-existent)
ترديد (dichotomization)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if blackness exists in the mind, then it cannot be subject to absolute negation because absolute negation requires the thing to have no existence at all (including mental).
Correction: The existence in the mind is only a condition for the judgment of absolute negation, not for the negation itself. Moreover, the judgment is not actually made—the thing is dichotomized instead.

Checkpoint

B) Because absolute negation does not apply to mental existence.
C) Because blackness cannot exist in the mind.
D) Because the condition is only for external existence.

Key Takeaway
The condition of mental existence for absolute negation applies only to the judgment, not to the negation itself, and the dichotomy between absolute non-existence and existence in some respect avoids any logical problem.

Refuting the Second Interpretation of the Disjunction
The commentator refutes the second interpretation of the disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the interpretation of existence/non-existence as a disjunction between a thing's existence for another and its negation is invalid.

Core Idea
A dichotomization can be invalid if its affirmative part is logically impossible.

This passage presents two compelling reasons that dismantle the second interpretation of the dichotomization between existence and non-existence. The first reason exposes a logical impossibility in the affirmative side, while the second clarifies the correct relationship between subject and predicate. By examining these arguments, you will see why this interpretation cannot be sustained.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: it first states the interpretation, then declares it invalid, and then gives two reasons marked by 'لأن'.
2
Analyze the first reason
The first reason claims that the affirmative part (e.g., 'the body is black') is a judgment of unity of two things, which is inconceivable. Think about why that would be problematic.
3
Analyze the second reason
The second reason argues that when the predicate is different from the subject, the correct meaning is that the subject is described by the predicate, not that they are unified. How does this undermine the dichotomization?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني وهو أن يكون الترديد في قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون بين ثبوت الشيء لغيره وسلبه عنه كما في قولنا الجسم إما أسود أو لا (باطل) أيضاً (لأن الجزء الثبوتي منه لا يعقل على وجه يكون معناه صحيحاً لأنه حكم بوحدة الاثنين وذلك مما لا يتصور صحته قطعاً، ولأن المحمول إذا كان مغايراً للموضوع كما فيما نحن بصدده وجب أن يكون المعنى أن الموضوع موصوف بالمحمول، فقد اعتبر.
And the second (interpretation) is that the dichotomization in our statement 'a thing either exists or does not exist' is between a thing's existence for another and its negation from it, as in our statement 'the body is either black or not'—this is also invalid, because the affirmative part of it is not conceivable in a way that its meaning is correct, since it is a judgment of the unity of two things, and that is absolutely inconceivable in its correctness. And because if the predicate is different from the subject, as in the case before us, the meaning must be that the subject is described by the predicate, so it is considered.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage refutes the second possible interpretation of the dichotomization between existence and non-existence. The interpretation is that the dichotomization is between a thing's existence for another (e.g., blackness being in a body) and its negation. The commentator argues this is invalid for two reasons. First, the affirmative part 'the body is black' involves a judgment of the unity of two distinct things (the subject and predicate being one in existence), which is inconceivable. Second, when the predicate is genuinely different from the subject, the correct meaning is that the subject is described by the predicate (i.e., the body has the attribute of blackness), but this does not constitute a true unity. Thus, the dichotomization as explained is not sound.

Key Terms
ترديد
ثبوت الشيء لغيره
حكم بوحدة الاثنين
محمول
موصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any dichotomization like 'either black or not' is always logically sound.
Correction: The passage shows that if the affirmative part involves a judgment of unity of distinct things, the dichotomization is invalid.

Checkpoint
Why is the second interpretation of the disjunction invalid according to the passage?
A) Because it assumes the subject and predicate are identical.
B) Because the affirmative part judges two distinct things as one, which is impossible.
C) Because the predicate is not different from the subject.
D) Because the dichotomization is between existence and non-existence, not between attributes.

Key Takeaway
The second interpretation of the disjunction is invalid because its affirmative part requires impossible unity and misrepresents the subject-predicate relationship.

What step will you take today to break free from such a dichotomy?

What concrete change will you make in how you judge yourself or others to avoid this logical and spiritual error?

The Muhassal Argument: Disjunction Between Existence and Non-Existence Is Meaningless
Argument from al-Muhassal that the disjunction of existence and non-existence has no meaningful sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Muhassal argues that the disjunction between existence and non-existence has no meaningful sense, and why it cannot be considered a necessary truth.

Core Idea
If existence is inseparable from quiddity, then saying 'it exists or it doesn't' makes no sense.

This passage from al-Muhassal tackles a foundational assumption in kalam: that every quiddity can be meaningfully said to either exist or not exist. Al-Muhassal argues that if existence is essential to a quiddity, then the disjunction collapses into meaninglessness. Let's see how he builds this case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the parenthetical clarification in the text.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the sentence where al-Muhassal states that 'blackness exists' and 'blackness does not exist' have no meaningful sense. That is the conclusion.
3
Understand the argument structure
Trace the reasoning: denying existence from quiddity implies emptiness (objection refuted elsewhere), then a counter-response, and finally the decisive argument from al-Muhassal.
The disjunction is meaningless because one side is inseparable, not just false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضاً فإنه (أي نفي الوجود عن السواد وسلبه عن ماهيته) يقتضي خلو الماهية عن الوجود وسنبطله في مسألة أن المعدوم ليس بشيء، إذ يستدل هناك على امتناع خلو الماهية عن الوجود، فيستحيل الحكم عليها بالعدم، وقد يجاب بأن عدم خلوها عن الوجود لا ينافي الترديد بينه وبين العدم. قال في المحصل: فقد ظهر أنه ليس لقولنا: السواد موجود والسواد معدوم معنى محصل، فلا يكون أيضاً للترديد بينهما مفهوم محصل، فامتنع التصديق به فضلاً عن أن يكون ذلك التصديق بديهياً.
Also, it (i.e., the negation of existence from blackness and its withdrawal from its quiddity) necessitates the emptiness of the quiddity from existence, and we will invalidate it in the question that the nonexistent is not a thing, for it is argued there against the impossibility of the quiddity being empty of existence, so judgment upon it by non-existence is impossible. And it may be answered that the non-emptiness of it from existence does not contradict the disjunction between it and non-existence. He said in al-Muhassal: It has become clear that our statement 'blackness exists' and 'blackness does not exist' has no meaningful sense; thus the disjunction between them also has no meaningful concept, so belief in it is impossible, let alone that belief being intuitive.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument from al-Muhassal (the summary) concerning the impossibility of a meaningful disjunction between existence and non-existence for a quiddity. The context is a refutation of the claim that the disjunction 'either it is or it is not' is a necessary truth. The author states that denying existence from blackness and negating it from its quiddity would imply that the quiddity is empty of existence, but this is impossible because the quiddity cannot be devoid of existence (as will be proven elsewhere). A potential response is that the non-emptiness of the quiddity does not prevent the disjunction between existence and non-existence — that is, even if the quiddity always has existence, we can still say 'either it exists or it does not', and one side is false. However, al-Muhassal counters that the very statement 'blackness exists' or 'blackness does not exist' has no meaningful sense (because existence is inseparable from the quiddity), so the disjunction between them also lacks a meaningful concept. Therefore, belief in such a disjunction is impossible, let alone it being an intuitive or necessary truth. The passage thus undermines the necessity of the disjunction that is often assumed in kalam arguments.

Key Terms
الوجود
العدم
الماهية
الترديد
المحصل
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disjunction between existence and non-existence is always a necessary truth for any quiddity.
Correction: Al-Muhassal argues that when existence is essential to a quiddity (like blackness), the disjunction is meaningless because the negative side cannot even be coherently stated. Thus, it is not a necessary truth in such cases.

Checkpoint
What does al-Muhassal conclude about the statement 'blackness exists' and 'blackness does not exist'?
A) One of them is true and the other false.
B) They are both true in different respects.
C) Neither has a meaningful sense.
D) They are both false.

Key Takeaway
Al-Muhassal argues that if existence is inseparable from a quiddity, then the disjunction between existence and non-existence becomes meaningless and cannot be believed as a necessary truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have said to yourself countless times: 'Either this exists or it does not.' But al-Muhassal argues that for some realities, that very disjunction is meaningless. When you consider the existence of the Creator, do you assume your logical disjunctions are always valid? What if your own categories of thought fail to capture the reality? How does this lesson force you to question the tools you use to think about God?
?
The passage shows that even a seemingly basic logical principle—the law of excluded middle—may not apply to necessary existence. Are you willing to let your theological conclusions be shaped by the nature of the subject rather than by your ingrained habits of reasoning? What specific assumption about 'self-evident' truths do you need to re-examine today?
?
Al-Muhassal says that belief in the disjunction is 'impossible, let alone it being intuitive'. Is there a theological claim you have held as obviously true without realizing it might be built on meaningless terms? Take a moment to identify one such belief and ask yourself: Is it rooted in the quiddity itself or in my own mental habits?

Refutation of the Second Interpretation: The Positive Part Is Unintelligible
Refutation of the second interpretation of the disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the positive part of the disjunction between existence for another and its negation is unintelligible, and how the impossibility of a 'judgment of unity of two' and the correct understanding of attribution invalidate this interpretation.

Core Idea
The positive part requires two things to be one, which is impossible.

After refuting the first interpretation, the author turns to a second possible reading: that the disjunction is between a thing's existence for another and its negation. In this lesson, we will see why this interpretation fails. The argument rests on two key observations: first, the positive statement would require a judgment that two distinct things are actually one, and second, the attribution between subject and predicate cannot be coherently understood as either existent or nonexistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the author's style of refutation.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two reasons
The author gives two reasons why the interpretation is invalid. Find the phrases that introduce each reason.
Look for 'la'an' (because) and 'wa la'an' (and because).
3
Understand the first reason
Why is the positive part a 'judgment of unity of two'? What does that mean? The author argues that affirming 'the body is black' treats the body and blackness as one, which is impossible.
This reason is based on the impossibility of identifying distinct entities.
4
Understand the second reason
The second reason involves the nature of attribution. If the predicate differs from the subject, the meaning is that the subject is qualified by the predicate. But this attribution cannot be correctly considered because it would have to be either existent or nonexistent, leading to absurdity.
The author uses the concept of 'attribution' (al-mawṣūfiyya) and its opposite.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how these two reasons together show that the second interpretation is invalid. The positive part is unintelligible because it either involves an impossible identity or an incoherent attribution.
This refutation strengthens the argument that the original disjunction must be about existence in itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والثاني) وهو أن يكون الترديد في قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون بين ثبوت الشيء لغيره وسلبه عنه كما في قولنا الجسم إما أسود أو لا (باطل) أيضاً (لأن الجزء الثبوتي منه لا يعقل على وجه يكون معناه صحيحاً لأنه حكم بوحدة الاثنين وذلك مما لا يتصور صحته قطعاً، ولأن المحمول إذا كان مغايراً للموضوع كما فيما نحن بصدده وجب أن يكون المعنى أن الموضوع موصوف بالمحمول، فقد اعتبر بينهما موصوفية، ولا يمكن اعتبارها على وجه يصح) لأن الموصوفية ليست عدمية لأنه نقيض اللاموصوفية، وهي اللاموصوفية عدمية لصدقها على المعدوم.
(The second) is that the disjunction in our statement 'a thing is either it is or it is not' is between the affirmation of a thing for another and its negation from it, as in our statement 'a body is either black or not' — (invalid) also (because the positive part thereof is not intelligible in a way that its meaning is correct, since it is a judgment of unity of the two, and that is such that its correctness is absolutely inconceivable; and because the predicate, if it is different from the subject as in the case before us, it must be that the meaning is that the subject is qualified by the predicate, so an attribution is considered between them, and it cannot be considered in a way that is correct) because the attribution is not nonexistent, since it is the opposite of the lack of attribution, and the lack of attribution is nonexistent because it applies to the nonexistent.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage refutes a second possible interpretation of the disjunction. The interpretation is that the disjunction 'either it is or it is not' refers not to existence in itself but to the existence of one thing for another (e.g., blackness existing in a body) and its negation. The author declares this interpretation invalid for two reasons. First, the positive part (the affirmation that the body is black) is not intelligible as a correct meaning, because it would require a judgment that two things (the body and blackness) are one — which is impossible. Second, when the predicate (blackness) is different from the subject (body), the only possible meaning is that the subject is described by the predicate, i.e., there is an attribution between them. However, this attribution cannot be correctly considered because if it were a real relation, it would have to be either existent or nonexistent. If it were nonexistent, then the negation would be the same as the affirmation (both nonexistent), which is absurd. But it cannot be existent either, because the lack of attribution (the opposite) is clearly nonexistent (since it applies to nonexistent things), and the opposite of a nonexistent must be existent? Actually the text says: the attribution is not nonexistent because it is the opposite of lack of attribution, and lack of attribution is nonexistent. The implication is that the attribution must be existent, but then how can it be a relation between a subject and a predicate that are distinct? The argument is dense and relies on an infinite regress discussed in the commentary. The key point is that the positive part of the disjunction is not a coherent proposition.

Key Terms
الجزء الثبوتي
الترديد
الموصوفية
اللاموصوفية
حكم بوحدة الاثنين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disjunction 'a thing is either it is or it is not' can be interpreted as 'the thing exists for another or it does not exist for another'.
Correction: This interpretation is invalid because the positive part (existence for another) is not intelligible as a correct meaning, as it would require a judgment that two things are one, and the attribution between subject and predicate cannot be coherently understood.

Checkpoint
Why is the positive part of the second interpretation considered unintelligible?
A) It involves a judgment that two things are one, which is impossible.
B) It is impossible for a body to be black.
C) The predicate is always identical to the subject.
D) The attribution between subject and predicate is always nonexistent.

Key Takeaway
The second interpretation fails because its positive part is unintelligible, as it would require a judgment of unity of two distinct things and a coherent understanding of attribution is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say something like 'this action is good' or 'this belief is true,' do you ever stop to consider that you are implicitly judging two distinct things (the action and goodness, the belief and truth) as one? How does this passage expose the hidden complexity in your everyday affirmations? Will you now pause before making such judgments?
?
The author argues that any claim of a property (e.g., 'the body is black') is philosophically problematic, leading to an infinite regress. How does this affect your understanding of Allah's attributes? Are you willing to accept that your own human way of predicating attributes may be fundamentally flawed, and that only divine revelation can guide you correctly? What will you do with this humility?
?
This lesson reveals that even a simple logical statement can be unintelligible if not properly understood. In your own life, are there beliefs or opinions you hold that, upon deeper scrutiny, might contain inherent contradictions (like trying to unite two distinct realities as one)? Can you identify one such belief today and commit to re-examining it using the rigorous approach demonstrated here?

Why the Author Avoided the Infinite Regress Argument
Explanation of why the author did not use infinite regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author chose not to invoke the infinite regress of attributions in this particular context, despite using it earlier.

Core Idea
The infinite regress argument was avoided because it relies on controversial assumptions about relational entities.

In the previous discussion, an infinite regress of attributions was used. Here, however, the author refrains from that argument. Why? The commentator explains two reasons: the argument depends on the permissibility of regress in relational matters, and it would multiply problematic aspects. This lesson unpacks those reasons and their implications for the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the commentator's explanation for the author's choice.
The passage is concise.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find the two reasons given: (1) the argument is based on allowing regress in relational matters, (2) it would multiply problematic aspects.
3
Compare with the earlier argument
Recall that earlier a regress was used. Consider why the same reasoning does not apply here.
This highlights the context-sensitivity of theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لأن الموصوفية إلخ) لم يقل هاهنا أن الموصوفية لكونها مغايرة للموضوع تحتاج إلى موصوفية أخرى، باعتبارها تحمل، وهكذا، فلزم التسلسل كما ذكره سابقاً لأن هذا الوجه مبني على جواز التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية حيث خص لزوم التسلسل على تقدير كون الموصوفية وجودية، ولأن فيه تكثير الوجوه القادحة. قوله: (أي اللاموصوفية أي مفهومها فيكفي في ذلك صدقها على المعدوم إذ لو كانت وجودية امتنع اتصاف المعدوم بها فما قيل من أن المراد بعدمية اللاموصوفية عدمية جميع أفرادها، وهي إنما تثبت لو ثبت صدقها دائماً على المعدوم، وهم محض وكذا، ما قيل: عدمية صورة النفي موقوفة على وجودية مدخل حرف النفي، فالاستدلال بعدميتها على وجودية المدخول دور والجواب أن موقوفية عدمية صورة النفي على وجودية المدخول لا ينافي كون العلم بوجودية المدخول مستفاداً من العلم بعدميتها بوجه آخر، كما فيما نحن فيه وهكذا الحال في كل معلوم بالقياس إلى علته. قوله: (فقد اعتبر بينهما موصوفية إلخ) قال الأبهري: لقائل أن يقول: لا نسلم أن الحمل هاهنا يقتضي الموصوفية، وإلا انتقض بقولنا الحيوان جسم، والإنسان حيوان، إلى ما لا يحصى، والجواب أن ما ذكره نقض إجمالي لا يشفى لأن المعلل يمنع صحة صورة النقض كما لا يخفى.
His statement: (Because the attribution etc.) He did not say here that the attribution, because it is different from the subject, needs another attribution, by virtue of it being predicated, and so on, so an infinite regress would follow as he mentioned previously, because this aspect is based on the permissibility of infinite regress in relational matters, since he specified the necessity of infinite regress on the assumption that the attribution is existent, and because it would multiply the problematic aspects. His statement: (i.e., the lack of attribution, i.e., its concept; it suffices for that its applying to the nonexistent, because if it were existent, it would be impossible for the nonexistent to be described by it. So what is said that the intended meaning of the non-existence of the lack of attribution is that all its individuals are nonexistent, which would only be established if it were proven that it always applies to the nonexistent, is pure illusion. Likewise, what is said: the non-existence of the form of negation depends on the existence of the input of the negation particle, so inferring from its non-existence the existence of the input is circular. And the answer is that the dependence of the non-existence of the form of negation on the existence of the input does not contradict the fact that knowledge of the existence of the input is derived from knowledge of its non-existence in another way, as in the case at hand, and so is the case for every known thing relative to its cause. His statement: (So an attribution is considered between them etc.) Al-Abhari said: An objector may say: We do not accept that predication here necessitates attribution; otherwise it would be contradicted by our statement 'animal is body', 'human is animal', and so on ad infinitum. And the answer is that what he mentioned is a summary objection that does not suffice, because the one giving the reason denies the validity of the form of the counterexample, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage deals with objections and refinements concerning the necessity of attribution in predication. The commentator clarifies why the author did not raise the infinite regress argument here: because it is based on allowing regress in relational matters, and specifying it on the assumption that attribution is existent would multiply problematic aspects. Then he addresses a counterargument about the lack of attribution: some argued that inferring the existence of the input (the subject of negation) from the non-existence of the form of negation is circular. The response is that the dependence of the negation form's non-existence on the existence of the input does not mean that knowledge of the input's existence cannot be derived from the negation form's non-existence in another way; this is common in causal reasoning. Then al-Abhari's objection is presented: an objector claims that predication does not always require a distinct attribution, citing examples like 'animal is body' and 'human is animal', where the predicate is not a distinct accident but a genus or differentia. The author answers that this objection is a summary one that does not refute the point, because the one asserting the necessity of attribution denies the validity of the counterexample — presumably because in those cases the predication is essential (predication of identity or genus) and does not involve a distinct predicate that would require attribution. The passage thus defends the original argument that when the predicate is a distinct notion (like blackness), a separate attribution is necessary, and this leads to an infinite regress if that attribution is considered a real relation.

Key Terms
الموصوفية
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the author contradicts himself by not using the same regress argument everywhere.
Correction: The author's choice is strategic: the regress argument has specific assumptions that are not met here, so he avoids it to prevent multiplying difficulties.

Checkpoint
Why did the author avoid using the infinite regress argument in this passage?
A) Because the regress is impossible in relational matters.
B) Because the argument relies on assuming regress is permissible and would multiply problems.
C) Because the author forgot to use it.
D) Because the commentator disagreed.

Key Takeaway
The author strategically avoids the infinite regress argument here to avoid relying on controversial assumptions and to keep the discussion focused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you insisted on using an argument that seemed strong but actually relied on assumptions you weren't sure about? Are you willing to set aside such arguments to keep your faith simple and focused on what is certain?
?
The author avoided multiplying problematic aspects. In your own life, do you sometimes multiply problems by insisting on arguments or positions that create more difficulties than they resolve? What would it look like to choose the simpler, more certain path?
?
The infinite regress argument is not used here because it assumes something controversial about relational matters. Is there a belief you hold that depends on an assumption you haven't examined? Will you examine it today?

The Role of 'Lack of Attribution' in Response to Circularity
Defense of using 'lack of attribution' against circularity objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of 'lack of attribution' (al-lamawsufiyya) is used to counter objections about circularity in the argument.

Core Idea
Lack of attribution suffices because it applies even to the nonexistent, avoiding circularity.

An objector argued that inferring the existence of a subject from the non-existence of the negation form is circular. The commentator responds by explaining that the dependence of the negation's non-existence on the existence of its input does not prevent deriving knowledge of the input from the negation's non-existence in another way. This lesson unpacks this subtle logical point and its application to the concept of 'lack of attribution'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation about al-lamawsufiyya and the objection about circularity.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the objection: inferring the existence of the input (the subject of negation) from the non-existence of the negation form is a circular argument.
The objection relies on the dependency between the negation and its input.
3
Follow the response
The response says that the dependency does not invalidate deriving knowledge in a different direction, similar to how a cause is known from its effect.
4
Connect to the main argument
See how this defense supports using 'lack of attribution' as a valid concept that applies to the nonexistent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that deriving existence from non-existence is always circular.
Correction: It is circular only if the dependency is the same in both directions; but here the knowledge is derived via a different relation, so it is valid.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator respond to the objection that inferring existence from non-existence is circular?
A) He denies that the non-existence of the negation form depends on the input.
B) He argues that the dependency is irrelevant because circularity is allowed.
C) He says that the dependence does not prevent deriving knowledge in another way, like cause from effect.
D) He concedes the circularity and changes the argument.

Key Takeaway
The concept of 'lack of attribution' is defended against circularity by appealing to the distinction between ontological dependence and epistemic inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the universe, do you ever feel that its dependence on God makes it impossible to use it as evidence for God, as if that would be circular? But this passage shows that dependency does not block inference. How might this affect your daily awareness of God's existence and your use of creation as a sign?
?
Have you ever doubted something about your faith because you thought a proof was circular (e.g., using the Quran to prove the Quran's authenticity)? How does this lesson's distinction between ontological dependence and epistemic inference change your approach to such doubts?
?
The passage teaches that we can know a cause from its effect even though the effect depends on the cause. Is there a belief about Allah's attributes that you have hesitated to accept due to an imagined circularity? Will you now re-examine that belief with this principle in mind, and take a step to strengthen your conviction?

Al-Abhari's Objection and the Author's Reply
Al-Abhari's objection and the author's summary rejection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Abhari's objection that predication does not always require a distinct attribution, and why the author rejects this objection.

Core Idea
Al-Abhari's counterexample of 'animal is body' does not refute the need for attribution in cases where the predicate is a distinct accident.

Al-Abhari raises an objection: if predication always required a distinct attribution, then serial predications like 'animal is body', 'human is animal' would also require it, leading to an infinite chain. The author replies that this is a summary objection without force, because the defender of attribution denies that these examples involve a distinct predicate needing attribution. This lesson analyzes the exchange and its implications for the theory of predication.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Abhari's objection
Read the passage where al-Abhari presents his objection using examples like 'animal is body' and 'human is animal'.
2
Understand the author's reply
The author says the objection is a 'summary objection' that does not suffice, because the defender denies the validity of the counterexample.
This is a defensive move—the defender says the examples are not cases where the predicate is a distinct accident.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Consider why 'animal is body' might not require a distinct attribution: because it is an essential predication (genus-species) where the predicate is not an accidental concept.
