The Third Doubt: Influence of Temperaments and Habits
Al-Iji presents the third doubt of deniers of self-evident truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third doubt raised by deniers of self-evident truths, which argues that temperaments and habits influence beliefs and potentially undermine the certainty of self-evident judgments.

Core Idea
Temperaments and habits can make false beliefs seem certain.

Al-Iji presents a powerful challenge: if our temperaments and habits can make us certain of falsehoods, how can we trust our self-evident beliefs? This lesson unpacks the denier's claim and its examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the denier presents the claim that temperaments and habits influence beliefs.
The denier uses examples: a strong heart may not find cruelty bad, and people become certain of false doctrines through habituation.
2
Identify the conclusion
Find the conclusion of the doubt: that the certainty of self-evident truths might not be certain, just like propositions arising from specific temperaments and habits.
The doubt ends with a direct challenge to the universal agreement on self-evident truths.
3
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this doubt is considered a serious objection to the reliability of self-evident truths. Think about how it could be answered.
The next lesson will present Al-Iji's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة ( الثالثة ) لمنكري البديهيات فقط أن يقال: (للأمزجة والعادات تأثير في الاعتقادات فقوى القلب) بحسب المزاج ( يستحسن الإيلام ولا يستقبحه بل ربما يلتذ به (وضعيف القلب يستقبحه جداً، ولذلك ترى بعضهم لا يجوزون ذبح الحيوانات للانتفاع بأكلها ( ومن مارس مذهباً من المذاهب ) حقاً كان أو باطلاً، واعتاد به ( برهة من الزمان ونشأ عليه فإنه ) بمجرد اعتياده به من غير أن يلوح له ما يظهر به حقيته ( يجزم بصحته )، وإن كان باطلاً ) وبطلان ما يخالفه وإن كان حقاً ( فجاز أن يكون الجزم) من بديهة العقل ( في الكل أي كل ما حكمت به لمزاج أو عادة عامين) لجميع أفراد الإنسان المتفقين في البديهيات، فلا تكون يقينية كالقضايا الصادرة من الأمزجة والعادات المخصوصة ) لا يقال : نحن نفرض أنفسنا خالية عن جميع الأمزجة والعادات ومع ذلك نجد من أنفسنا الجزم بهذه (الأمور البديهية، فالحاكم فيها صريح العقل بلا تأثير من مزاج أو عادة (الأنا) نقول : لا نسلم إمكان فرض الخلو) عن جميع الأمزجة والعادات ( إذ قد لا نشعر ببعض من الهيئات المزاجية أو العادية، فكيف نفرض الخلو عن ذلك البعض مع عدم الشعور به؟ (ولئن سلم) إمكان فرض الخلو عن الجميع ( فلا يلزم من فرض الخلو الخلو في نفس الأمر ألا یری أن البخيل لا يزول عنه بخله بمجرد فرض خلوه عنه ( ولعل عادة مستمرة صارت ملكة مستقرة لا تزول بتهذيب النفس ) عنها ( مدة العمر فضلا عن مجرد فرض ( زوالها والخلو عنها ) والجواب أنه أي ما ذكرتم من تأثير الأمزجة والعادات في الاعتقاديات وحصول القضايا ( لا يدل على جواز كون الكل أي جميع القضايا البديهية ذلك في الجزم بسبب قوله : (لا) نسلم إمكان فرض الخلو إلخ
The third doubt for deniers of self-evident truths only is that it is said: temperaments and habits have an influence on beliefs; so a strong heart, according to temperament, deems inflicting pain good and does not deem it bad, but perhaps takes pleasure in it; and a weak heart deems it very bad; therefore you see some of them do not permit slaughtering animals for benefit by eating them. And whoever practices a school of thought, true or false, and becomes accustomed to it for a period of time and grows up on it, then by mere habituation, without any indication of its truth appearing to him, he becomes certain of its correctness, even if it is false, and deems false what opposes it, even if it is true. So it is possible that certainty from the intellect's self-evidence in all — i.e., everything that one judges by temperament or universal custom common to all individuals of humanity who agree on self-evident truths — is not certain, like propositions arising from specific temperaments and habits. It may not be said: We assume ourselves free from all temperaments and habits, and nevertheless we find in ourselves certainty about these self-evident matters, so the judge in them is pure intellect without influence of temperament or habit. (We) say: We do not concede the possibility of assuming freedom from all temperaments and habits, since we may not be aware of some temperamental or habitual dispositions, so how can we assume freedom from that part without being aware of it? And if the possibility of assuming freedom from all is granted, it does not follow from the assumption of freedom actual freedom in reality; do you not see that the miser does not cease his miserliness merely by assuming his freedom from it? Perhaps a continuous habit has become a settled disposition that does not disappear by disciplining the soul from it for a lifetime, let alone by mere assumption of its removal and freedom from it. And the answer is that what you have mentioned of the influence of temperaments and habits on beliefs and the occurrence of propositions does not prove the possibility of all — i.e., all self-evident propositions — being like that in certainty, because of the statement: (No) we do not concede the possibility of assuming freedom, etc.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the third doubt raised by deniers of self-evident truths (منكري البديهيات) and provides a refutation. The doubt argues that temperaments and habits influence beliefs, so even self-evident truths may not be certain. Examples are given: a strong heart may not find cruelty bad, and people become certain of false doctrines due to habituation. The objection that one can assume freedom from temperaments and habits and still find certainty in self-evident truths is then addressed. The response (لأنا نقول) denies the possibility of such an assumption because one may be unaware of subtle temperamental or habitual influences. Even if the assumption is granted, it does not entail actual freedom, as a miser does not cease miserliness by merely assuming it. Finally, the answer (والجواب) concludes that the examples of temperamental and habitual influence do not prove that all self-evident truths are similarly affected, referring back to the denial of the possibility of assuming freedom.

Key Terms
الأمزجة
العادات
الجزم
اليقينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this doubt completely disproves self-evident truths and leaves no certainty.
Correction: The doubt only challenges the claim that self-evident truths are necessarily certain; it does not prove they are false. Al-Iji will refute it in the next lesson.

Checkpoint
What example does the denier give to show that temperament influences belief?
A) A strong heart may take pleasure in inflicting pain.

C) People always agree on moral matters.
D) Habituation only causes doubt.

Key Takeaway
The third doubt claims that temperaments and habits can make us certain of falsehoods, suggesting that self-evident truths might be similarly unreliable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one belief you hold with absolute certainty that you have never questioned? Could it be that you hold it merely because of how you were raised or what you are accustomed to? Will you investigate it today?
?
Think of a time when you were convinced something was true simply because everyone around you believed it. How do you know your current certainties are not also just habits? What will you do to test them?
?
When you feel a strong emotional reaction to an idea—such as repulsion or attraction—could your temperament be clouding your judgment? How will you separate the influence of your nature from the truth?

Refuting the Third Doubt: Impossibility of Assuming Freedom from All Influences
Al-Iji refutes the third doubt through a dialectical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Iji refutes the third doubt by showing that the assumption of being free from all temperamental and habitual influences is impossible and does not prove that self-evident truths are uncertain.

Core Idea
You cannot assume you are free from influences you may not even be aware of.

The denier's argument from temperaments aims to undercut certainty, but Al-Iji's refutation exposes a flaw: we cannot simply imagine ourselves free from all influences. This lesson unpacks the dialectical exchange between the objector and the responder.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the first response
Read the part starting with 'It may not be said...' and the initial response. Notice how the responder denies the possibility of assuming freedom from all temperaments and habits.
The key point: we may not be aware of some influences, so we cannot assume they are absent.
2
Follow the second response
Read the conditional argument: even if the assumption were possible, it does not entail actual freedom. The example of the miser illustrates this.
The response uses the analogy of a miser who does not cease being miserly merely by assuming he is free from miserliness.
3
Understand the final answer
Read the concluding answer: the doubt does not prove that all self-evident truths are like that, because the denier cannot even validly assume freedom from all influences.
This ties back to the earlier denial of the possibility of the assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة ( الثالثة ) لمنكري البديهيات فقط أن يقال: (للأمزجة والعادات تأثير في الاعتقادات فقوى القلب) بحسب المزاج ( يستحسن الإيلام ولا يستقبحه بل ربما يلتذ به (وضعيف القلب يستقبحه جداً، ولذلك ترى بعضهم لا يجوزون ذبح الحيوانات للانتفاع بأكلها ( ومن مارس مذهباً من المذاهب ) حقاً كان أو باطلاً، واعتاد به ( برهة من الزمان ونشأ عليه فإنه ) بمجرد اعتياده به من غير أن يلوح له ما يظهر به حقيته ( يجزم بصحته )، وإن كان باطلاً ) وبطلان ما يخالفه وإن كان حقاً ( فجاز أن يكون الجزم) من بديهة العقل ( في الكل أي كل ما حكمت به لمزاج أو عادة عامين) لجميع أفراد الإنسان المتفقين في البديهيات، فلا تكون يقينية كالقضايا الصادرة من الأمزجة والعادات المخصوصة ) لا يقال : نحن نفرض أنفسنا خالية عن جميع الأمزجة والعادات ومع ذلك نجد من أنفسنا الجزم بهذه (الأمور البديهية، فالحاكم فيها صريح العقل بلا تأثير من مزاج أو عادة (الأنا) نقول : لا نسلم إمكان فرض الخلو) عن جميع الأمزجة والعادات ( إذ قد لا نشعر ببعض من الهيئات المزاجية أو العادية، فكيف نفرض الخلو عن ذلك البعض مع عدم الشعور به؟ (ولئن سلم) إمكان فرض الخلو عن الجميع ( فلا يلزم من فرض الخلو الخلو في نفس الأمر ألا یری أن البخيل لا يزول عنه بخله بمجرد فرض خلوه عنه ( ولعل عادة مستمرة صارت ملكة مستقرة لا تزول بتهذيب النفس ) عنها ( مدة العمر فضلا عن مجرد فرض ( زوالها والخلو عنها ) والجواب أنه أي ما ذكرتم من تأثير الأمزجة والعادات في الاعتقاديات وحصول القضايا ( لا يدل على جواز كون الكل أي جميع القضايا البديهية ذلك في الجزم بسبب قوله : (لا) نسلم إمكان فرض الخلو إلخ
The third doubt for deniers of self-evident truths only is that it is said: temperaments and habits have an influence on beliefs; so a strong heart, according to temperament, deems inflicting pain good and does not deem it bad, but perhaps takes pleasure in it; and a weak heart deems it very bad; therefore you see some of them do not permit slaughtering animals for benefit by eating them. And whoever practices a school of thought, true or false, and becomes accustomed to it for a period of time and grows up on it, then by mere habituation, without any indication of its truth appearing to him, he becomes certain of its correctness, even if it is false, and deems false what opposes it, even if it is true. So it is possible that certainty from the intellect's self-evidence in all — i.e., everything that one judges by temperament or universal custom common to all individuals of humanity who agree on self-evident truths — is not certain, like propositions arising from specific temperaments and habits. It may not be said: We assume ourselves free from all temperaments and habits, and nevertheless we find in ourselves certainty about these self-evident matters, so the judge in them is pure intellect without influence of temperament or habit. (We) say: We do not concede the possibility of assuming freedom from all temperaments and habits, since we may not be aware of some temperamental or habitual dispositions, so how can we assume freedom from that part without being aware of it? And if the possibility of assuming freedom from all is granted, it does not follow from the assumption of freedom actual freedom in reality; do you not see that the miser does not cease his miserliness merely by assuming his freedom from it? Perhaps a continuous habit has become a settled disposition that does not disappear by disciplining the soul from it for a lifetime, let alone by mere assumption of its removal and freedom from it. And the answer is that what you have mentioned of the influence of temperaments and habits on beliefs and the occurrence of propositions does not prove the possibility of all — i.e., all self-evident propositions — being like that in certainty, because of the statement: (No) we do not concede the possibility of assuming freedom, etc.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the third doubt raised by deniers of self-evident truths (منكري البديهيات) and provides a refutation. The doubt argues that temperaments and habits influence beliefs, so even self-evident truths may not be certain. Examples are given: a strong heart may not find cruelty bad, and people become certain of false doctrines due to habituation. The objection that one can assume freedom from temperaments and habits and still find certainty in self-evident truths is then addressed. The response (لأنا نقول) denies the possibility of such an assumption because one may be unaware of subtle temperamental or habitual influences. Even if the assumption is granted, it does not entail actual freedom, as a miser does not cease miserliness by merely assuming it. Finally, the answer (والجواب) concludes that the examples of temperamental and habitual influence do not prove that all self-evident truths are similarly affected, referring back to the denial of the possibility of assuming freedom.

Key Terms
الفرض
الخلو
الهيئات المزاجية
الملكة المستقرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the refutation relies on a circular argument (assuming what needs to be proven).
Correction: The refutation is not circular; it points out a logical flaw: the denier's assumption of freedom from all influences is impossible because we may be unaware of some influences. Even if possible, it does not entail actual freedom.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given for denying the possibility of assuming freedom from all temperaments and habits?
A) It is logically contradictory.
B) We may not be aware of some temperamental or habitual dispositions.
C) Freedom from influence is impossible by definition.
D) The assumption leads to skepticism.

Key Takeaway
Al-Iji refutes the third doubt by showing that the denier cannot validly assume freedom from all influences, and even if assumed, it does not prove that self-evident truths are uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume you are free from biases and habits that shape your deepest convictions. But how do you know you are not unaware of a hidden influence? Will you honestly examine your heart today to uncover a settled disposition that controls you without your awareness?
?
The miser cannot stop being miserly just by imagining he is generous. What is the miserly habit in your soul that you have assumed you are free from, yet it still controls your actions? When will you discipline yourself to truly remove it, not just pretend?
?
If you claim your certainty in Allah’s existence is purely rational and unaffected by your upbringing or temperament, are you willing to face the possibility that some of your certainty might be rooted in comfort or habit? Would your faith survive if all those external supports were stripped away? What are you clinging to besides the truth itself?

Refuting the Temperament Influence Doubt
Sanusi refutes the claim that temperament influence could negate certainty in all self-evident propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the certainty of self-evident propositions against the claim that temperament and habit could influence all necessary judgments.

Core Idea
Influence on some beliefs does not undermine necessity of self-evident truths.

The objection claims that since temperaments affect some beliefs, they could affect all, including necessary judgments. Sanusi responds by distinguishing types of assumptions and showing the objection fails. This lesson walks through his careful refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the English translation. Notice the dense logical structure.
The argument is technical; don't worry about every detail yet.
2
Identify the two types of assumptions
Sanusi distinguishes between the impossible assumption (mere estimation) and the possible assumption (intellectually permissible). Find these in the text.
This distinction is key to the refutation.
3
Understand the refutation for each assumption
Explain why the impossible assumption leads to contradiction, and why the possible assumption fails due to lack of awareness of some influences.
Follow the author's order in the passage.
4
Reflect on the final point about self-evident propositions
Note the comment that some self-evident truths like 'the whole is greater than the part' are immune to temperament influence.
This reinforces the defence of necessary judgments.

Therefore, the possibility of the assumption only necessitates the possibility of awareness, not actual awareness. And the author by his statement: 'so it does not follow from assuming freedom' where he did not address possibility. Also, his statement: 'And even if we grant, so it does not follow from assuming freedom actual freedom' is not compatible with it except with difficulty, as is not hidden from the contemplator. His statement: (And even if we grant etc.) The purport of granting is the sufficiency of total awareness and its realization. His statement: (i.e., all self-evident propositions) The restriction to self-evident indicates the permissibility that certainty in some self-evident propositions may be due to temperament or habit, though the meaning of self-evident is primary, unless the certainty is similarly based on the influence of temperament or habit, then the certainty that the whole is greater, i.e., greater than the part, is something that definitely has no entry for temperaments or habits.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the certainty of necessary judgments. The objector argued that since temperaments and habits can influence some beliefs, they could influence all self-evident propositions, thus undermining their necessity. The response first challenges the assumption that one can be completely free from all temperamental and habitual influences. It points out that we may not be aware of some of these influences, so we cannot validly assume freedom from them. Even if such an assumption were possible, it would not entail actual freedom, as illustrated by the miser who does not cease being miserly just by imagining his freedom from it. The response then distinguishes between two types of assumptions: the impossible assumption (mere estimation without possibility) and the possible assumption (what the intellect allows). The impossible assumption is invalid because it would lead to contradiction, such as the persistence of certainty in the same propositions. The possible assumption is rejected because the intellect's permission to assume freedom from something depends on awareness of that something; we may not be aware of all influences. Therefore, the objection does not prove that all self-evident propositions are subject to temperament and habit. The commentary further elaborates on the usage of the term 'possibility' and notes that even if some self-evident propositions could be influenced, certain ones like 'the whole is greater than the part' are clearly not. The response is structured to argue from the opponent's own premises and avoid circularity.

Key Terms
الأمزجة والعادات (temperaments and habits)
القضايا البديهية (self-evident propositions)
الفرض الممتنع (impossible assumption)
الفرض الممكن (possible assumption)
الجزم (certainty)
الإمكان (possibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If temperaments influence some beliefs, they must influence all beliefs, including self-evident truths.
Correction: Influence on some does not entail influence on all, especially for necessary judgments that are independent of temperament.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why is it not possible to assume complete freedom from all temperaments and habits?
A) Because temperaments are always present and cannot be removed.
B) Because we may not be aware of some temperaments and habits.
C) Because it is logically impossible to assume such freedom.
D) Because it contradicts self-evident propositions.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it confuses possible and impossible assumptions and overlooks that awareness is required for valid assumption, and some self-evident truths are clearly immune to temperament influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you aware of any temperament or habit that might be subtly influencing your acceptance of certain religious truths? Have you ever honestly examined whether your certainty is based on evidence or mere familiarity?
?
When you assume you are free from biases in your beliefs, are you truly free, or are you like the miser who imagines himself generous without changing? What would it take for you to actually uproot a deep-seated habit that contradicts your beliefs?
?
This lesson argues that some truths (like 'the whole is greater than the part') are immune to temperament. Which of your core beliefs do you hold with such necessity? And which ones might you have assumed too easily are immune?

The Fourth Doubt: Apparent Conflict of Decisive Proofs
Presents the fourth doubt and its initial refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth doubt raised against self-evident judgments and the key aspects of its refutation, including that the conflict is only apparent and refutation is by denial, objection, or counterargument.

Core Idea
Apparent conflicts between decisive proofs do not undermine the certainty of self-evident knowledge.

This lesson addresses a skeptical challenge: how can we trust rational knowledge if two apparently decisive proofs can conflict? The passage presents the doubt and the initial response, showing that the conflict is only apparent and that refutation is possible by denial, objection, or counterargument. The debate focuses on whether certainty in premises can coexist with falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt statement
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the formulation of the fourth doubt by those who deny self-evident judgments. Notice how they claim that practice of rational sciences reveals possible conflict between decisive proofs.
Pay attention to 'مزاولة العلوم العقلية دلت على أنه قد يتعارض دليلان قاطعان'.
2
Identify the refutation's structure
The commentator explains the qualifiers (بحسب الظاهر) and the methods of refutation (المنع والنقض والمعارضة). Also note the response about certainty in premises.
The refutation emphasizes that real conflict is impossible; it's only apparent.
3
Understand the scope of the argument
The passage clarifies that the argument only applies to those who accept self-evident premises, not to those who deny them entirely. It also defines refutation as not accepting a proof when considered independently from its contradictory.
This limits the force of the doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

إحداهما خطأ قطعاً، وإلا ) أي وإن لم تكن إحداهما وهي للمجيب منع استلزامه ذلك الجواز الكلي فلا يرد أن الجواب مشعر بجواز تأثيرها في بعض البديهيات، أي الأوليات وليس كذلك. قوله : ( فإن الجزم بكون الكل إلخ هذا تبرع من المجيب ولا حاجة له إليه، لأنه مانع يكفيه مجرد الجواز، فلا يرد أن لهم أن يمنعوا ذلك فإنهم ينكرون البديهيات، فلا يسمعون دعوى البداهة في عدم المدخلية للمزاج والعادة . قوله : ( بحسب الظاهر قيد به إذ لا يمكن تعارض القواطع حقيقة . قوله : ( عن القدح فيهما ) بالمنع والنقض والمعارضة. قوله : (إلا) للجزم بمقدماتهما إلخ) أي الجزم بصحتها بداهة كما صرح به الشارح، أما الصحة فلان الجزم بالمقدمات ليس معناه إلا الجزم بصحتها وكونها صادقة وأما البداهة فلأنه لا يتم التقريب بدونها، إذا الجزم بأحكام النظر مع كونه إحداهما خطأ يوجب ارتفاع الوثوق عن أحكام البداهة، وهذه مقدمة ثانية للدليل معطوفة على قوله مزاولة العلوم العقلية إلخ، وذلك لأنه لولا الجزم بها لكان لنا القدرة على القدح فيها ولا أقل من المنع. على التنزل أو يقال : سلب الدلالة على جواز الإيجاب الكلي لا ينافي سلب الدلالة على جواز الإيجاب الجزئي، حتى يرد الاعتراض نعم تعرض للأول ليكون رد المدعي الخصم صريحاً، والحق أن المراد من القضايا البديهية القضايا المعدودة منها، وحينئذ لا محذور فتأمل. قوله: (ليس مما للأمزجة أو العادات إلخ) لهم أن يمنعوا بذلك فإنهم ينكرون البديهيات فلا يسمعون دعوى البديهة في عدم المدخلية للمزاج أو العادة والحق أن هذا وسائر ما ذكر من قبل في إثبات كون البديهيات موثوقاً بها، إنما ينتهض على من يعترف بمعلومية المقدمات البديهية أو المنتهية إليها المذكورة في صدد الإثبات لا على من أنكرها، وقد سبق الإشارة إلى مثله في الاستدلال على أن الكل ليس بنظري. قوله : ( وما هو إلا للجزم بمقدماتهما ) الواو في قوله وما هو حالية والجملة قيد لما قبلها، فمحصول الكلام أنه قد يتعارض قاطعان بحيث يعجز عن القدح بهذا السبب، وليس المراد أن العجز في جميع مواقع التعارض لذلك ثم المراد من القدح أن يقال : لا نسلم إذا نظر إلى كل واحد لدليلين من قطع النظر عن الآخر المعارض، وهذا ظاهر الوجوه، فعلى هذا التقدير لا
The fourth doubt of the group that denies self-evident judgments, only their statement: (Practice of the rational sciences indicated that) two decisive proofs sometimes contradict each other in appearance, so that one is unable to refute both, and that is only due to certainty in their premises, even though one of them is definitely false. And if not, i.e., if one of them is not false, then for the respondent there is a denial of its entailing that universal permissibility, so it is not objected that the answer indicates the permissibility of their influence on some self-evident propositions, i.e., primary ones, but it is not so. His statement: (For certain that the whole is greater etc.) This is a voluntary addition by the respondent, and he has no need for it, because he is a preventer, and mere possibility suffices him, so it is not objected that they can deny that, for they deny self-evident propositions, so they do not accept the claim of self-evidence in the non-influence of temperament and habit. His statement: (in appearance) he qualified with it because real conflict of decisive proofs cannot occur. His statement: (from refuting both) by denial, objection, and counterargument. His statement: (except by certainty in their premises etc.) i.e., certainty in their correctness by self-evidence, as the commentator clarified. As for correctness, because certainty in premises means only certainty in their truth and being truthful. As for self-evidence, because the approximation is not complete without it, since certainty in judgments of reflection, while one of them is false, would cause loss of confidence in self-evident judgments. And this is a second premise of the proof, conjoined to his statement 'practice of the rational sciences etc.', because if not for certainty in them, we would have the ability to refute them, at least by denial. Upon concession or to the effect that the negation of indication of universal permissibility does not negate the indication of particular permissibility, so that the objection would arise. Yes, he addressed the first to make the refutation of the opponent's claim explicit. And the truth is that by 'self-evident propositions' is meant those counted among them, and then there is no difficulty, so consider. His statement: (not from temperaments or habits etc.) They have grounds to deny that, for they deny self-evident propositions, so they do not accept the claim of self-evidence in the non-influence of temperament or habit. And the truth is that this and all that was mentioned before in proving that self-evident propositions are reliable, only applies to one who acknowledges the knowability of self-evident premises or those that ultimately lead to them mentioned in the context of proof, not to one who denies them. And reference was already made to an example of this in the argument that the whole is not theoretical. His statement: (And it is only by certainty in their premises) The waw in his statement 'and it is' is a circumstantial clause, and the sentence is a qualifier for what precedes it. The gist is that two decisive proofs may conflict such that one is unable to refute them for this reason, and it is not meant that inability occurs in all cases of conflict for that reason. Then the meaning of refutation is to say: we do not accept, when considering each of the two proofs independently, disregarding the other contradictory proof, and this is obvious. On this estimation, no.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt and its initial refutation. The doubt is raised by those who deny self-evident judgments. They argue that the practice of rational sciences shows that two apparently decisive proofs can conflict, leaving one unable to refute either because of certainty in their premises, even though one of those premises must be false. This challenges the reliability of self-evident knowledge. The response first notes that the conflict is only apparent, not real, because decisive proofs cannot truly contradict each other. The inability to refute them is due to certainty in their premises by self-evidence. However, if one premise is false, that certainty would be undermined. The response clarifies that the refutation can be done by denial, objection, or counterargument. The commentator explains that the respondent does not need to argue that certain self-evident propositions are immune to influence, because the opponent who denies all self-evidence will not accept such appeals. The argument ultimately only applies to those who acknowledge self-evident premises. The passage ends by defining refutation as not accepting a proof when considered independently from its contradictory.

Misconception: The existence of conflicting decisive proofs shows that self-evident knowledge is unreliable.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the conflict is only apparent, and the inability to refute is due to approaching each proof independently; it does not undermine self-evident knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The fourth doubt about conflicting decisive proofs is refuted by noting that the conflict is only apparent and that refutation can be done by denial, objection, or counterargument.

The Fourth Shubha and First Response on Conflicting Proofs
Al-Jurjani presents the fourth shubha and initial reply about temporary incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth shubha against self-evident judgments and al-Jurjani's first response, which limits the conflict to temporary incapacity.

Core Idea
Even conflicting certain proofs do not permanently undermine trust in intuition.

This lesson presents the fourth sophistical challenge raised by those who deny self-evident judgments. They argue that if two apparently certain proofs conflict, one must be false, and yet the intellect is certain of both—so trust is lost. Al-Jurjani responds that such conflict is temporary; truth eventually prevails, but even temporary doubt sows uncertainty. Let's examine the argument and the reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice how the shubha builds from a specific scenario to a general conclusion.
2
Identify the shubha's structure
The shubha claims that through rational investigation we may encounter two conflicting certain proofs. Trace how this leads to a problem for intuition: one premise must be false, yet the intellect is certain of it, so trust is removed.
The key move is from 'conflict exists' to 'intuition can be certain of a false premise'.
3
Understand the response
Al-Jurjani first concedes temporary inability to refute, but denies it is permanent. Reflect on why temporary certainty in a false premise is still problematic for the shubha's claim.
The response aims to limit the damage: the shubha's scenario does not show a universal flaw, only a transient one.

الشبهة ( الرابعة ) للفرقة المنكرة للأحكام البديهية فقط قولهم : ( مزاولة العلوم العقلية دلت على أنه ( قد ( يتعارض ) دليلان ( قاطعان) بحسب الظاهر بحيث ( تعجز عن القدح فيهما وما هو ) أي العجز عن القدح فيهما إلا للجزم بمقدماتهما مع أن إحداها أي إحدى تلك المقدمات، الأمور المعتبرة في صحة الدليلين ( خطأ قطعاً، وإلا ) أي وإن لم تكن إحداها خطأ بل كانت بأسرها صواباً (اجتمع النقيضان في الواقع لصحة الدليلين حينئذ، وإذا كانت إحداهما خطأ مع جزم بديهة العقل بصحتها فقد ارتفع الوثوق عن أحكامها ) فإن قيل : لا نسلم العجز عن القدح فيهما دائماً ( فإن ذلك ) العجز (لا يدوم ويحق الحق ويبطل الباطل من ذينك الدليلين المتعارضين ( عن كثب ) أي قرب ( قلنا : نحن لا ندعي العجز عن القدح دائماً بل بالإطلاق فحين العجز، ولو أنا نجزم بما لا يجوز الجزم به وإنه ) أي الجزم في آن بما لا يجوز الجزم به ( كاف في رفع الثقة عن أحكام البديهة
The fourth doubt for the group that denies self-evident judgments is their saying: 'The practice of rational sciences indicates that there may conflict two proofs that are certain apparently, such that one is [unable to refute them] — that is, the inability to refute them is only due to certainty in their premises, while one of those premises — the matters considered in the validity of the two proofs — is definitely false; otherwise, if not one of them false but all were true, then contradictories would combine in reality due to the validity of the two proofs, and if one of them is false while the intellect's intuition is certain of its truth, then trust in its judgments has been removed.' Then it is said: 'We do not concede permanent inability to refute them, because that inability does not last, and truth prevails and falsehood is invalidated from those two conflicting proofs after a short while.' We say: 'We do not claim permanent inability to refute, but absolutely at the time of inability, and if we are certain of what is not permissible to be certain of, that is sufficient to remove trust in the judgments of intuition.'
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth sophistical argument (shubha) from a group that denies the reliability of self-evident judgments (badihiyyat), followed by the first part of the response. The shubha argues that through rational investigation, one may encounter two arguments that appear certain and yet conflict, and one cannot refute either, the reason being that one is certain of their premises. But one of those premises must be false, or else both would be true and lead to contradiction. If one is false while intuition is certain of it, then trust in all intuitive judgments is lost. The response first concedes that such a situation may occur temporarily, but denies that it is permanent. Truth will eventually prevail and falsehood be exposed. However, even temporary certainty in something false is sufficient to shake confidence in intuitive knowledge. This answer acknowledges the problem but limits it to the moment of incapacity. The commentary further clarifies that the shubha does not prove a universal flaw in intuition.

Key Terms
البديهية (badihiyya, self-evident truths)
الجزم (jazzm, certainty)
القاطع (qat'i, certain proof)
التعارض (ta'arud, conflict)
القدح (qadh, refutation)
العجز ('ajz, incapacity)
الوثوق (wuthuq, trust)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth shubha proves that certainty is impossible in all cases.
Correction: The shubha only points to a temporary situation where certainty conflicts with truth; the response shows it does not permanently eliminate trust.

Checkpoint

A) Intuitive judgments are always correct.
B) Conflicting certain proofs are impossible.
C) Since intuition can be certain of a false premise, all intuitive judgments become untrustworthy.
D) The practice of rational sciences eliminates all doubts.

Key Takeaway
The fourth shubha challenges self-evident judgments using the possibility of conflicting certain proofs, but al-Jurjani's response shows the conflict is only temporary and does not void all trust.

The Definition of Badihi and Its Dependence on Proper Abstraction
Al-Jurjani defines badihi and explains how error occurs without undermining all intuitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of intuitive judgment (badihi) and how the possibility of error in some intuitive judgments does not undermine the reliability of all intuitive knowledge.

Core Idea
Intuitive judgments are reliable only when their terms are properly abstracted — error reveals incomplete abstraction, not flawed intuition.

In defending the reliability of intuitive knowledge, al-Jurjani provides a precise definition of what 'intuitive' (badihi) means. He then shows that errors only occur when the conditions of that definition are not fully met, thus preserving the trustworthiness of intuitions that are properly formed. This lesson explores that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the definition and the conditional clause about error.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the definition of badihi
Find the sentence that defines badihi. What are its components? (conception of termini, observation of relation, judgment).
Badihi is not just any immediate judgment; it has specific conditions.
3
Understand the role of abstraction (tajrid)
The definition states that badihi depends on abstracting the termini from irrelevant factors. Why is this necessary? How does incomplete abstraction cause error?
Error occurs when there is 'khafa' (obscurity) in the abstraction.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage argues that error in some intuitive judgments does not remove trust from all intuitive judgments. Why? Because the error is due to a defect in the abstraction, not in intuition itself.
This answer protects the reliability of properly formed intuitions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب بعد تسليم كون مقدمات ذينك الدليلين المتعارضين بديهية ( إن البديهي ما يجزم به بتصور (الطرفين) مع ملاحظة النسبة بينهما ( فيتوقف ( البديهي ( على تجريدهما ) أي تجريد الطرفين عما لا مدخل له في ذلك الحكم، وتعلقهما على وجه هو مناط الحكم فيما بينهما ( فاعل فيه ) أي في تجريد الطرفين وتعلقهما على ذلك الوجه ( خللاً) لوجود خفاء فيهما إما قوله : ( بعد تسليم إلخ ) أي لا نسلم أن مقدماتهما بديهية حتى يكون خطؤنا فيها موجباً لرفع الوثوق عن الأحكام مطلقاً، واعلم أن خلاصة الشبهة المذكورة أن البديهة قد تجزم ببعض مع كونها خطأ، فارتفع الوثوق عن أحكامها مطلقاً، وحاصل الجواب أن البديهي يتوقف على تصور الطرفين كما هو مناط الحكم، فإذا لم يتصور كذلك أخطأت البديهة في ذلك البديهي، وحكمت بخلاف الواقع، وذلك لا يوجب ارتفاع الوثوق عن أحكامها فيما تصور أطرافها على ما هو مناط الحكم بلا شبهة، فتدبر فقد زل فيه أقدام
And the answer after conceding that the premises of those two conflicting proofs are intuitive: 'The intuitive is that which one judges by mere conception of the two termini with observation of the relation between them. So the intuitive depends on their abstraction — that is, abstracting the two termini from what has no input in that judgment, and their relation in a manner that is the basis of the judgment between them. If there occurs a defect in that — that is, in abstracting the two termini and their relation in that manner — due to some obscurity in them...' He said: 'After conceding, etc.' — that is, we do not concede that their premises are intuitive, such that our error in them would cause the removal of trust from judgments absolutely. And know that the summary of the mentioned doubt is that intuition may be certain of something while it is false, so trust in its judgments is removed absolutely. The gist of the answer is that the intuitive depends on the conception of the two termini as is the basis of the judgment. So if it is not conceived in that manner, intuition errs in that intuitive judgment and rules contrary to reality, and that does not necessitate the removal of trust from its judgments concerning what it conceives of its termini on the basis of the judgment without doubt. So reflect, for feet have slipped here.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage provides the second, more fundamental answer to the shubha. It begins by conceding the premise that the conflicting proofs' premises are intuitive, then offers a definition of the intuitive (badihi). The intuitive is a judgment made upon mere conception of the two terms and the relation between them. Such judgment depends on proper abstraction (tajrid) of the terms from irrelevant factors and relating them in the manner that is the basis (manat) of the judgment. If there is a defect in this abstraction due to obscurity in the terms, error can occur. The commentary then clarifies that this answer does not require conceding the premises are intuitive; rather, it explains that even if an intuitive judgment errs, it is because of incomplete abstraction, not because intuition itself is unreliable. Thus, trust is only removed for that specific case, not for all intuitive judgments. The author warns that many have stumbled here, urging careful reflection.

التصور (tasawwur, conception)
النسبة (nisba, relation)
التجريد (tajrid, abstraction)
المناط (manat, basis)
الخفاء (khafa', obscurity)
الخطأ (khata', error)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An error in any intuitive judgment shows that all intuitive judgments are unreliable.
Correction: The passage explains that error occurs only when the abstraction of the terms is defective; properly formed intuitive judgments remain trustworthy.

Checkpoint

A) Intuition is inherently unreliable.
B) The premises are not actually intuitive.
C) There is a defect in the abstraction of the termini and their relation.

Key Takeaway
Intuitive judgments are defined by proper abstraction of termini; errors in intuition are always due to incomplete abstraction, not a flaw in intuition itself.

Objection on Incapacity to Refute and Its Answer
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and its answer regarding certainty in intuitive judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the reliability of intuitive judgments against the objection that temporary inability to refute a false argument undermines all intuitive knowledge.

Core Idea
Only defective intuitive judgments are vulnerable; properly stripped ones remain trustworthy.

This lesson examines a critical objection to the reliability of intuitive knowledge: if we can be certain of something that later turns out false, how can we trust any intuitive judgment? Al-Jurjani responds by clarifying the conditions for genuine intuitiveness, showing that error arises only when the conceptualization is flawed. The passage is essential for understanding how classical scholars preserve the foundation of certain knowledge against skeptical challenges.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objector's claim: that temporary incapacity to refute a conflicting argument leads to certainty in falsehood, which undermines trust in all intuitive judgments. Notice how the objector uses the example of two conflicting arguments.
The objector concedes that incapacity is not permanent, but argues that the mere occurrence of certainty in what is false is enough to damage trust.
2
Analyze the first part of the answer
Al-Jurjani first responds by limiting the scope of the claim: we do not claim permanent incapacity, only incapacity at a particular time. He then argues that being certain of something that should not be certain at that time is sufficient to remove trust from that specific judgment, but not all intuitive judgments.
This part addresses the objector's assumption that any error in an intuitive judgment would generalize.
3
Understand the main response about conditions
The core of the answer is the definition of 'intuitive' (badihi): a judgment that one is certain of by conceiving the two extremes (subject and predicate) and observing the relation between them, after stripping away extraneous elements. Error occurs only when the stripping or connection is flawed, either because the terms are theoretical or due to other obscurities.
Thus, only intuitive judgments that are not properly stripped can be erroneous; genuine intuitive judgments remain reliable.
4
Connect the answer to the objection
Finally, see how the answer fully rebuts the objection: the objector's case involves a defective intuitive judgment (because the premises are not properly stripped), so it does not affect truly intuitive judgments. This preserves the foundation of knowledge.
The key is that the objector's example does not meet the strict conditions for intuitiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم العجز عن القدح فيهما دائماً ( فإن ذلك ) العجز (لا يدوم ويحق الحق ويبطل الباطل من ذينك الدليلين المتعارضين ( عن كثب ) أي قرب ( قلنا : نحن لا ندعي العجز عن القدح دائماً بل بالإطلاق فحين العجز، ولو أنا نجزم بما لا يجوز الجزم به وإنه ) أي الجزم في آن بما لا يجوز الجزم به ( كاف في رفع الثقة عن أحكام البديهة (والجواب بعد تسليم كون مقدمات ذينك الدليلين المتعارضين بديهية ( إن البديهي ما يجزم به بتصور (الطرفين) مع ملاحظة النسبة بينهما ( فيتوقف ( البديهي ( على تجريدهما ) أي تجريد الطرفين عما لا مدخل له في ذلك الحكم، وتعلقهما على وجه هو مناط الحكم فيما بينهما ( فاعل فيه ) أي في تجريد الطرفين وتعلقهما على ذلك الوجه ( خللاً) لوجود خفاء فيهما إما لكونهما نظريين، أو لغير ذلك فيتطرق الخطأ إلى البديهي لهذا السبب، فلا يلزم رفع الثقة عن البديهيات التي جرد أطرافها على ما هو حقها .
If it is said: We do not concede the incapacity to refute them always; indeed that incapacity does not persist, and truth becomes clear and falsehood becomes invalid from those two conflicting arguments from near, meaning soon. We say: We do not claim incapacity to refute always, but absolutely at the time of incapacity. And if we are certain of something which it is not permissible to be certain of, and that certainty at a time of something which it is not permissible to be certain of, is sufficient to remove trust from the judgments of intuition. And the answer after conceding that the premises of those two conflicting arguments are intuitive: Indeed the intuitive is that which one is certain of by conceiving the two extremes with observation of the relation between them. So the intuitive depends on their being stripped, i.e., stripping the two extremes of what has no entry in that judgment, and their connection in a manner that is the basis of the judgment between them. So if there occurs in it, i.e., in stripping the two extremes and their connection in that manner, a defect due to the presence of obscurity in them, either because they are theoretical or for other reasons, then error occurs in the intuitive for this reason. So it is not necessary that trust be removed from the intuitive judgments whose extremes have been stripped in the proper manner.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage contains an objection-and-answer pair within the discussion of the fourth doubt regarding the reliability of intuitive knowledge. The objector argues that if we are temporarily incapable of refuting a false argument, our certainty in the premises that led to it remains, and later when we discover the error, we realize we were certain of something false, thereby undermining trust in all intuitive judgments. The answer rebuts this by distinguishing between the incapacity to refute and the actual certainty: we do not claim permanent incapacity, but at the moment of incapacity we may still be certain of something that turns out false, which does indeed remove trust from that particular judgment. However, the main response is that intuitive judgments depend on proper conceptualization: an intuitive proposition is one we are certain of by clearly conceiving the two terms (subject and predicate) and their relationship. If there is any obscurity in the terms or their connection—due to their being theoretical or other reasons—then error can occur. Thus, error in an apparent intuitive judgment only happens when the conditions for genuine intuitiveness (proper stripping of extraneous elements) are not met. Consequently, this does not undermine trust in truly intuitive judgments where those conditions are fulfilled.

Key Terms
بديهي
قدح
جزم
تجريد
طرفان
مناط الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we are temporarily unable to refute a false argument, then all intuitive judgments become untrustworthy.
Correction: Only intuitive judgments that are not properly stripped (i.e., have obscure terms or connections) are affected; truly intuitive judgments remain reliable.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani's answer, what condition must be met for an intuitive judgment to be reliable?
A) The premises must be conceded by all parties.
B) The two extremes must be properly stripped of extraneous elements and the relation correctly observed.
C) The judgment must be made with absolute certainty at all times.
D) The argument must be immune to any future refutation.

Key Takeaway
Error in an apparent intuitive judgment only occurs when its terms are not properly stripped; genuine intuitive judgments remain reliable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have been certain about something that later turned out false, did you ever stop to examine whether that certainty was built on properly stripped premises—or was it clouded by unexamined assumptions? Al-Jurjani says genuine intuitive judgments remain reliable. Are you willing to revisit those past errors and see where the stripping failed, rather than abandoning trust in all intuition? What would change if you applied this condition to every conviction you hold today?
?
The passage warns that error enters when the terms are not properly stripped. Think of a belief you hold strongly about your own faith or practice—perhaps about a theological issue or a daily ritual. Have you honestly investigated whether that belief is based on extremes stripped of extraneous influences (like cultural assumptions, emotional attachment, or incomplete knowledge)? What would you find if you examined the 'stripping' process? Can you commit to testing one such belief this week by honestly analyzing its foundation?
?
The objector argues that temporary incapacity to refute undermines trust in all intuition. Do you sometimes dismiss an entire category of knowledge—say, your inner sense of right and wrong, or your certainty in basic Islamic beliefs—because one instance failed you? How does Al-Jurjani's answer challenge your tendency to generalize from a single doubt? What is one judgment you have allowed doubt to corrode that, upon honest examination, might actually be properly stripped and worthy of trust? What step will you take to restore that trust and act on it?

The Dispute over the Judgment that Harm is Evil
Iji introduces the first disputed claim of necessary knowledge: the judgment that harm is evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'aris and philosophers classify the judgment 'harm is evil' as a well-known proposition rather than a primary intuition.

Core Idea
The intuition that harm is evil is not universally accepted as self-evident.

A key debate in Islamic theology concerns the status of moral judgments: are they known necessarily by the intellect, or are they conventional? This passage introduces the first disputed claim—that harm is evil—and presents the disagreement between those who uphold its intuitive certainty and those who relegate it to the category of widely accepted but fallible opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic text and its translation to get a clear understanding of the core claim and the responses from the Ash'aris and philosophers.
2
Identify the Two Positions
Notice that one group claims the judgment is intuitive (badiha al-aql), while the other denies it is primary and instead categorizes it as mashhurat.
3
Reflect on the Classification
Consider what it means for a judgment to be among the 'well-known propositions' (mashhurat) rather than primary ones. Think about the implications for moral reasoning.
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضار قبيح قالوا : يحكم بذلك بديهة العقل وأنكره الأشاعرة والحكماء واتفقوا على أنها ليست من القضايا الأولية بل من المشهورات التي قد تكون كاذبة وقد تكون صادقة
Harm is evil. They said: the intellect judges that by intuition. The Ash'aris and the philosophers denied it, and they agreed that it is not among the primary propositions but among the well-known ones (mashhurat) which may be false and may be true.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the first of several disputed claims about necessary knowledge (al-'ulum al-daruriyya). The claim is that the judgment 'harm is evil' is known by the primary intuition of the intellect. However, the Ash'aris (al-Ash'ariyya) and the philosophers (al-hukama) deny this claim of intuition. They do not consider it a primary proposition (al-qadaya al-awwaliyya), which are self-evidently true without need for evidence. Instead, they categorize it among the 'well-known propositions' (al-mashhurat), which are opinions accepted widely but can be false or true. The reason, as later commentary explains, is that the good and evil in question are not absolute moral qualities but relate to reward and punishment, which the intellect cannot determine independently.

Key Terms
الضار
القبيح
بديهة العقل
القضايا الأولية
المشهورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'aris deny the objective reality of good and evil altogether.
Correction: They only deny that it is a primary rational judgment, especially concerning reward and punishment, not that good and evil are completely unreal.

Checkpoint
What do the Ash'aris and philosophers claim about the judgment that harm is evil?
A) It is a primary intellectual judgment.
B) It is among the well-known propositions that may be false.
C) It is completely false.
D) It is not recognized.

Key Takeaway
The judgment that harm is evil is not a necessary intellectual intuition but a widely held opinion that can be true or false within Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something is obviously good or evil without questioning whether your judgment is based on intuition or societal consensus? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the foundation of your moral judgments?
?
If the claim 'harm is evil' is not a self-evident truth for the Ash'aris, then even our most basic moral intuitions may need to be tested by revelation. What moral intuition do you hold most strongly, and are you willing to subject it to the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
The passage says that mashhurat can be false. Think of a common moral belief in your society that might be false when judged by Islamic criteria. How does this realization push you to seek knowledge rather than follow the crowd?

Refuting the Servant's Independent Creation of Actions
Iji presents the second disputed claim and its refutation via preponderant cause argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'aris and philosophers refute the claim that the servant independently creates his actions, using the argument of a preponderant cause and the dependence of all contingent acts on God's will.

Core Idea
The servant's actions cannot be independent because they require a preponderant cause beyond his own power.

In this passage, Iji presents the second disputed claim of necessary knowledge: the assertion that human beings independently create their own voluntary actions. He then explains how the Ash'aris and philosophers counter this claim with a logical argument based on the necessity of a preponderant cause, which ultimately leads back to God's eternal will. This refutation is central to the Islamic theological understanding of human agency and divine predestination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then refer to the translation. Notice how Iji sets up the claim and then the counter-argument.
Pay attention to the structure: claim, counter-claim, reasoning, conclusion.
2
Identify the core claim
Identify what the Mu'tazila claim about the servant's actions and why they consider it intuitive.
The claim is that the servant is an independent creator of his actions and that this is a necessary judgment.
3
Follow the counter-argument
Trace the Ash'ari-Philosopher reasoning: the necessity of a preponderant cause, its externality to avoid infinite regress, and its ultimate identification with God's will.
Consider the example of movement to the right or left.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Reflect on the conclusion that the servant retains no independence in choice, and how this does not negate choice itself but only absolute independence.
Understand that this argument places human actions within divine causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية لهم أيضاً فإنهم قالوا: (العبد) (موجد) بالاستقلال ( لأفعاله) الاختيارية متمكن من فعلها وتركها بيده زمام الاختيار فيها وادعى بعضهم أن هذا الحكم بديهي (وهما ) أي الأشاعرة والحكماء (منعاه ) أي كذبا هذا الحكم ) وعارضاه ) أي قابلا ادعاء الضرورة فيه ) بضرورة أخرى في أنه لا بد له أي للفعل الصادر عن العبد من مرجح) يرجح أحد طرفيه الجائزين على الآخر، فإن حركته يمنة ويسرة إذا كانتا جائزتين منه على سواء، فلا بد بالضرورة في صدور إحداهما عنه من مرجح يرجحها على الأخرى ( فهو ) أي فذلك المرجح ( من خارج ) أي لا يكون صادرا عن العبد ) وإلا تسلسل ما صدر صدر عنه من أفعاله إلى ما لا يتناهى، بل ذلك المرجح أمر واجب هو إرادته تعالى إما بغير واسطة وإما بوسائط فإن استناد الجائز إلى الواجب أمر ضروري، ومع هذا الاستناد لم يبق للعبد التمكن والاستقلال بالاختيار
The second one is theirs also: indeed they said: the servant (al-'abd) is an independent creator (mujid) of his voluntary actions (af'alih al-ikhtiyariyya), capable of doing and leaving them, with the reins of choice in his hand. Some claimed that this judgment is intuitive (badihi). The two groups, i.e., the Ash'aris and the philosophers, denied it, i.e., they belied this judgment, and they countered the claim of necessity (darura) in it with another necessity: that there must be for the action issued from the servant a preponderant cause (murajjih) that weighs one of its two possible sides over the other, because if his movement to the right and left are both possible from him on an equal footing, then necessarily in the issuance of one of them from him there must be a preponderant cause that weighs it over the other. So that preponderant cause is external, i.e., not issuing from the servant, otherwise there would be an infinite regress of what issued from him of his actions. Rather, that preponderant cause is a necessary thing—it is His will, exalted, either without intermediation or through intermediaries—for the dependence of the contingent upon the necessary is necessary, and with this dependence the servant retains no capability and independence in choice.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the second disputed claim of necessary knowledge. The Mu'tazila (and some others) claim that the servant independently creates his own voluntary actions and that this is intuitively known. The Ash'aris and philosophers reject this claim of intuition. They counter with another necessity: any contingent action must have a preponderant cause that tips the balance between its possible occurrence and non-occurrence. For example, if a person's movement to the right or left is equally possible, the actual movement requires a cause that makes one preferred. This cause must be external to the servant to avoid an infinite regress of acts. Ultimately, that cause is the eternal will of God. Since the action depends on God's will, the servant cannot be truly independent. Thus, the claim of intuitive independence is refuted.

Key Terms
العبد
موجد بالاستقلال
بديهي
مرجح
تسلسل
إرادته تعالى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies all human choice and free will.
Correction: The argument only denies absolute independence; it affirms that choice occurs but within the framework of divine causation.

Checkpoint
What is the counter-necessity the Ash'aris and philosophers use against the claim of the servant's independent creation of actions?
A) The action must have a preponderant cause external to the servant.
B) The servant's actions are determined by his own will without external cause.
C) The action is impossible without the servant's direct power.
D) The servant's actions are eternal and uncreated.

Key Takeaway
The claim that the servant independently creates actions is refuted by the necessity of a preponderant cause external to the servant, which is ultimately God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a small choice, like which direction to walk, do you ever pause to recognize that even that movement depends on a preponderant cause from God? How would your awareness of this change your reliance on Him throughout the day?
?
If you truly believe that your actions are not independently created by you, then what is your real role in your good deeds? Are you more of a recipient of grace than an originator? Does this humble you or make you complacent?
?
Consider a recent sin you committed. If that sin was ultimately dependent on God's will, does that make you feel excused? Or does it drive you to seek His forgiveness more earnestly, knowing that He could have given you the murajjih to avoid it? Which response is more truthful to the lesson?

Dispute over Intuitive Impossibility of Certain Visions
Iji presents the third disputed claim: intuitive impossibility of certain visions, denied by Ash'aris.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'aris reject the claim that certain visions are intuitively impossible, and how this relates to the nature of necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
What seems intuitively impossible may still be within God's power.

The philosophers and Mu'tazila argue that some visual impossibilities are known by intuition, such as a blind person seeing a distant bug in the dark. However, the Ash'aris challenge this, asserting that God's omnipotence transcends usual causal conditions. This lesson examines the third disputed claim of necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the examples used by the philosophers and Mu'tazila.
The passage is concise; each phrase carries theological weight.
2
Identify the two impossible visions
Find the two specific claims of intuitive impossibility: seeing a distant bug in darkness and seeing non-facing objects. Understand why they are considered impossible by intuition.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
The Ash'aris 'considered it possible'—meaning they deny the claim of impossibility. Reflect on why they would reject such seemingly obvious impossibilities.
This is a key point about the nature of necessary knowledge in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة للحكماء والمعتزلة أيضاً قالوا : يمتنع بالبديهة رؤية أعمى الصين في ظلمة الليل بقة الأندلس ويمتنع أيضا بالبديهة رؤية ما لا يكون مقابلا للرائي أو في حكمه كما في رؤية الأشياء في المرآة فإنها في حكم المقابل وجوزه أي ما ذكر من الرؤيتين الأشعرية فقد كذبوهم في دعوى الامتناع فضلا عن كون العلم بالامتناع ضروريا
The third one is for the philosophers and also the Mu'tazila: they said that it is impossible by intuition for a blind Chinese person to see a louse of Andalus in the darkness of night, and it is also impossible by intuition to see what is not facing the viewer or in its ruling, like seeing things in a mirror, for they are in the ruling of what is facing. The Ash'aris considered it possible, i.e., both of the mentioned visions, so they belied them in the claim of impossibility, let alone that the knowledge of impossibility is necessary.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents the third disputed claim of necessary knowledge. The philosophers and the Mu'tazila claim that it is intuitively impossible for a blind person in China to see a small bug in Andalusia on a dark night, and also impossible to see something that is not directly facing the viewer (or in the ruling of facing, like images in mirrors). They assert these impossibilities are known by intuition. However, the Ash'aris consider both visions possible, thus rejecting the claim of impossibility and denying that such knowledge is necessary. The Ash'aris argue that God can create vision without the usual conditions, so there is no rational impossibility.

Key Terms

أعمى الصين

مقابل
في حكم المقابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some think intuitive impossibility means something is absolutely impossible under any circumstances.
Correction: Ash'aris argue that what is intuitively impossible under normal conditions may still be possible by divine power, so the impossibility is not absolute.

Checkpoint

B) They believe God can create vision without normal conditions.
C) They think the blind person can see with inner vision.
D) They argue that darkness does not affect vision.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris affirm that God can make any vision possible, so claims of intuitive impossibility are not necessarily binding.

?
The philosophers said certain things are intuitively impossible. What 'intuitive impossibilities' do you hold about your own spiritual growth? Do you believe God can change your heart even when it seems impossible? Commit to one step of faith today, asking God to do what you think cannot be done.

The Permanence of Accidents: Intuition vs. Ash'ari Denial
Iji presents the fourth disputed claim: the intuitive permanence of accidents, denied by Ash'aris and many Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether accidents persist over time and how the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila argue for momentary renewal.

Core Idea
Accidents do not persist; they are continuously renewed moment by moment.

This passage introduces a key dispute in kalam: common sense suggests accidents like colors endure, but the Ash'aris and many Mu'tazila deny this. They claim that what seems permanent is actually a rapid succession of newly created accidents. Let's examine the intuitive claim and the counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice how Iji presents the view of the general populace first, then the denial.
The passage is concise; focus on the two opposing positions.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Contrast the claim of permanence (supported by intuition) with the Ash'ari denial. Understand why the denial is counterintuitive.
The denial is not based on empirical evidence but on a theological principle.
3
Understand the two renewal mechanisms
The Ash'aris propose two ways accidents are renewed: either by re-creation after non-existence (i'ada al-ma'dum) or by immediate succession of similars (ta'aqub al-amthal). Compare them.
This distinction is key to understanding their metaphysical framework.
4
Reflect on the illusion of continuity
Consider how rapid succession can create an illusion of continuity, like a spinning firebrand appears as a circle of light. This helps explain the intuitive perception.
This analogy is not in the passage but is a common tool in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة للكل أي لجمهور الناس حتى العوام فإنهم قالوا : الأعراض كالألوان وغيرها باقية مستمرة الوجود في أزمنة متطاولة تشهد به بديهة العقل وأنكره أي بقاء الأعراض الأشعرية وكثير من المعتزلة وزعموا أنها متجددة آنا فاناً إما بإعادة المعدوم وإما بتعاقب الأمثال
The fourth one is for everyone, i.e., the majority of people, even the common people: indeed they said that accidents (al-a'rad), such as colors and others, are permanent, continuously existing in extended periods of time, witnessed by the intuition of the intellect. The Ash'aris and many of the Mu'tazila denied it, i.e., the permanence of accidents, and they claimed that they are renewed moment by moment, either by re-creation of the non-existent or by succession of similars.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth disputed claim of necessary knowledge. The general populace, including common people, claim that accidents (al-a'rad) like colors persist over time, and that this is intuitively known by the intellect. However, the Ash'aris and many Mu'tazila deny the permanence of accidents. They argue that accidents are constantly renewed at every moment. They propose two explanations: either the accident is re-created after a moment of non-existence (i'ada al-ma'dum), or a similar accident succeeds it without an intervening moment of non-existence (ta'aqub al-amthal). The intuitive perception of continuity is attributed to the rapid succession of similar accidents, similar to how a fast-moving flame appears as a circle.

Key Terms
الأعراض
باقية
متجددة آنا فاناً
إعادة المعدوم
تعاقب الأمثال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari denial of permanence contradicts obvious sensory evidence.
Correction: The Ash'aris argue that the sensory perception of continuity is an illusion caused by the rapid succession of similar accidents, not actual persistence.

Checkpoint

A) They are permanent and unchanging.
B) They are renewed every moment.
C) They exist only in the mind.
D) They are attributes of God.

Key Takeaway
Accidents do not persist; they are renewed at each instant, and apparent continuity is due to rapid succession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you take the stability of your own attributes—like your eyesight or your knowledge—for granted? What difference would it make to your gratitude if you believed they were being re-created by Allah every moment?
?

?

Third Claim: Impossibility of Sight Without Facing
Presents the third claimed necessary knowledge and the Ash'ari denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third claim of necessary knowledge regarding the impossibility of seeing without facing, and how the Ash'aris responded by affirming possibility.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge claims can be contested, as the Ash'aris show by affirming the 'impossible'.

This passage presents a third case where philosophers and Mu'tazila assert necessary knowledge of impossibility. They give extreme examples to illustrate that certain sights are intuitively impossible. However, the Ash'aris challenge even the necessity of such judgments, raising deep questions about the nature of intuitive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims of impossibility
Read the Arabic text and find the two things the philosophers claim are impossible by intuition: seeing a blind Chinese mosquito at night, and seeing an object not facing the viewer.
The second claim includes a ruling like a mirror reflection.
2
Understand the examples
Reflect on why these examples are chosen: they seem extreme or paradoxical. The first involves distance, blindness, darkness, and a tiny object; the second involves seeing something not directly in front.
3
Note the Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris affirm the possibility of these sights, thus denying that the impossibility is known necessarily. This shows that what one school considers self-evident may be contested.
This is a key point in epistemology of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالثة للحكماء ) والمعتزلة أيضاً قالوا : ( يمتنع ) بالبديهة ( رؤية أعمى الصين ) في ظلمة الليل ( بقة الأندلس و يمتنع أيضا بالبديهة ( رؤية ما لا يكون مقابلا للرائي ( أو في ( حكمه كما في رؤية الأشياء في المرآة فإنها في حكم المقابل ( وجوزه ) أي ما ذكر من الرؤيتين ( الأشعرية ) فقد كذبوهم في دعوى الامتناع فضلا عن كون العلم بالامتناع ضرورياً

Sixth Claim: Bodies Must End in Plenum or Void
Presents the sixth claimed necessary knowledge and its denial by philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth claimed necessary knowledge of the theologians regarding bodies terminating in plenum or vacuum, and the philosophers' rejection of it as a false imaginary judgment.

Core Idea
Do bodies end in emptiness or fullness? Theologians say it's evident; philosophers call it a false fantasy.

This lesson introduces a crucial point of contention between the theologians (mutakallimūn) and the philosophers. The theologians assert that it is intuitively necessary that every body terminates either in a plenum (another body) or a vacuum. The philosophers, who deny the existence of vacuum, reject this as a mere imaginary judgment. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the strong claim of necessity by the theologians and the rejection by the philosophers.
Note the term 'بالبديهة' (intuitively).
2
Identify the two positions
The theologians claim termination in plenum or void is intuitively necessary. The philosophers deny vacuum and label it a false imaginary judgment.
3
Reflect on 'wahmī'
The philosophers call it an 'imaginary' judgment, implying that the intuition is not rational but based on faulty imagination. Consider why they might say that.
This relates to the epistemology of necessary knowledge.

Source Text

The philosophers who deny vacuum reject this, labeling it a false imaginary judgment (ḥukm wahmī kādhib).

The Seventh Claim: Time's Non-Existence Requires Time
Philosophers' seventh claim: time's non-existence cannot precede it without time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the non-existence of time cannot be conceived prior to time itself without another time, and why proponents of temporal origination reject this reasoning.

Core Idea
Time's own non-existence cannot be before it without time.

The philosophers who affirm the pre-eternity of time present a logical argument: if time were originated, its non-existence would have to precede it, but that priority itself requires time. This short but dense passage introduces that argument and the opposing view. Let's examine it closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the literal translation to get the surface meaning.
2
Identify the key claim
Locate the phrase 'لا يعقل تقدم عدم الزمان عليه إلا بزمان'. This is the core claim: it is inconceivable for time's non-existence to precede it except by another time.
3
Understand the conditional argument
The passage then says 'فلو كان حادثاً مسبوقاً بعدمه لكان موجوداً حال ما كان معدوماً'. This shows the contradiction if time were originated: it would be existent while nonexistent.
4
Note the denial
The final phrase 'والقائلون بالحدوث' indicates that proponents of temporal origination deny this reasoning. Think about why they might reject it.
5
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider the argument as a reductio ad absurdum: assuming time's origination leads to a contradiction. This is a famous argument for time's pre-eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( السابعة للحكماء) القائلين بقدم الزمان قالوا : ( لا يعقل تقدم عدم الزمان عليه إلا بزمان ) فلو كان حادثاً مسبوقاً بعدمه لكان موجوداً، حال ما كان معدوماً ( والقائلون بالحدوث )
The seventh (for the philosophers who affirm the pre-eternity of time): They said: It is not conceivable that the non-existence of time precedes it except with time; for if it were originated, preceded by its non-existence, it would be existent while it was non-existent. And the proponents of temporal origination (deny it).
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh claim of necessary knowledge, attributed to the philosophers who believe time is eternal (qadīm). They argue that it is inconceivable for time's non-existence to be prior to time itself without another time; if time were originated (ḥādith), it would have been preceded by its own non-existence, which would require time to exist while it was non-existent—a contradiction. The proponents of temporal origination (qā'ilūn bi al-ḥudūth) deny this reasoning, presumably arguing that the priority is not temporal.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers deny time's creation outright without proof.
Correction: The philosophers do not simply deny creation; they argue that the very concept of time's non-existence preceding it is contradictory because that precedence would itself be temporal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the philosophers claim about the non-existence of time?
A) It can be conceived without time.
B) It cannot be conceived except with time.
C) It is eternal.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that time cannot be originated because the priority of non-existence would itself require time, leading to a contradiction.
