The Proposition of Adjacency or Separation
Proposition on existence and the muwahhidun's rejection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the division of existents into adjacent or separate is an intuitive truth or merely imaginary.

Core Idea
The muwahhidun deny that adjacency/separation is an intuitive truth.

This lesson introduces a key proposition about existence: that every existent must be either adjacent to the world or separate from it. While some claim this is known intuitively, the muwahhidun (those affirming divine unity) unanimously reject it as an imaginary judgment. Let's examine the argument and its refutation directly from the source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once with the translation. Notice the structure: a statement, a claim of intuition, a unanimous rejection.
Pay attention to the gloss explaining the logical division.
2
Understand the proposition
The proposition: every existent is either adjacent to (mulaqi) or separate from (mubayin) the world. It is said to be intuitive (badihiyya).
3
Examine the muwahhidun's rejection
The muwahhidun unanimously deny this judgment, calling it wahmi (imaginary) rather than intuitive. Consider why they might reject it.
Think about the theological implications for Allah's existence.
4
Reflect on the gloss
The gloss explains that the two categories are exhaustive—no third possibility can intervene. This reinforces the claim but does not change the muwahhidun's stance.
The denial is not about the logic of the division but about its intuitive status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل موجود إما مقارن للعالم أو مباين له، فإن البديهة تشهد بأن ما لا يختص بجهة، ولا يكون ملاقياً للعالم ولا مبايناً له، فليس بموجود. وأنكره الموحدون عن آخرهم، أي اتفقوا على إنكار هذا الحكم وتكذيبه فضلاً عن أن يكون العلم به بديهيا، وقالوا: إنه حكم وهمي. قوله: (إما مقارن للعالم أو مباين له) لأنه إنما يمكن تخلل ثالث بينهما أولاً.
Every existent is either adjacent to the world or separate from it. For intuition testifies that whatever is not specific to a direction, and is neither in contact with the world nor separate from it, is not existent. And the muwahhidun rejected it to the last of them, i.e., they agreed upon denying this judgment and falsifying it, let alone knowledge of it being intuitive, and they said: It is an imaginary judgment. He said: (Either adjacent to the world or separate from it) because a third can only possibly intervene between the two of them first.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents a proposition and its refutation. The proposition states that any existent must be either adjacent to the world (mulaqi li-l-'alam) or separate from it (mubayin lahu), and that intuition (badiha) testifies that anything lacking direction or contact with the world is non-existent. The muwahhidun (upholders of divine unity) unanimously deny this judgment, regarding it as imaginary (wahmi) rather than intuitive. The gloss explains the logical reason for the dichotomy: a third possibility cannot intervene between the two, making the division exhaustive.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The judgment that every existent is either adjacent or separate is an intuitive, universally accepted truth.
Correction: The muwahhidun unanimously reject it as an imaginary judgment (hukm wahmi), not an intuitive one.

Checkpoint

A) It is an intuitive truth.
B) It is a wahmi (imaginary) judgment.
C) It is a necessary empirical fact.
D) It is a logical axiom.

Key Takeaway
The muwahhidun deny the intuitive status of the adjacency/separation dichotomy, calling it an imaginary judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The muwahhidun rejected this judgment as 'imaginary' because they saw that accepting it would force them to attribute spatial relations to God. ?
The muwahhidun unanimously denied this judgment. Their unity came from tawhid. Is your heart aligned with theirs? Do you have the courage to reject a belief cherished by many around you if it conflicts with pure tawhid? Or do you compromise for the sake of comfort or community?

Bodies End in Plenum or Void: Intuition or Imagination?
Mutakallimun and philosophers debate on bodies ending in plenum or void

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's claim that bodies necessarily end in plenum or void, and the philosophers' objection that this is an imaginary judgment.

Core Idea
Bodies must end in either plenum or void — a necessary intuition or false imagination?

The mutakallimun who affirm the void argue that intuition demands every body terminates in either plenum or void. Philosophers reject this as a product of the imagination, not reality. This lesson unpacks their disagreement and the key terms involved.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation carefully. Notice the structure: a claim followed by two explanatory glosses.
The parentheses in the Arabic indicate added comments by later commentators.
2
Identify the core claim and the objection
The mutakallimun state: 'It is necessary by intuition that bodies end in plenum or void.' The philosophers reject this as a false imaginary judgment. Find these two positions in the text.
The term 'intuition' (badihah) is key: it means self-evident knowledge.
3
Understand the first gloss: void as imaginary dimension
The first gloss clarifies that the mutakallimun's void is not real non-existence but an imaginary dimension that a body could occupy. Philosophers consider it pure negation.
This addresses a common misunderstanding about what 'void' means in this context.
4
Understand the second gloss: distributive vs. collective whole
The second gloss explains that 'end of bodies' means each individual body ends, not all bodies together. This makes the disjunction 'plenum or void' meaningful for each body.
Without this distributive reading, the claim would be unclear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة للمتكلمين القائلين بالخلاء قالوا: (يجب) بالبديهة (انتهاء الأجسام أي انتهاء كل واحد منها إلى ملاً أو خلاء وينكره الحكماء) النافون للخلاء ويقولون: هذا من الأحكام الوهمية الكاذبة. قوله: (القائلين بالخلاء خارج العالم أي بالبعد الموهوم التي يمكن أن يشغله الجسم، كالبعد المفروض بين الجسمين والحكماء ينكرونه ويقولون: إنه نفي صرف وعدم محض يثبته الوهم، ويقدره من عند نفسه خلاف ما في نفس الأمر. قوله: (أي انتهاء كل واحد يعني أن الجمع المعرف باللام للكل الإفرادي كما هو الشائع في الاستعمال لا للكل المجموعي ليصح الحكم بالترديد.
The sixth [proposition]: For the mutakallimun who affirm the void, they said: It is necessary by intuition that bodies end—i.e., each one of them ends in a plenum or a void—and the philosophers, who deny the void, reject it and say: This is among false imaginary judgments. He said: (Those who affirm the void outside the world) i.e., [they mean] the imaginary dimension that a body can occupy, like the dimension supposed between two bodies, and the philosophers deny it and say: It is a pure negation and mere non-existence that the imagination (wahm) affirms and estimates from itself, contrary to what is in reality. He said: (i.e., the ending of each one) means that the collective noun with the definite article is for the distributive whole, as is common in usage, not for the collective whole, so that the judgment by disjunction is valid.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth disputed proposition in the chapter on necessary knowledge. The mutakallimun who affirm the void (khala') claim that intuition requires that each individual body ends either in a plenum (mala') or a void (khala'). The philosophers, who deny the void, reject this as a false imaginary judgment. The first gloss clarifies that the void they affirm is an imaginary dimension that could be occupied by a body, while philosophers consider it a pure non-entity. The second gloss explains that the phrase 'end of bodies' must be interpreted distributively (each body individually) to make the disjunction meaningful.

Key Terms

ملاء
بديهة
وهم
حكماء
متكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mutakallimun affirm a real void (empty space) as an actual existing thing.
Correction: The mutakallimun mean an imaginary dimension that a body could occupy, not a real existing void. Philosophers consider it a pure non-entity.

Checkpoint

A) Bodies are eternal.
B) Each body ends in a plenum or a void.
C) Bodies are infinitely divisible.
D) All bodies end in a plenum.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's claim that bodies end in plenum or void is based on intuition but is contested by philosophers who see it as an imaginary judgment not reflecting reality.

The Philosophers' Argument for the Pre-Eternity of Time
Philosophers on the pre-eternity of time and the response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that time must be eternal because its non-existence cannot be conceived without another time, and the counter-argument based on the internal succession of time.

Core Idea
Time's supposed beginning would itself require a prior time.

The philosophers argue that if time began, then before its existence there was a state of non-existence, but that 'before' already implies time. This leads to either infinite regress or the conclusion that time is eternal. This passage presents that reasoning and the response from proponents of temporal origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's structure become clear.
Notice the compact style: each phrase carries a logical burden.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the phrase 'لا يعقل تقدم عدم الزمان عليه إلا بزمان'. This is the philosophers' central thesis.
Understand that 'precedence of non-existence' means time's non-existence is before time itself.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction
If time were originated (hadith), it would be preceded by its own non-existence. But that 'precedence' is itself a temporal relation, implying a time when it was nonexistent. This leads to a contradiction: time would be existent and non-existent simultaneously.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the counter-argument
Read the response from those who affirm temporal origin: they counter with the fact that some parts of time precede others. This internal precedence also requires a prior time, so the philosophers' objection is not unique.
The counter shows that the philosophers' reasoning applies even if time is eternal.
5
Reflect on the gloss
The final sentence clarifies that the priority (qabliyya) discussed is a temporal priority where the prior and posterior do not coexist. Such priority is inherently temporal.
This reinforces the connection between priority and time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابعة للحكماء القائلين بقدم الزمان قالوا: (لا يعقل تقدم عدم الزمان عليه إلا بزمان) فلو كان حادثاً مسبوقاً بعدمه لكان موجوداً، حال ما كان معدوماً. والقائلون بالحدوث يكذبونهم في هذا الحكم ويعارضونهم بتقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على بعضها. قوله: (إلا بزمان) لأنها قبلية لا يجامع فيها القبل البعد، وكل قبلية كذلك فهي بالزمان.
The seventh [proposition]: For the philosophers who affirm the pre-eternity of time, they said: (The precedence of the non-existence of time over it cannot be conceived except with time). For if it were temporally originated, preceded by its non-existence, it would be existent while it was non-existent. And those who affirm temporal origin deny them this judgment and counter them with the precedence of some parts of time over others. He said: (Except with time) because it is a priority in which the prior is not accompanied by the posterior, and every such priority is by time.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proposition, advanced by philosophers who affirm the pre-eternity (qidam) of time. They argue that the non-existence of time cannot be conceived as preceding time itself except with reference to another time, implying that time must be eternal. If time were originated (hadith), it would be preceded by its own non-existence, but that would entail it being existent while non-existent—a contradiction. Those who affirm temporal origin (al-qailun bi-l-huduth) reject this and counter by pointing out that some parts of time precede others, which also requires a prior time. The gloss explains the nature of priority (qabliyya): it is a relation that does not coexist with the posterior, and such priority is inherently temporal.

Key Terms
قدم الزمان
حدوث
قبلية
عدم
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers are arguing for an infinite time without beginning.
Correction: They specifically argue that time cannot have a beginning because its non-existence would require a prior time, not that time is infinite in both directions.

Checkpoint
What is the main contention of the philosophers in the seventh proposition?
A) Time is a measure of motion.
B) Time cannot have a beginning because its non-existence would require a prior time.
C) Time is an imaginary concept.
D) Time is a substance.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument that time must be eternal hinges on the idea that any 'before' time is itself temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the moment before time began, does your mind struggle to conceive of it? How does this argument expose the limitations of human reason? Will you humble your intellect before the divine mysteries, or will you insist on logical proofs for matters beyond reason?

Will you actively re-examine your mental categories to ensure they align with revealed truth rather than philosophical assumptions?

This passage shows a direct counter-argument to a seemingly rational proof. When have you accepted a persuasive argument for your beliefs without considering counterpoints? Is there a belief you hold today that you have never critically examined from an opposing perspective? Will you take the time to seek out and honestly engage with such counter-arguments?

Man as Locus of Pain and Pleasure: Mutakallimun vs. Philosophers
Mutakallimun on man perceiving pain and pleasure directly

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the mutakallimun and philosophers on who perceives pain and pleasure and the role of the body as an instrument.

Core Idea
Pain and pleasure: perceived by man himself or by his body?

This lesson explores the tenth proposition concerning the locus of pain and pleasure. The mutakallimun maintain that man perceives them directly, while the philosophers attribute this to the body and its faculties. We'll examine their arguments and the supporting citation from al-Nihaya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice the contrast between 'mutakallimun' and 'philosophers'.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Find the positions of the mutakallimun and the philosophers. Note how each defines the locus of pain and pleasure.
The mutakallimun say man perceives by his essence; philosophers say the body and faculties perceive.
3
Understand the citation from al-Nihaya
Examine how the citation supports interpreting 'locus' as 'perceiver', aligning with the philosophers' doctrine.
The gloss explains this is to justify the author's interpretation.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this disagreement matters for understanding human nature and perceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشرة للمتكلمين قالوا: (الإنسان محل لألمه ولذته أي يدركهما بذاته) وقال الحكماء: بل محلهما ومدركهما هو الجسم والقوى الحالة فيه، وهو أي ذلك الجسم الذي حل فيه تلك القوى آلة له أي للإنسان وليس هو ذات الإنسان. قال في النهاية: اتفق المتكلمون على أن أول العلوم الضرورية. قوله: قال في النهاية إلخ استشهاد على حمل المحل في المتن على المدرك، وهذا بناء على المذهب المشهور عن الحكماء، وهو أن القوى الجسمانية مدركة بذاتها دون ما هو.
The tenth [proposition]: For the mutakallimun, they said: (Man is a locus for his pain and his pleasure, i.e., he perceives them by his essence). And the philosophers said: Rather, their locus and perceiver is the body and the faculties subsisting in it, and it—i.e., that body in which those faculties subsist—is an instrument for him, i.e., for man, and it is not the essence of man. He said in al-Nihaya: The mutakallimun agreed that the first of the necessary sciences... He said: (He said in al-Nihaya, etc.) is a citation to support interpreting 'locus' in the text as 'perceiver', and this is based on the well-known doctrine of the philosophers, which is that the bodily faculties perceive by themselves without what is...
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proposition regarding the locus of pain and pleasure. The mutakallimun claim that man himself is the locus (mahall) and perceives pain and pleasure directly by his essence (bi-dhatihi). The philosophers, on the other hand, attribute these perceptions to the body and its faculties, with the body serving as an instrument (ala) for man, not his essence. The commentary cites al-Nihaya to support interpreting 'locus' as 'perceiver', aligning with the philosophers' view that bodily faculties are themselves the perceivers. The gloss indicates that this citation is to justify the author's interpretation.

Key Terms
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both parties agree on a unified soul and the disagreement is only about the tool of perception.
Correction: The disagreement is more fundamental: mutakallimun say man himself perceives, philosophers say the body and faculties perceive, with the body as instrument.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the instrument (ala) for man in perceiving pain and pleasure?
A) The intellect.
B) The body and its faculties.
C) The soul.
D) The senses.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun and philosophers disagree on whether man directly perceives pain and pleasure or whether his body does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience intense pain, do you immediately attribute it to 'yourself' or to your body? How does your instinctive response align with either the mutakallimun or the philosophers? And what does that say about your lived understanding of who you really are — can you continue to claim the mutakallimun's position without truly feeling that pain is 'yours' in a deeply personal sense?
?
If, as the philosophers argue, your body and its faculties are merely instruments, then what becomes of your identity after death when the instrument is destroyed? Are you willing to stake your hope of an afterlife on the reality of a soul distinct from the body? Or do you, in practice, believe that 'you' are your body? Reflect on how your answer changes the way you live today.
?
The mutakallimun insist that you directly perceive pain by your very essence. If that is true, then every ache and pleasure is a direct encounter with your own soul. How would you live differently tomorrow if you truly believed that each emotional and physical sensation is a sign from your Lord speaking to your innermost self — not merely a chemical reaction? What would you change about how you respond to discomfort and desire?

Rational Judgments
beginner
5 min
Self-Evident Impossibility of Action from a Sleeper or Nonexistent
Matn and commentary on the eleventh point: Ash'ari claim and Mu'tazila response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari claim that action from a sleeper or nonexistent is self-evidently impossible and the Mu'tazila alternative of generated actions, including the example of murder during sleep.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris say it is self-evident that a sleeper or dead person cannot act voluntarily; the Mu'tazila allow generated actions.

One central disagreement between the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila concerns whether actions can proceed from a person who is asleep or dead. Ash'aris assert this is impossible by self-evident reason. The Mu'tazila, however, accept generated actions, such as a murder resulting from an arrow shot while awake but killing while the archer is asleep. This lesson clarifies the Ash'ari position and the nature of generated actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the matn
Read the matn statement: the Ash'aris assert that action from a sleeper or nonexistent is self-evidently impossible.
2
Identify the exception
Note that the commentary excludes life-necessary actions like breathing. Also, actions like turning and scratching occur in a semi-conscious state.
This shows the Ash'ari claim applies to voluntary actions only.
3
Understand generated actions
Learn the Mu'tazila concept of generated actions with the example: murder during sleep from an arrow shot while awake.
Generated actions are not voluntary.
4
Reflect on the debate
Consider why the Mu'tazila allow generated actions: because the agent cannot do or leave them, yet is still accountable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحادية عشر للأشعرية قالوا : ( يمتنع) بالبديهة (الفعل عن نائم أو معدوم وجوزه المعتزلة توليداً وجوابهما ) أي جواب الشبهة الخامسة والسادسة يعلم من جواب ) الشبهة (الرابعة) فيقال: في جواب الخامسة لا نسلم أن مقدمات الدليل الذي نجزم بصحته آونة بديهية، ولئن سلم ذلك فالبديهي قد يتطرق إليه الاشتباه لخلل في تجريد طرفيه وتعقلهما على الوجه الذي هو مناط الحكم بينهما، وذلك لا يعم جميع البديهيات كما عرفت، وفي جواب السادسة أن أصحاب المذاهب ادعوا في تلك القضايا أنها ضرورية، ولذلك أوردها الإمام الرازي في شبه السوفسطائية، فلا يلزم ادعاء البداهة بمعنى الأولية التحقيق من أن المدرك هو النفس الناطقة إلا أن ارتسام الجزئيات فهي كالصحيفة عند الناظر، ولك أن تحمل المحل على معناه الظاهر، فيكون الخلاف في أن حصول الألم واللذة الجسميين في ذات الإنسان أو البدن الذي هو آلة له على ما هو التحقيق، وإنما لم يحمل الشارح على ذلك رعاية للمطابقة لما في النهاية فإنه المنقول عنه. قوله : ( يمتنع بالبديهة الفعل عن نائم إلخ) أي غير ما يلزم الحياة كالنفس، وأما ما يصدر عنه من التقلب والحكة فليس منه في حال النوم، بل في حال بين النوم واليقظة، ولعل هذا هو مذهب بعض الأشعرية وإلا فالمصنف نص في مبحث القدرة باتفاق كثير منا على جواز صدور الأفعال المتقنة القليلة عن النائم واختلفوا في كونها مكتسبة أو ضرورية، وما قيل : أن المراد الفعل الاختياري فيرد عليه أن الفعل المولد ليس باختيار عند القائلين بالتوليد، فإن قولهم بالتوليد لأجل عدم تمكن العبد من فعله وتركه مع كونه مثاباً عليه ومعاقباً به . قوله : ( وجوزه المعتزلة توليداً كالقتل المتولد حال نوم الرامي أو موته من الرمي الصادر عنه حال اليقظة والحياة . قوله: (ضرورية) وهي أعم من البديهية بمعنى الأولية والاشتباه في الأعم لا يوجب الاشتباه في الأخص لجواز كونه في ضمن غير الأولية. قوله: (فإنها الإنسان بالحقيقة وأما عند جمهور المتكلمين فالإنسان هو هذا الهيكل المحسوس، وقد يقال : مدرك اللذة والألم عند الحكماء أيضاً هو الإنسان بواسطة الآلة، وهو قواها الجسمانية، والخلاف على هذا في الإدراك بلا واسطة أمر خارج فالمتكلمون يثبتونه والفلاسفة ينفونه . قوله : ( يمتنع الفعل) أي الاختياري إذ مطلق الفعل قد يصدر عن النائم اتفاقاً . قوله : ( في شبه (السوفسطائية وهم منكرون للبديهيات والحسيات أيضاً، فلو كان المدعي في القضايا المذكورة هو الأولية لم يفد القدح في الحسبات .
The eleventh: The Ash'aris said: 'It is self-evidently impossible that an action proceed from a sleeper or a nonexistent.' And the Mu'tazila permitted it as generated. And the answer to them both, i.e., the answer to the fifth and sixth objections, is known from the answer to the fourth objection. So it is said: In answering the fifth: we do not accept that the premises of the argument whose validity we are certain of at times are self-evident. And if that is accepted, then the self-evident can become obscure due to a flaw in isolating its two sides and conceptualizing them in the way that is the basis of the judgment between them, and that does not apply to all self-evident truths as you know. And in answering the sixth: that the proponents of the schools claimed in those propositions that they are necessary, and therefore Imam al-Razi included them among the sophistries of the Sophists, so claiming self-evidence in the sense of primary is not necessary. The verification: that the perceiver is the rational soul, except that the impression of particulars is like a page to the viewer. And you may interpret 'locus' in its apparent meaning, so the difference is whether the occurrence of bodily pain and pleasure is in the human's essence or in the body that is an instrument for him, as is the verification. And the commentator did not interpret it that way out of conformity with what is in al-Nihaya, for it is what is transmitted from it. His saying: 'It is self-evidently impossible that action [proceed] from a sleeper etc.' means other than what is necessary for life, like breath. As for what issues from him like turning and scratching, it is not of that in the state of sleep, but in a state between sleep and wakefulness. Perhaps this is the doctrine of some Ash'aris; otherwise the author stated in the discussion of capacity, by agreement of many of us, the permissibility of the occurrence of precise, few actions from a sleeper, and they differed on whether they are acquired or necessary. And what was said: that what is meant is voluntary action, then it is objected that generated action is not voluntary according to those who affirm generation, for their saying about generation is because the servant is not able to do it or leave it while being rewarded and punished for it. His saying: 'And the Mu'tazila permitted it as generation, like murder generated during the sleep of the archer or his death from the shooting that issued from him while awake and alive.' His saying: 'necessary' is more general than self-evident in the sense of primary, and obscurity in the more general does not necessitate obscurity in the more specific, because it may be within the non-primary. His saying: 'for it is the human in reality' – but according to the majority of the mutakallimun, the human is this sensible structure. And it may be said: the perceiver of pleasure and pain according to the philosophers is also the human by means of an instrument, which is his bodily powers. So the difference on this is about perception without intermediary: the mutakallimun affirm it and the philosophers deny it. His saying: 'the action is impossible' means voluntary action, for absolute action may issue from a sleeper accidentally. His saying: 'in the sophistries of the Sophists' – they are deniers of self-evident and sensory things as well; so if what is claimed in the mentioned propositions were primary, it would not be effective in discrediting the sensory.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh point on the self-evident impossibility of action from a sleeper or nonexistent, as claimed by the Ash'aris, and the Mu'tazila's permission of generated actions even from a sleeper. The commentator then provides the response to the fifth and sixth objections (based on the earlier response to the fourth). The response to the fifth argues that the premises of a supposedly self-evident argument may not actually be self-evident, and even if they were, self-evidence can be obscured due to flawed conceptual isolation; however, this does not apply to all self-evident truths. The response to the sixth notes that the claims in question are necessary (a broader category) rather than primary self-evident, citing al-Razi's inclusion of them among sophistries. There follows a digression on the tenth point's verification (the perceiver is the rational soul and the difference is in interpretation). Then the commentary explains the matn phrase about the sleeper: it excludes life-necessary actions; some actions like turning may occur in a semi-asleep state. The author himself permits precise actions from a sleeper, differing on acquisition. The Mu'tazila's generated action example is the killer asleep whose shot kills. The term 'necessary' is distinguished from 'self-evident' as broader. Finally, the commentary clarifies that the human according to philosophers is the rational soul, and the difference about immediate perception: mutakallimun affirm it, philosophers deny it. The last line addresses the Sophists' denial of self-evident and sensory knowledge.

توليد
فعل اختياري
مكتسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Ash'aris deny any action from a sleeper, including involuntary ones like breathing.
Correction: The commentary explicitly excludes life-necessary actions like breathing. Also, turning and scratching are considered to occur in a semi-conscious state, not deep sleep.

Checkpoint
What does the Ash'ari claim about actions from a sleeper apply to?
A) All actions without exception
B) Only voluntary actions
C) Only actions that are life-necessary
D) Only generated actions

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris hold that a sleeper or dead person cannot perform voluntary actions, a self-evident truth; the Mu'tazila allow generated actions as exceptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a habit you have that feels automatic, almost like you are asleep while doing it. According to the Ash'ari stance, such actions are not voluntary from the sleeper. But the Mu'tazila hold you accountable for generated actions. Which of your 'automatic' actions are you truly responsible for? Will you audit them today?
?
You often find yourself in a spiritual 'slumber', going through motions without awareness. What actions are you still performing? How will you stop being a 'sleeper' in your deeds so that every act becomes a voluntary submission to Allah?

Rational Judgments

Epistemological Defenses: How Self-Evidence Can Be Obscured
Answers to fifth and sixth objections: explaining obscurity of self-evidence and distinguishing necessary from primary self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to objections that self-evident truths can be obscured and that some claimed necessary propositions are not primary self-evident, distinguishing between self-evident (بديهي) and necessary (ضروري).

Core Idea
Self-evident truths can become obscure due to flawed conceptualization, and 'necessary' is broader than 'primary self-evident'.

In defending the claim that action from a sleeper is self-evidently impossible, Ash'aris had to address objections that self-evident truths can be mistaken. This lesson presents the two answers: first, that even if premises appear self-evident, obscurity can arise from flawed isolation of terms; second, that the opponents' claimed necessary propositions are not primary self-evident but belong to a broader category, as illustrated by al-Razi's inclusion among sophistries.

Guided Path
1
Read the response to the fifth objection
The argument: even if premises are self-evident, obscurity can occur due to a flaw in isolating the terms.
2
Note the limitation
This obscurity does not apply to all self-evident truths.
3
Read the response to the sixth objection
The opponents claim necessity, not primary self-evidence. Al-Razi included them among sophistries.
4
Learn the distinction
Necessary (ضروري) is more general than self-evident (بديهي) in the primary sense. Obscurity in the broader does not entail obscurity in the narrower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The eleventh: The Ash'aris said: 'It is self-evidently impossible that an action proceed from a sleeper or a nonexistent.' And the Mu'tazila permitted it as generated. And the answer to them both, i.e., the answer to the fifth and sixth objections, is known from the answer to the fourth objection. So it is said: In answering the fifth: we do not accept that the premises of the argument whose validity we are certain of at times are self-evident. And if that is accepted, then the self-evident can become obscure due to a flaw in isolating its two sides and conceptualizing them in the way that is the basis of the judgment between them, and that does not apply to all self-evident truths as you know. And in answering the sixth: that the proponents of the schools claimed in those propositions that they are necessary, and therefore Imam al-Razi included them among the sophistries of the Sophists, so claiming self-evidence in the sense of primary is not necessary. The verification: that the perceiver is the rational soul, except that the impression of particulars is like a page to the viewer. And you may interpret 'locus' in its apparent meaning, so the difference is whether the occurrence of bodily pain and pleasure is in the human's essence or in the body that is an instrument for him, as is the verification. And the commentator did not interpret it that way out of conformity with what is in al-Nihaya, for it is what is transmitted from it. His saying: 'It is self-evidently impossible that action [proceed] from a sleeper etc.' means other than what is necessary for life, like breath. As for what issues from him like turning and scratching, it is not of that in the state of sleep, but in a state between sleep and wakefulness. Perhaps this is the doctrine of some Ash'aris; otherwise the author stated in the discussion of capacity, by agreement of many of us, the permissibility of the occurrence of precise, few actions from a sleeper, and they differed on whether they are acquired or necessary. His saying: 'And the Mu'tazila permitted it as generation, like murder generated during the sleep of the archer or his death from the shooting that issued from him while awake and alive.' His saying: 'necessary' is more general than self-evident in the sense of primary, and obscurity in the more general does not necessitate obscurity in the more specific, because it may be within the non-primary. His saying: 'for it is the human in reality' – but according to the majority of the mutakallimun, the human is this sensible structure. So the difference on this is about perception without intermediary: the mutakallimun affirm it and the philosophers deny it. The commentator then provides the response to the fifth and sixth objections (based on the earlier response to the fourth). Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, why does obscurity in the broader category of 'necessary' not affect the narrower 'self-evident'?
A) Because all necessary propositions are self-evident
B) Because obscurity in the broader may be in non-primary self-evidents
C) Because self-evident truths are always clear
D) Because the philosophers deny self-evidence

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris defend self-evident truths by admitting occasional obscurity due to flawed analysis and by distinguishing primary self-evidence from broader necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a clear truth about Allah or your faith because a doubt made it seem obscure? What would it mean to trust that the foundational truths are clear even when some details feel unclear? Will you commit to re-examining that doubt in light of this lesson?
?
When you encounter an objection to an essential belief, do you assume that the truth must be immediately obvious to everyone? How does this lesson’s distinction between primary self-evidence and broader necessity challenge your expectation? Are you willing to accept that some truths require careful conceptualization without being any less certain?
?
Consider a time you dismissed a religious truth as 'not obvious' and used that as an excuse to ignore it. How does the Ash'ari defense of self-evident truths—that obscurity can arise from flawed analysis—hold you accountable? Will you now commit to examining your own assumptions before rejecting a truth?

The Rational Soul and the Locus of Perception
Defines the human as rational soul and clarifies perception locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of the human as the rational soul and how they explain perception of pain and pleasure through bodily powers.

Core Idea
The true human is the rational soul, not the visible body.

In this passage, the text delves into the nature of the human being and the locus of sensory perception. The philosophers argue that the perceiver of pain and pleasure is not the human essence itself but the bodily powers, which are appendages of the rational soul. The passage also clarifies the Ash'ari position and the debate about direct perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style and technical terms like النفس الناطقة.
2
Identify the philosophers' definition of man
The passage states that for philosophers, the true human is the rational soul (النفس الناطقة). Note how this differs from the common view that man is the sensible body.
The phrase فإنها الإنسان بالحقيقة is key.
3
Understand the locus of perception
The philosophers hold that the perceiver of pain, pleasure, etc. is not the human essence but the bodily powers (قواها الجسمانية), which are instruments of the soul. This leads to the verification that the soul is the perceiver via these powers.
The passage uses the metaphor of a page for the viewer.
4
Examine the disagreement about direct perception
The passage concludes by contrasting the theologians and philosophers: theologians affirm perception without a medium, while philosophers deny it. Recognize this as a key point of contention.
This disagreement is about whether the soul can perceive directly or only through bodily instruments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علم الإنسان بنفسه وألمه ولذته وجوعه وعطشه واتفقت الفلاسفة على أن مدرك الألم واللذة والجوع والعطش ليس ذات الإنسان بل قواها الجسمانية التي هي من توابع ذاته التي هي النفس الناطقة فإنها الإنسان بالحقيقة ( الحادية عشر للأشعرية ) . التحقيق من أن المدرك هو النفس الناطقة إلا أن ارتسام الجزئيات فهي كالصحيفة عند الناظر، ولك أن تحمل المحل على معناه الظاهر، فيكون الخلاف في أن حصول الألم واللذة الجسميين في ذات الإنسان أو البدن الذي هو آلة له على ما هو التحقيق، وإنما لم يحمل الشارح على ذلك رعاية للمطابقة لما في النهاية فإنه المنقول عنه. قوله: (فإنها الإنسان بالحقيقة وأما عند جمهور المتكلمين فالإنسان هو هذا الهيكل المحسوس، وقد يقال : مدرك اللذة والألم عند الحكماء أيضاً هو الإنسان بواسطة الآلة، وهو قواها الجسمانية، والخلاف على هذا في الإدراك بلا واسطة أمر خارج فالمتكلمون يثبتونه والفلاسفة ينفونه.
Man knows himself and his pain, pleasure, hunger, and thirst. The philosophers agreed that the perceiver of pain, pleasure, hunger, and thirst is not the essence of man, but rather his bodily powers, which are among the appendages of his essence, which is the rational soul, for it is man in reality (the eleventh [opinion] of the Ash'aris). The verification is that the perceiver is the rational soul, except that the impression of particulars is like a page for the viewer. You may interpret the 'place' in its apparent meaning, so the disagreement is whether bodily pain and pleasure occur in the essence of man or in the body, which is his instrument, as is the verification. The commentator did not interpret that, out of conformity with what is in al-Nihaya, for it is what is transmitted from him. His statement: 'For it is man in reality, but according to the majority of theologians, man is this sensible structure. It may be said: The perceiver of pleasure and pain according to the philosophers is also man by means of the instrument, which is his bodily powers. The disagreement on this is about perception without a medium, which is external; the theologians affirm it and the philosophers deny it.'
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines the human being according to the philosophers and clarifies the locus of sensory perception. It begins by stating that man has self-knowledge and awareness of pain, pleasure, hunger, and thirst. The philosophers agree that the perceiver of these sensations is not the human essence itself but rather the bodily powers, which are appendages of the essence. The true human is the rational soul (النفس الناطقة). The parenthetical note ('the eleventh [opinion] of the Ash'aris') likely indicates that this is the Ash'ari position on the issue. Next, the commentator provides the verification: the perceiver is indeed the rational soul, but the impression of particulars is like a page for a viewer—the soul acts as a pure recipient. One may interpret 'place' (المحل) in its apparent meaning, so the disagreement is whether bodily pain and pleasure occur in the essence of man or in the body as an instrument. The commentator notes that the original commentator (الشارح) did not adopt this interpretation to match what is in al-Nihaya. Finally, the commentator expands: 'His statement: For it is man in reality'—but according to the majority of theologians, man is this sensible structure (الهيكل المحسوس). It may be said that even the philosophers consider man to perceive pleasure and pain through an instrument (the bodily powers). The real disagreement is about perception without a medium: theologians affirm it (the soul perceives directly) while philosophers deny it (all perception is through bodily instruments).

Correction: In fact, philosophers affirm that the soul perceives, but through the instrument of bodily powers, not directly.

Impossibility of Action from a Sleeper and the Mu'tazila's Objection of Generation
Ash'ari claim and defense against Mu'tazila objection of generation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that voluntary action is intuitively impossible from a sleeper or dead person, and how they respond to the Mu'tazila's objection of generation.

Core Idea
Voluntary action from a sleeper is impossible by intuition — generation does not make it voluntary.

In this passage, the author presents a key Ash'ari claim: that it is self-evidently impossible for a sleeper or a dead person to perform a voluntary action. The Mu'tazila object that such actions can occur through ‘generation’ (tawlīd), as in a killing resulting from an arrow shot by an archer who later falls asleep. The Ash'ari response defends the intuitive basis of their claim and clarifies that generated actions are not voluntary even for the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari claim
Read the opening statement that action is impossible from a sleeper by intuition, and note the types of actions excluded (e.g., involuntary bodily functions).
The term 'intuition' (badīha) refers to self-evident knowledge.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila objection
See how the Mu'tazila allow such actions through generation, illustrated with the archer example: a killing can occur from an arrow shot while awake, even if the archer later sleeps or dies.
Generation (tawlīd) is the idea that an action can cause an effect indirectly.
3
Understand the Ash'ari defense
Trace how the answers to the fifth and sixth objections rely on the fourth. The fifth objection challenges the intuition of the premises; the sixth involves claiming necessity rather than primary intuition. The commentator clarifies distinctions between voluntary and involuntary actions.
The distinction between 'necessary' (ḍarūriyya) and 'primary' (awwaliyya) is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : ( يمتنع) بالبديهة (الفعل عن نائم أو معدوم وجوزه المعتزلة توليداً وجوابهما ) أي جواب الشبهة الخامسة والسادسة يعلم من جواب ) الشبهة (الرابعة) فيقال: في جواب الخامسة لا نسلم أن مقدمات الدليل الذي نجزم بصحته آونة بديهية، ولئن سلم ذلك فالبديهي قد يتطرق إليه الاشتباه لخلل في تجريد طرفيه وتعقلهما على الوجه الذي هو مناط الحكم بينهما، وذلك لا يعم جميع البديهيات كما عرفت، وفي جواب السادسة أن أصحاب المذاهب ادعوا في تلك القضايا أنها ضرورية، ولذلك أوردها الإمام الرازي في شبه السوفسطائية، فلا يلزم ادعاء البداهة بمعنى الأولية. قوله : ( يمتنع بالبديهة الفعل عن نائم إلخ) أي غير ما يلزم الحياة كالنفس، وأما ما يصدر عنه من التقلب والحكة فليس منه في حال النوم، بل في حال بين النوم واليقظة، ولعل هذا هو مذهب بعض الأشعرية وإلا فالمصنف نص في مبحث القدرة باتفاق كثير منا على جواز صدور الأفعال المتقنة القليلة عن النائم واختلفوا في كونها مكتسبة أو ضرورية، وما قيل : أن المراد الفعل الاختياري فيرد عليه أن الفعل المولد ليس باختيار عند القائلين بالتوليد، فإن قولهم بالتوليد لأجل عدم تمكن العبد من فعله وتركه مع كونه مثاباً عليه ومعاقباً به . قوله : ( وجوزه المعتزلة توليداً كالقتل المتولد حال نوم الرامي أو موته من الرمي الصادر عنه حال اليقظة والحياة . قوله: (ضرورية) وهي أعم من البديهية بمعنى الأولية والاشتباه في الأعم لا يوجب الاشتباه في الأخص لجواز كونه في ضمن غير الأولية. قوله : ( يمتنع الفعل) أي الاختياري إذ مطلق الفعل قد يصدر عن النائم اتفاقاً . قوله : ( في شبه (السوفسطائية وهم منكرون للبديهيات والحسيات أيضاً، فلو كان المدعي في القضايا المذكورة هو الأولية لم يفد القدح في الحسبات .
They said: 'Action is impossible by intuition from a sleeper or a dead person, and the Mu'tazila allowed it as generation (tawlīd), and the answer to both [objections]—that is, the answer to the fifth and sixth objections—is known from the answer to the fourth objection. So it is said: In answer to the fifth, we do not concede that the premises of the argument whose correctness we judge at times are intuitive. And if that is conceded, then the intuitive may be subject to confusion due to a defect in stripping its two sides and understanding them in the manner that is the criterion of judgment between them, and that does not apply to all intuitives (badīhiyyāt), as you know. And in answer to the sixth, that the proponents of the schools claimed that those propositions are necessary (ḍarūriyya), and that is why Imam al-Razi included them among the sophistries, so it is not necessary to claim that they are primary (awwaliyya). His statement: 'Action is impossible by intuition from a sleeper, etc.'—that is, other than what is necessary for life like breathing. As for what issues from him like tossing and scratching, that is not from him in the state of sleep, but in a state between sleep and wakefulness. Perhaps this is the school of some Ash'aris; otherwise, the author stated in the discussion of power (qudra) that many of us agree on the permissibility of the occurrence of precise few actions from a sleeper, and they differed on whether they are acquired (muktasaba) or necessary (ḍarūriyya). It was said that what is meant is voluntary action (fiʿl ikhtiyārī), but it is objected that the generated act (al-fiʿl al-muwallad) is not voluntary according to the proponents of generation, because their saying about generation is due to the servant not being able to do it and leave it, while being rewarded and punished for it. His statement: 'And the Mu'tazila allowed it as generation, like the killing generated during the sleep of the archer or his death from the shooting that occurred from him while awake and alive.' His statement: 'Necessary (ḍarūriyya)—and it is more general than the intuitive (badīhiyya) in the sense of primary (awwaliyya), and confusion in the more general does not necessitate confusion in the more specific, because it is possible that it is within the non-primary. His statement: 'Action is impossible'—that is, voluntary action, for absolute action may occur from a sleeper occasionally. His statement: 'In the sophistries (al-sūfastāʾiyya)—and they are deniers of intuitives and sense perceptions as well, so if the claimant in those propositions claimed they were primary, it would not refute the sense perceptions.'
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents a key Ash'ari claim: that it is intuitively impossible for a sleeper or a dead person to perform an action. The Mu'tazila countered by allowing such actions as 'generated' (مولد, tawlīd)—for example, a killing that occurs from an archer's arrow after he falls asleep or dies, but the action (the shooting) originated while he was awake and alive. The text then gives the answer to the fifth and sixth objections, referring back to the answer to the fourth. For the fifth objection: the premises of a proof we deem certain are not always intuitive; even if they were, intuition can be mistaken due to a defect in understanding the terms. For the sixth objection: the proponents claimed these propositions are necessary (ضرورية), not primary (أولية) intuitives. The commentator then clarifies: the intuitive impossibility applies to voluntary actions, not to involuntary bodily functions. Some Ash'aris hold that a sleeper can perform precise actions (like turning over) in a borderline state. The generated act is not voluntary for the Mu'tazila themselves, since they claim the agent cannot refrain from it. The passage also distinguishes 'necessary' (ضرورية) from 'primary' (بديهية بمعنى الأولية), noting that the former is more general, so confusion in the general does not imply confusion in the specific. Finally, it mentions the Sophists, who deny all intuitives and sense perceptions, so claiming these propositions are primary would not refute the sense-based objections.

Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila consider generated acts voluntary.
Correction: The text clarifies that the Mu'tazila do not consider generated acts voluntary because the agent cannot refrain from them, yet they still allow reward and punishment.

Checkpoint

B) Voluntary action
C) Necessary bodily functions

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument that voluntary action is impossible from a sleeper by intuition is defended against the Mu'tazila objection of generation by clarifying the intuitive, necessary, and primary propositions.

?

?

?
The Mu'tazila allowed generated actions to be rewardable despite lacking volition, highlighting a tension between moral accountability and involuntary acts. Consider a habit you perform reflexively—like a harsh word when angry. Is it truly involuntary? According to this lesson, what makes an action 'yours' to be answerable for? Before your next reflexive reaction, commit to asking: 'Is this action mine to control?'

Answer to the Sixth Objection: Intellect vs. Estimative Faculty
Answers the sixth objection by distinguishing intellect and estimative faculty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between the necessity of the intellect and the necessity of the estimative faculty, and how this distinction refutes the sixth objection against the self-evidence of primary propositions.

Core Idea
The estimative faculty judges falsely; only the intellect’s necessity is absolutely reliable.

The sixth objection challenged the self-evidence of certain propositions by claiming that they are not universally accepted. The answer does not reject the possibility of error but distinguishes two sources of immediate certainty: the intellect and the estimative faculty. This lesson explains that distinction and shows why the intellect’s judgments are trustworthy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument. Note the terms بديهة العقل (intellect) and بديهة الوهم (estimative faculty).
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage contrasts the necessity of the intellect (truthful) with that of the estimative faculty (false). Understand the example given (dead body, inanimate, fear).
The example shows how the estimative faculty leads to contradictory judgments.
3
Understand the refutation
The sixth objection is answered by showing that apparent contradictions arise from the estimative faculty, not the intellect. The intellect's necessity remains unassailable.
4
Examine the dialectical exchange
The passage includes an objection and response that further clarify the argument. Notice how the response depends on the proof that distinguishes the two faculties.

The Fourth Observatory—On Establishing the Necessary Sciences. [It] does not apply universally: we concede, but the primary [intuitive] may have a defect in conceiving its two sides, as in the primary intuitives. And it was answered about it—that is, about the last objection, namely the sixth—that the one who judges them—that is, those propositions whose self-evidence the proponents of the schools claimed—[judges] by the necessity of the estimative faculty (waqm) not the necessity of the intellect. And it—that is, the necessity of the estimative faculty—is false, no reliance on its judgments, because it judges by what produces their contradictories—that is, the contradictories of the judgments arising from it. For it judges that the dead is inanimate, and that the inanimate is not feared, and these two produce the opposite of what it judged, namely that the dead is feared—unlike the necessity of the intellect, for it is absolutely true. It may be said: He meant that the necessity of the estimative faculty judges by what produces the contradictories of these propositions which it affirmed. We say: Then the affirmation of them—that is, of the intuitives and their correctness—is dependent on this proof by which the falsity of the necessity of the estimative faculty appears, for by it the necessity [of the intellect] is distinguished. His statement: 'In the sophistries (al-sūfastāʾiyya)'—those who deny necessary sciences absolutely. Those propositions, if they were not from the primary intuitives, the confusion in them would confirm their claim, which is the lack of certainty in necessary sciences absolutely. His statement: 'That is, about the last [objection]'—by this interpretation he pointed to the clue that the pronoun refers to the sixth objection, and that is that the pronoun refers to the nearest mentioned. His statement: 'And it—that is, the estimative necessity—is false'—meaning in general. His statement: 'Because it judges produces, etc.'—the premises judge by the premises that produce the opposite of what it judged, so it is false in one of the two judgments, so there is no reliance on its judgments absolutely, for there is no testimony for the accused. His statement: 'And it may be said, etc.'—according to the previous interpretation, the pronoun of 'their contradictories' refers to the estimative faculty by a slight connection, or by deleting the genitive (i.e., its judgments). Unlike this interpretation, it refers to what the pronoun 'them' referred to, namely the mentioned propositions. The first is more apparent in meaning, because the claim that the estimative faculty judges in those propositions by what produces their contradictories is far-fetched. Know that it has been imagined that this answer repels the third, fourth, and fifth objections as well, so there is no reason to specify it for the sixth. But it is nothing, because the essence of the third is the possibility that certainty in the primaries arises from temperament or universal habit, so it is not certain like propositions arising from specific temperament and habit. So repelling it requires proving that temperament and habit have no role in the primaries. The essence of the fourth is that the intuitive certainty of the correctness of the premises of two decisive proofs that are contradictory with all... His statement: 'That is, about the last objection, I mean the sixth. It was said: This answer can be an answer to the third objection by saying: The estimative faculty, due to temperaments and habits, caused certainty in some propositions. And to the fourth by saying: The contradiction occurred between the estimative and intellectual intuitives, and in first sight they seemed both decisive. And to the fifth by saying: The certainty about a premise and proof at the time was according to the estimative faculty, not the intellect, so he thought it was by the intellect's intuition, but it is not so, and therefore his error appeared. And to the sixth as the commentator stated it, so there is no reason to specify it as an answer to the sixth objection.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage provides a specific answer to the sixth objection, which claimed that certain propositions (like 'a sleeper cannot act') are self-evident. The answer distinguishes between two types of necessity: the necessity of the intellect (بديهة العقل) and the necessity of the estimative faculty (بديهة الوهم). The latter is false, because it judges by premises that produce contradictory conclusions. For example, the estimative faculty judges that a dead body is inanimate and that an inanimate object is not feared; yet in some contexts, the dead are feared. This contradiction shows that the estimative faculty's judgments are unreliable. In contrast, the intellect's necessity is absolutely true. The answer then states that certainty about such intuitive propositions depends on a proof that distinguishes the intellect's necessity from the estimative faculty's. The commentator clarifies that the pronoun 'their' in 'their contradictories' refers to the propositions themselves, not the estimative faculty. He also addresses a potential misconception: that this answer could also refute the third, fourth, and fifth objections. He explains why it does not: each objection has a distinct essence. The third concerns the role of temperament and habit in generating certainty; the fourth concerns contradictory proofs; the fifth concerns the assumption that a premise is intuitive. The answer to the sixth is specifically about distinguishing between the estimative faculty and the intellect. The commentator then notes that some have said this answer could apply to the third, fourth, and fifth as well, but he argues it is not the case, though he does not fully elaborate. The passage ends with a summary of how this answer could be extended to the other objections, but concludes that it is specifically tailored to the sixth.

Misconception: That this answer refutes all objections (third, fourth, fifth, and sixth).
Correction: The answer is specifically for the sixth objection; each objection has a unique refutation.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the sixth objection, what type of necessity is considered false?
A) The necessity of the intellect
B) The necessity of the estimative faculty
C) The necessity of the senses
D) The necessity of habit

Key Takeaway
The necessity of the estimative faculty is unreliable; only the intellect's necessity provides absolute certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you relied on an initial gut feeling (estimative judgment) that later turned out to be contradictory or false? How did you realize your error, and how could you have used your intellect to avoid it?
?
The passage teaches that only the intellect's necessity provides absolute certainty. In your daily life, are there areas where you trust your immediate impressions over careful reasoning? What change will you make to prioritize intellectual certainty over estimative guesses?
?
If the necessity of the estimative faculty is unreliable, how might this affect your understanding of common 'self-evident' truths you hold? Which of your current beliefs might actually be based on estimative judgments rather than intellectual proof?

Distinguishing Wahmi from Aqli Intuition
Distinguishing wahmi from aqli intuition and showing wahmi's falsity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between wahmi (imaginative) intuition and aqli (intellectual) intuition, and why wahmi intuition is unreliable.

Core Idea
Imaginative intuition contradicts itself; intellectual intuition never does.

In responding to the sixth doubt about self-evident knowledge, the commentator draws a crucial distinction between two types of intuition. The first, called badiha al-wahm, comes from the imagination and can produce contradictory judgments. The second, badiha al-aql, comes from the intellect and is always reliable. This lesson introduces that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the example given.
2
Identify the two types of intuition
The passage explicitly names two intuitions: badiha al-wahm and badiha al-aql. Note the characteristics attributed to each.
3
Understand why wahmi intuition is false
The commentator provides an example: the dead is judged to be inanimate and not feared, yet also feared. This contradiction shows unreliability.
Contrast with aqli intuition, which is always true.

The Fourth Observation Post - On Proving Necessary Knowledge. (And it was answered about it), i.e., about the last doubt, meaning the sixth, (that the one who is certain of them), i.e., those propositions which the proponents of the schools claimed to be self-evident, (is the intuition of the imagination (badiha al-wahm), not the intuition of the intellect (badiha al-aql). (And it), i.e., the intuition of the imagination, (is false), no reliance on its judgments, (for it judges by what yields their contradictories), i.e., the contradictories of the judgments issued from it, for it judges that the dead is inanimate, and that the inanimate is not feared, and both yield the contradictory of what it judged, namely that the dead is feared, unlike the intuition of the intellect, for it is absolutely true. And it may be said: He meant that the intuition of the imagination judges by what yields the contradictories of these propositions which it was certain of. We said: Then certainty of them), i.e., of the self-evident propositions and their correctness, (depends on this proof by which the falsity of the intuition of the imagination is shown, since thereby the intuition is distinguished). His saying: (concerning the sophistical doubt) of those who deny necessary knowledge absolutely, for those propositions if they were not among the primary ones, then the confusion in them would prove their claim, which is the lack of reliance on necessary knowledge absolutely. His saying: (i.e., about the last doubt) he pointed by this interpretation to the presumption that the pronoun refers to the sixth doubt, and that is that the pronoun refers to the nearest of the mentioned things. His saying: (and it is false) i.e., in sum. His saying: (for it judges by yielding etc.) the premises judge by the premises that yield the contradictory of what it judged, so it is false in one of the two judgments, so there is no reliance on its judgments absolutely, for there is no testimony for an accused. His saying: (and it may be said: etc.) according to the previous direction, the pronoun of its contradictories refers to the intuition of the imagination with the slightest connection, or by deleting the genitive i.e., its judgments, unlike this direction, for in it it refers to what the pronoun of 'with it' referred to, namely the mentioned propositions, and the first is more apparent in meaning because the claim that the intuition of the imagination judges in those propositions by what yields their contradictories is forced. And know that it has been imagined that this answer repels the third, fourth, and fifth doubts as well, so there is no reason for specifying the sixth, and it is nothing, because the essence of the third is the possibility that certainty in the self-evident propositions arises from a temperament or a common habit, so they are not certain like propositions arising from a specific temperament and habit, so repelling it requires proving that temperament and habit have no influence in the self-evident propositions. And the essence of the fourth is that certainty by intuition in the correctness of the premises of both decisive conflicting proofs with all. His saying: (i.e., about the last doubt, meaning the sixth) it was said that this answer is suitable to be an answer to the third doubt by saying: the imagination, due to temperaments and habits, caused certainty in some propositions, and to the fourth by saying: the conflict only occurred between the imaginative and intellectual self-evident propositions, so it seemed at first glance that both are categorical, and to the fifth by saying: the certainty of a premise and a proof at the time was only according to the imagination, not the intellect, so he thought it was by the intuition of the intellect and it is not so, and that is why his error appeared, and to the sixth as the commentator explained, so there is no self-evident reason to specify it as an answer to the sixth doubt. The intellect yields from it (so it circles), i.e., circularity occurs, because this proof depends on the correctness of the self-evident propositions used in it. (And also) if confidence in the certainty of a self-evident proposition depends on its not being certain by what yields its contradictory, since if it were also certain of it, then that proposition would be among the imaginative judgments which have no reliability, (then the reliable certainty would not be obtained) in a self-evident proposition (as long as we are not certain that there is no contradictory of it), i.e., as long as we are not certain that that self-evident proposition is not among the things of which the intuition has certainties that yield its contradictory. (And) that is something which (cannot be certain; rather its utmost is non-finding) with thorough investigation, and that does not indicate non-existence with a categorical indication.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a response to the sixth doubt concerning self-evident knowledge (badihiyyat). The commentator explains that the judgment in those disputed cases comes from the intuition of the imagination (badiha al-wahm), not the intuition of the intellect (badiha al-aql). Wahmi intuition is false because it produces contradictory judgments: e.g., it judges that the dead is an inanimate object, and that an inanimate object is not feared, yet also that the dead is feared. This contradiction shows its unreliability. In contrast, intellectual intuition is always true. The passage then addresses an objection: if wahmi intuition judges by premises that yield contradictories of the propositions it affirms, then certainty in those propositions depends on a proof showing the falsity of wahmi intuition, leading to circularity (dawr). The commentator elaborates that this circularity arises because the proof itself relies on the self-evident propositions it is trying to justify. Furthermore, confidence in any self-evident proposition would require certainty that no contradictory judgment exists from wahmi intuition, which is impossible to achieve with certainty—the most one can do is not find such a contradictory after thorough investigation, which is not a categorical proof of absence.

Key Terms
بديهة الوهم
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think all intuitive judgments are equally reliable.
Correction: The commentator shows that imaginative intuition can be contradictory, whereas intellectual intuition is always true.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which type of intuition is unreliable?
A) Badiha al-aql
B) Badiha al-wahm
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Imaginative intuition (badiha al-wahm) is unreliable because it yields contradictory judgments, unlike intellectual intuition (badiha al-aql) which is always true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which intuitions in your daily life might actually be from imagination rather than intellect? How would you test them using the contradiction criterion from this lesson?
?
The dead are not to be feared; only Allah is to be feared. Is your heart truly free from fear of the deceased? If not, what does that reveal about the source of your fear?
?
Are you willing to abandon comforting but contradictory beliefs in favor of intellectual certainty? What would that cost you personally?

Rational Judgments
intermediate

The Circularity Objection in Proving Self-Evident Knowledge
Addressing the circularity objection in proving the falsity of wahmi intuition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circularity objection raised against the proof that distinguishes wahmi from aqli intuition, and how the commentator addresses it.

Core Idea
Proving wahmi intuition false leads to circularity because the proof itself relies on self-evident propositions.

After establishing that wahmi intuition is false because it produces contradictions, an objection is raised: this very proof depends on the self-evident propositions it is trying to justify, leading to circular reasoning. The commentator acknowledges the problem and discusses its implications. This lesson examines that objection and the commentator's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Start by reading the passage that presents the objection and the commentator's initial reply. Notice the structure: 'It may be said... We said...'
The objection is about circularity.
2
Identify the circularity (dawr)
The commentator states that the proof of truth of self-evident propositions depends on those same propositions, causing a logical circle.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
The passage discusses two ways to understand the pronoun reference, affecting whether circularity is immediate.
The first interpretation is forced; the second avoids circularity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion about certainty
Even if circularity is avoided, achieving certainty about self-evident propositions requires verification of no contradictory wahmi judgment, which is impossible. The best is non-finding, not proof of absence.

B) The argument goes in a circle of logical steps.
C) The intuition of the intellect is circular.
D) The answer to the doubt is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The attempt to prove the falsity of wahmi intuition leads to circularity, and achieving certainty about self-evident propositions requires an unattainable verification that no contradictory wahmi judgment exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What are the most basic beliefs you hold that you consider self-evident? Is there any hidden assumption or potential error you have never examined?
?
If you discovered that one of your fundamental certainties might be based on a circular justification, would you be willing to abandon it? How would you rebuild it?
?
This lesson shows the difficulty of proving that your intuitive judgments are always reliable. Where in your life do you trust your immediate reactions without scrutiny, and what might that cost you?
