The Comprehensive Answer to the Six Doubts
Commentator's unified answer to six doubts about necessary knowledge, distinguishing between the first five and the sixth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the same answer addresses all six doubts about necessary knowledge and why the first five require more rigorous proof than the sixth.

Core Idea
A single answer refutes all six doubts—but only after distinguishing intellectual from imaginative certainty.

In this passage, the commentator provides a unified response to the six doubts against necessary knowledge. He explains that their premises are not sensory but either self-evident or theoretical. He then highlights a crucial difference: the first five doubts require proving that the certainty involved arises from imagination (wahm), a very difficult task, while the sixth can be dismissed by merely suggesting that possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the comprehensive answer
Read the Arabic passage from 'وقد أجيب' to 'كما لا يخفى'. Focus on the main claim that the premises are not sensory and the distinction between the first five and the sixth.
Notice the phrase 'خرط القتاد' which indicates great difficulty.
2
Identify the two types of premises
The commentator says the premises are either badihiyyat (self-evident) or nazariyyat (theoretical) based on badihiyyat. Understand why they are not sensory.
3
Understand the different treatment of the first five vs. the sixth
The first five require proving that certainty comes from wahm, which is extremely difficult. The sixth only needs the mere possibility that the judge is wahm. Reflect on why this is so.
This distinction shows the depth of the commentator's argument.
4
Reflect on the key takeaway
Consider how this answer protects the reliability of self-evident knowledge while acknowledging the role of imagination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجيب عن الشبه الست كلها بأن المقدمات المذكورة فيها ليست قضايا حسية فهي إما بديهيات أو نظريات مستندة إلى بديهيات، فلو جميعها كون أحداهما خطأ يوجب رفع الوثوق من جميع البديهيات لجواز أن يكون الجزم في كلها من هذا القبيل، فلا بد في دفعها من إثبات أن الجزم في تلك الصورة ناشئ من بديهة الوهم، وخلاصة الشبهة الخامسة أن ظهور خطأ دليل جزم بصحة مقدماته بديهة آونة يوجب رفع الوثوق عن البديهيات لجواز ظهور خطئها بعد أزمنة متطاولة، فلا بد في دفعها من إثبات أن ذلك الجزم ناشئ من بديهة الوهم وهو لا يوجب رفع الوثوق عن بديهية العقل، ولا شك أن ذينك الإثباتين دونهما خرط القتاد بخلاف السادسة فإنه يكفى في دفعها مجرد جواز أن يكون الحاكم تلك القضايا بديهة الوهم كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( أي يلزم الدور) إشارة إلى أن الفعل مسند إلى المصدر كما في قولهم لقدحيل بين العير والنزوان . قوله : ( يتوقف على صحة البديهيات (الخ فلا بد من الجزم بصحتها، فيلزم توقف الجزم بصحة البديهيات مطلقاً على الجزم بصحة هذه البديهيات وهو موقوف على الجزم بصحتها مطلقاً، هذا إذا أريد بالدور معناه الحقيقي، وإن إريد به توقف الشيء على نفسه، نقول : فيلزم توقف الجزم بهذه البديهيات على الجزم بها لكونها من جملة البديهيات . قوله : ( وأيضاً إذا توقف إلخ) وروده على تقدير قد يقال : ظاهر وأما على تقرير الشارح ففيه بحث لأنه حينئذ يتوقف الوثوق بجزم البديهة بقضية على أن ليس الحاكم بها بديهة الوهم لا على أنها ليست جازمة بما ينتج نقيضها إلا أن يقال : ليس وجه امتياز بديهة الوهم عن بديهة العقل، إلا بهذا الوجه كما يدل عليه تقديم الجار والمجرور في قوله : إذ به يمتاز بديهة الوهم عن بديهة العقل . قوله : ( أي ما لم يتيقن أن ذلك إلخ) فقوله : لا ينتج على صيغة المجهول من قولهم أنتجت الناقة بصيغة المجهول وينتجها أهلها . قوله: (أي يلزم الدور) وجه التفسير هو الإشارة إلى أن الفعل أعني يدور مسند إلى مصدره بالتأويل المشهور فالتركيب من قبيل وقد حيل بين العير والنزوان
And it has been answered regarding all six doubts altogether that the premises mentioned in them are not sensory propositions; rather they are either self-evident propositions (badihiyyat) or theoretical propositions (nazariyyat) based on self-evident ones. So if all of them [the premises] are such that an error in one of them would necessitate removing confidence from all self-evident propositions because it is possible that certainty in all of them is of this kind, then repelling them requires proving that the certainty in that scenario arises from the intuition of the imagination (badiha al-wahm). And the essence of the fifth doubt is that the appearance of error in a proof whose premises were believed with intuitive certainty at one time necessitates removal of confidence from self-evident propositions due to the possibility of their error appearing after long periods. So repelling it requires proving that that certainty arises from the intuition of the imagination, and this does not necessitate removal of confidence from the intuition of the intellect. And there is no doubt that these two proofs are more difficult than cutting a harsh plant, unlike the sixth [doubt], for repelling it suffices merely the possibility that the judge of those propositions is the intuition of the imagination, as is not hidden. His saying: (i.e., circularity occurs) is an indication that the verb is attributed to the infinitive, as in their saying: 'a barrier was placed between the wild ass and the domestic ass.' His saying: (depends on the correctness of the self-evident propositions, etc.) so it is necessary to be certain of their correctness, so it follows that certainty of the correctness of self-evident propositions absolutely depends on certainty of the correctness of these self-evident propositions, and that is dependent on certainty of their correctness absolutely. This is if circularity is intended in its real sense. And if it is intended to mean the dependence of a thing on itself, we say: then it follows that certainty of these self-evident propositions depends on certainty of them because they are among the self-evident propositions. His saying: (And also if it depends, etc.) Its occurrence is on the assumption that it might be said: it is apparent. But according to the commentator's presentation, there is discussion because then confidence in the certainty of a self-evident proposition depends on the fact that the judge of it is not the intuition of the imagination, not on its not being certain by what yields its contradictory, unless it is said: the distinction between the intuition of the imagination and the intuition of the intellect is not except by this aspect, as indicated by the fronting of the prepositional phrase in his saying: 'since thereby the intuition of the imagination is distinguished from the intuition of the intellect.' His saying: (i.e., as long as we are not certain that, etc.) So his saying: 'does not yield' is in the passive form from their saying: 'the she-camel gave birth' in the passive, and its owners make her give birth. His saying: (i.e., circularity occurs) the explanation is an indication that the verb, namely 'circles', is attributed to its infinitive according to the well-known interpretation, so the construction is of the type: 'a barrier was placed between the wild ass and the domestic ass.'
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive answer (jawab) to all six doubts (shubah) concerning necessary knowledge. The commentator states that the premises involved in these doubts are not sensory propositions (qadaya hissiyya); rather, they are either self-evident (badihiyyat) or theoretical (nazariyyat) based on self-evident ones. Therefore, if an error in one such premise were to undermine trust in all self-evident propositions, one would need to prove that the certainty in those disputed cases arises from wahmi intuition. Specifically, the fifth doubt argues that a mistake in a once-confident proof could cast doubt on all self-evident knowledge. The commentator insists that such a proof would only affect wahmi certainty, not intellectual certainty. However, providing such a proof is extremely difficult ('more difficult than cutting a harsh plant'), unlike the sixth doubt, where simply positing the possibility of wahmi judgment suffices. The rest of the passage re-explains the circularity objection and the grammatical interpretation of certain phrases, emphasizing that the verb 'circles' (yaduru) is attributed to the infinitive, meaning circularity is a necessary consequence.

Key Terms
الشبه الست
البديهيات
النظريات
البديهة الوهمية
البديهة العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an error in any self-evident proposition undermines all self-evident knowledge.
Correction: The commentator explains that only if the certainty were of the same kind would that follow. By distinguishing wahmi certainty from intellectual certainty, the damage is contained.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say repelling the first five doubts is more difficult than repelling the sixth?
A) Because the first five involve sensory propositions.
B) Because the sixth doubt is the only one about imagination.
C) Because the first five require proving that certainty arises from wahm, which is hard to establish.
D) Because the sixth doubt has already been answered elsewhere.

Key Takeaway
The unified answer to all six doubts is that their premises are not sensory, but the first five require a difficult proof distinguishing wahmi from intellectual certainty, while the sixth is easily dismissed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are absolutely certain about a belief, how do you honestly check whether that certainty comes from your intellect’s clear proof or from your imagination’s comfortable familiarity? Are you willing to put that certainty to the test, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
The commentator says proving that our certainty arises from imagination is ‘more difficult than cutting a harsh plant.’ What cherished belief in your life have you ever actually subjected to that kind of rigorous examination, and what did you find? If none, what is holding you back from starting?
?
If a single error in a self-evident premise could unravel your entire intellectual foundation, how diligently have you verified the first principles of your faith and knowledge? What concrete step will you take this week to examine one of those foundations more deeply?

The Circularity Objection and Its Grammatical Clarification
Commentator's logical and grammatical clarification of the circularity implied by the sixth doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical circularity problem in the sixth doubt and the commentator's grammatical analysis of the verb 'yaduru'.

Core Idea
The sixth doubt implies circularity because certainty in one self-evident proposition depends on certainty in all self-evident propositions.

Having presented the comprehensive answer, the commentator now examines a specific circularity (al-dawar) objection that arises from the sixth doubt. He explains how the circularity works logically and provides a grammatical interpretation of the verb 'circles' (yaduru) to clarify the dependency.

Guided Path
1
Read the circularity explanation
Read the second part of the passage starting from 'قوله : ( أي يلزم الدور)'. Focus on understanding the logical dependence that leads to circularity.
Pay attention to the distinction between real circularity and dependence on self.
2
Understand the two interpretations of circularity
The commentator gives two possible meanings of al-dawar: real circularity and dependence of a thing on itself. See how each leads to a problem for the sixth doubt.
3
Examine the grammatical analysis
The commentator notes that the verb 'yaduru' is attributed to its infinitive, similar to the phrase 'ḥīla bayna al-ʿayr wa al-nizwān'. This grammatical point reinforces the logical point.
This shows the integration of grammar and logic.

The Circularity Objection and Its Self-Refuting Answer
Objection and response regarding self-evident truths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of circularity against self-evident truths is answered by showing that the doubt itself relies on self-evident premises, making it self-defeating.

Core Idea
Doubts against self-evidence are self-refuting.

This passage presents a sophisticated challenge: if self-evident truths require proof, they would lead to circularity. Yet the response shows that the very premises used to doubt self-evidence are themselves self-evident or derived from them, making the objection self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the full objection and response. The language is dense, so take your time.
The passage contains a complex objection followed by a concise response.
2
Identify the two parts of the objection
Notice the two separate points: first, the circularity argument (فيدور), and second, the problem of certainty from intuition (وأيضاً).
These are marked by transitional words.
3
Analyze the response
Observe how the author refutes all six doubts by pointing out that their premises are self-evident or based on self-evident, so the doubts would refute themselves.
This is a powerful self-refutation argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينتج العقل عنها ( فيدور ) أي يلزم الدور لأن هذا الدليل يتوقف على صحة البديهيات التي استعملت فيه ( وأيضاً) إذا توقف الوثوق بجزم البديهة بقضية على أنها ليست جازمة بما ينتج نقائضها إذ لو جزمت به أيضاً لكانت تلك القضية من الأحكام الوهمية التي لا وثوق بها ( فلا يحصل الجزم) الموثوق به في بديهي ( ما لم نتيقن أنه لا نقيضه ) أي ما لم يتيقن أن ذلك البديهي ليس في مجزومات البديهة ما ينتج نقيضه ( و) ذلك مما ( لا يتيقن بل غايته عدم الوجدان ) مع التفحص البليغ وأنه لا يدل على عدم الوجود دلالة قطعية، وقد أجيب عن الشبه الست كلها بأن المقدمات المذكورة فيها ليست قضايا حسية فهي إما بديهيات أو نظريات مستندة إلى بديهيات، فلو كانت قادحة في البديهيات لكانت قادحة في أنفسها
The intellect produces from them (so it results in circularity), i.e., circularity is necessary because this proof depends on the validity of the self-evident truths (badihiyyat) used in it. (Also) if trust in the certainty of intuition (badiha) regarding a proposition depends on [the fact] that it is not certain by something that produces its contradictories—because if it were certain by it as well, that proposition would be among the estimative judgments (ahkam wahmiyya) that are not trustworthy—then the reliable certainty in a self-evident (badihi) is not obtained unless we are certain that it has no contradictory, i.e., unless we are certain that that self-evident is not among the givens of intuition that produce its contradictory. And that is something that cannot be known with certainty; rather, its utmost is non-found with exhaustive investigation, and it does not imply non-existence with certainty. And a response has been given to all six doubts (shubah) that the premises mentioned in them are not sensory propositions (qadaya hissiyya); rather, they are either self-evident or theoretical (nazariyya) based on self-evident. So if they were to refute self-evident truths, they would refute themselves.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response regarding self-evident truths. The objection claims that any proof for self-evident truths would require using self-evident premises, leading to circularity. Additionally, it argues that for a proposition to be reliably certain, one must be certain that no contradictory is also produced by intuition, which is impossible to ascertain. The response counters that the six doubts brought against self-evident truths are based on premises that are either self-evident themselves or theoretical propositions that depend on self-evident truths. Therefore, if these doubts were valid against self-evident truths, they would also refute themselves, as their own premises would be undermined.

الدور
الشبه الست
البديهة
الأحكام الوهمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any doubt about self-evident truths must be answered with rigorous proof, and the objection shows a flaw in relying on self-evidence.
Correction: The author demonstrates that such doubts are self-refuting because they themselves depend on self-evident premises.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the response to the six doubts against self-evident truths?
A) The doubts are based on theoretical premises that are independent of self-evidence.
B) The premises of the doubts are either self-evident or derived from self-evident, so the doubts would refute themselves.
C) The doubts are valid for sensory propositions but not for self-evident truths.
D) The doubts are answered by rigorous logical demonstration.

Key Takeaway
The doubts against self-evident truths are self-refuting because they rely on self-evident premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you harbored a doubt about a core Islamic belief that, upon reflection, was based on assumptions you already accept without proof? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the foundation of that doubt? Will you discard it now, or will you continue to entertain it?
?
The lesson shows that any objection against self-evident truths is self-refuting. Consider your own life: are there doubts you hold about your faith that actually rely on the very truths they question? What concrete step will you take today to silence such self-defeating doubts?
?
If the strongest objections against divine truths collapse under their own weight, what does that mean for your personal certainty in the fundamentals of Islam? Are you willing to trust the badihiyyat of faith more fully, even when your limited intellect struggles? How will this resolve change your daily practice?

The Sophists' Dilemma and Certainty in Self-Evident Truths
Sophists argue that answering doubts undermines self-evidence; the response: self-evidence needs no defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sophists' challenge against self-evident truths and the Islamic response that self-evident truths require no defense for certainty.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths are certain without defense; answering doubts only exposes their falsehood.

This passage presents a classic dialectical challenge from the sophists, who argue that responding to doubts about self-evident truths undermines their self-evidence. The response affirms that these truths are inherently certain and require no justification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains a dilemma and a response.
2
Identify the sophist's conditional argument
The sophists present two conditional branches: what happens if you answer the doubts, and what if you do not. Find these conditions in the text.
The phrase 'إن أجبتم عنها' introduces the first branch.
3
Analyze the response
The response states that we engage not out of need but to show the falsehood of the doubts. Notice the key phrase: 'الأوليات مستغنية عن أن يذب عنها'.
4
Reflect on the key insight
The lesson here is that certainty in self-evident truths is not dependent on rebutting objections. The answer to sophistry is not required for knowledge.
This connects to the broader theme of the self-sufficiency of first principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم أي المنكرين للبديهيات فقط بعد تقرير الشبه قالوا لخصومهم إن أجبتم عنها فقد التزمتم أن البديهيات لا تصفو عن الشوائب ولا يحصل الوثوق بصحتها إلا بالجواب عنها وإنه أي الجواب عنها إنما يحصل بالنظر الدقيق فلا تبقى البديهيات ضرورية لتوقفها حينئذ على ذلك النظر الدقيق وهو أي عدم بقائها ضرورية موثوقاً بها لأجل الضرورة هو المراد من إيراد تلك الشبه وأيضاً فيلزم الدور لتوقف البديهيات حينئذ على النظريات المتوقفة عليها هذا إذا كان الجواب بمقدمات نظرية وإن كان بمقدمات بديهية توقف الشيء أعني البديهي على نفسه وإن لم تجيبوا عنها تمت ونفت الجزم بالبديهيات وأجيب عن ذلك بأنا لا نشتغل بالجواب عنها لأن الأوليات مستغنية عن أن يذب عنها وليس يتطرق إلينا شك فيها بتلك الشبه التي نعلم أنها فاسدة قطعاً وإن لم يتيقن عندنا وجه فسادها أو نشتغل بالجواب لإظهار فساد الشبه لا لاحتياج العقل في جزمه بصحة البديهيات إلى ذلك الجواب فإنه جازم بها مع قطع النظر عنه
Then they—i.e., those who deny only the self-evident (badihiyyat) after presenting the doubts—said to their opponents: 'If you answer them (i.e., these doubts), then you have admitted that self-evident truths are not free from blemishes and that trust in their correctness is only obtained by answering them; and that the answer is only obtained by precise reasoning (nazar daqiq); thus self-evident truths would no longer be necessary (daruriyya), because they would then depend on that precise reasoning. And that—i.e., their not remaining necessary and trustworthy due to necessity—is the intended goal of raising those doubts. Also, circularity (dor) follows because self-evident truths would then depend on theoretical matters that depend on them, if the answer is with theoretical premises. And if the answer is with self-evident premises, then there is a dependence of the thing—i.e., the self-evident—on itself. And if you do not answer them, they stand and negate certainty in self-evident truths.' The answer to that is: 'We do not engage in answering them because the primary truths (awwaliyyat) are self-sufficient and need no defense, and no doubt about them occurs to us from those doubts that we know are definitely false, even if we are not certain of the specific aspect of their falsehood. Or we engage in answering to show the falsehood of the doubts, not because the intellect needs that answer for its certainty in the correctness of self-evident truths, for it is certain of them regardless of that answer.'
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma raised by the opponents of self-evident truths (the sophists) and the response. The opponents argue: either you answer the doubts against self-evidence, or you do not. If you answer, you admit that self-evident truths require theoretical reasoning to be validated, which means they are not truly self-evident. Moreover, answering would lead to circularity whether the answer uses theoretical or self-evident premises. If you do not answer, the doubts stand and negate certainty. The response is that self-evident truths (awwaliyyat) are inherently certain and need no defense. Doubts are known to be false, even if their specific fallacy is not identified. If we do answer, it is to expose the falsehood of the doubts, not because we need the answer for certainty—we are already certain.

Key Terms
المنكرين للبديهيات
السوفسطائية
الدور
النظر الدقيق
الأوليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a self-evident truth can be defended by reasoning, it is no longer self-evident.
Correction: The author clarifies that the defense is not needed for certainty but to show the fallacy of doubts; self-evident truths are certain regardless.

Checkpoint
What is the main response to the sophists' dilemma?
A) We answer the doubts because we need to prove self-evident truths.
B) We do not need to answer because self-evident truths are inherently certain and the doubts are known to be false.
C) We answer only if the doubts are sensory.
D) We accept the dilemma and admit that self-evident truths are not certain.

Key Takeaway
Self-evident truths require no defense; our certainty in them is not dependent on answering doubts, but we may answer to expose falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt your certainty about a foundational Islamic truth shaken by a sophisticated doubt? Did you realize that the doubt itself was false even before you could pinpoint why? How does this lesson change how you view such moments?
?
Is your personal conviction in the existence of Allah or the truth of the Qur'an dependent on being able to answer every objection? Or do you treat these as self-evident truths that are certain regardless? If the latter, why do you still sometimes feel the need to defend them?
?
The sophists' dilemma tries to force a choice: either answer and lose self-evidence, or remain silent and lose certainty. What false choices in your own life have you accepted that are similarly based on a flawed premise? How can you apply the lesson's logic to free yourself from such dilemmas?

The Skeptic's Objection and the Reply about Self-Evident Truths
Skeptics object and author responds regarding self-evident truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the skeptic's challenge to self-evident truths and how the author defends their necessity without circularity or dependence on theory.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths are certain; answering doubts is optional, not required for certainty.

This passage presents a classic objection from those who deny self-evident truths: if you answer our doubts, you admit they are not self-evident; if you don't answer, our doubts prevail. The author gives a clear reply, distinguishing between the inherent certainty of primary truths and the secondary act of refuting fallacies. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the passage from the beginning until '...and negate certainty in self-evident truths.' Understand the two conditional arguments the skeptics present.
Notice the dilemma: answer → circularity; no answer → doubt prevails.
2
Identify the two parts of the dilemma
The skeptics first argue that if you answer, self-evident truths become dependent on theory. Then they argue that if you don't answer, the doubts succeed. Spot the logical structure.
3
Study the author's reply
Read the answer: 'The answer to that is...'. Note the two ways the author addresses the issue: either we know the doubts are false without needing to answer, or we answer only to show their falsity, not to gain certainty.
The key distinction: self-evident truths are certain even without a refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم أي المنكرين للبديهيات فقط بعد تقرير الشبه قالوا : لخصومهم إن أجبتم عنها فقد التزمتم أن البديهيات لا تصفو عن الشوائب ولا يحصل الوثوق بصحتها إلا بالجواب عنها وإنه أي الجواب عنها إنما يحصل بالنظر الدقيق فلا تبقى البديهيات ضرورية لتوقفها حينئذ على ذلك النظر الدقيق وهو عدم بقائها ضرورية موثوقاً بها لأجل الضرورة هو المراد من إيراد تلك الشبه وأيضاً فيلزم الدور لتوقف البديهيات حينئذ على النظريات المتوقفة عليها هذا إذا كان الجواب بمقدمات نظرية، وإن كان بمقدمات بديهية توقف الشيء أعني البديهي على نفسه وإن لم تجيبوا عنها تمت ونفت الجزم بالبديهيات، وأجيب عن ذلك بأنا لا نشتغل بالجواب عنها لأن الأوليات مستغنية عن أن يذب عنها، وليس يتطرق إلينا شك فيها بتلك الشبه التي نعلم أنها فاسدة قطعاً، وإن لم يتيقن عندنا وجه فسادها، أو نشتغل بالجواب لإظهار فساد الشبه لا لاحتياج العقل في جزمه بصحة البديهيات إلى ذلك الجواب، فإنه جازم بها مع قطع النظر عنه
Then they, meaning the deniers of self-evident truths (badihiyyat) only, after presenting the doubts, said to their opponents: 'If you answer them, then you have admitted that self-evident truths are not free from blemishes and that confidence in their correctness is only obtained by answering them, and that answering them is only achieved by precise theoretical reasoning (nazar daqiq), so self-evident truths would no longer be necessary (daruriyya) because they would then depend on that precise theoretical reasoning—and that is what is intended by presenting these doubts. Also, a vicious circle (dawr) would follow, because self-evident truths would then depend on theoretical matters (nazariyyat) which themselves depend on them, if the answer is with theoretical premises; and if the answer is with self-evident premises, then a thing, namely the self-evident, depends on itself. And if you do not answer them, then they succeed and negate certainty in self-evident truths.' The answer to that is: We do not engage in answering them because primary truths (awwaliyyat) are independent and need no defense, and no doubt concerning them occurs to us from those doubts which we know for certain are false, even if we do not know the specific way of their falsehood. Or we engage in answering to show the falsehood of the doubts, not because the mind needs that answer in order to be certain of the correctness of self-evident truths, for it is certain of them regardless of that answer.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the nature of self-evident truths. The objectors (deniers of self-evident truths) argue that if one responds to their doubts, it implies that self-evident truths are not pure and require theoretical reasoning for confirmation, thus they cease to be self-evident. They also point out a vicious circle if the response uses theoretical premises, or self-dependence if it uses self-evident premises. If no response is given, the doubts prevail. The author replies that we do not need to answer because self-evident truths are inherently certain and the doubts are known to be false, even if we cannot pinpoint the error. Alternatively, we may answer merely to demonstrate the fallacy, not to obtain certainty.

Key Terms
بديهيات
ضرورية
نظر دقيق
دور
أوليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If I cannot refute a doubt immediately, then my knowledge of a self-evident truth is uncertain.
Correction: Self-evident truths are known with certainty even if the specific error in a doubt is not immediately apparent. The author states we know the doubts are false even without pinpointing the error.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do we not need to answer the skeptics' doubts?
A) Because self-evident truths are theoretical and require proof.
B) Because we know the doubts are false even if we cannot identify the specific error.
C) Because answering would always lead to a vicious circle.
D) Because the skeptics are irrational and cannot be convinced.

Key Takeaway
Self-evident truths are certain by themselves; answering doubts is only to expose fallacies, not to establish their certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a fundamental belief you hold about Allah or His attributes. Are you certain of it because you have 'proven' it through arguments, or because it is self-evident to your heart? The author says self-evident truths are certain without answering doubts. What doubts about Allah's existence or oneness have you let linger because you felt you needed a perfect argument? How does this lesson call you to trust the inherent certainty that Allah has placed in your fitrah?
?
The skeptics argued that answering doubts makes self-evident truths dependent. In your own life, do you treat your certainties about Islam as needing defense from every intellectual challenge? Or do you hold them as primary, knowing that even if you cannot refute every doubt, they remain certain? The author says we answer only to show falsehood, not for our own certainty. Are you seeking certainty from arguments, or resting in the self-evident truths of faith? What would change if you stopped demanding proofs for your most basic beliefs and simply held them as certain because they are primary?
?
When someone raises a doubt about divine decree or the attributes of Allah, does it create unease in your heart? The passage teaches that we can be certain of self-evident truths even without knowing the specific flaw in the doubt. Is your certainty in the fundamentals of aqeedah grounded in the same way? Ask yourself: do you truly know that Allah is one and powerful, or do you only 'believe' because you've heard it? How can you move from theoretical belief to the self-evident certainty this lesson describes?

Comprehensiveness of the Judge's Definition
The judge's definition is shown to encompass all valid and invalid kinds of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Judge's definition of nazar as 'thought by which knowledge or opinion is sought' encompasses all valid and invalid, certain and conjectural forms of reasoning.

Core Idea
The definition's comprehensiveness lies in the intention to seek knowledge or opinion, not the outcome.

Al-Ījī's definition of nazar as 'thought by which knowledge or opinion is sought' is deliberately broad. In this passage, al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī explains how this single definition covers every legitimate and illegitimate form of reasoning, from demonstrative proofs to rhetorical arguments. The key is that nazar is defined by its aim, not its success.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and the literal English translation. Notice the structure of the explanation: it starts with a statement of comprehensiveness, then breaks down the divisions.
2
Identify the key divisions mentioned
The passage lists several divisions: sound/unsound, certain/conjectural, leading to conception/assent. Find each one in the text and note how they are defined.
3
Understand the concepts of 'certain' and 'conjectural'
Pay attention to the examples: demonstrative syllogism (برهان) is certain; rhetorical syllogism (خطابة) is conjectural in matter; induction (استقراء) and analogy (تمثيل) are conjectural in form.
Certainty can come from both matter and form.
4
Grasp why the definition does not require obtaining the sought result
The commentary says 'all its individuals seek by it knowledge or opinion, whether it is obtained or not.' This is why invalid reasoning is included – it still seeks knowledge even if it fails.
This is the core insight for comprehensiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا الحد الذي ذكره القاضي تعريفه الشامل لجميع أقسامه الصحيح والفاسد والقطعي والظني والموصل إلى التصور سواء كان في مفرد أو مركب والموصل إلى التصديق على اختلاف أقسامه. قوله: (الشامل لجميع أقسامه) لأن جميع أفراده يطلب به العلم أو الظن سواء حصل أو لا. قوله: (والقطعي) باعتبار مادته وصورته كالنظر القياسي البرهاني. قوله: (والظني) من حيث المادة كالنظر القياسي الخطابي، أو من حيث الصورة: كالاستقراء والتمثيل. قوله: على اختلاف أقسامه من اليقيني والظني والجهلي، فإن النظر الواقع لتحصيلها فكر يطلب به العلم أو الظن إذ العاقل لا يطلب الجهل المركب.
Thus this definition that the Judge mentioned is a definition comprehensive for all its divisions: sound and unsound, certain and conjectural, leading to conception whether in a simple or composite, and leading to assent according to the differences of its divisions. His statement: (comprehensive for all its divisions) because all its individuals seek by it knowledge or opinion, whether it is obtained or not. His statement: (and the certain) in consideration of its matter and form, like the demonstrative syllogism. His statement: (and the conjectural) in terms of matter, like the rhetorical syllogism, or in terms of form, like induction and analogy. His statement: according to the differences of its divisions from the certain, the conjectural, and the ignorant, for the nazar that occurs to obtain them is thought by which knowledge or opinion is sought, since the rational person does not seek compound ignorance.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage explains the comprehensiveness of the judge's definition. The definition "الفكر الذي يطلب به العلم أو الظن" covers all divisions: sound and unsound, certain and conjectural, leading to conception whether simple or composite, and leading to assent. The commentary on "الشامل لجميع أقسامه" says that all individuals of nazar seek knowledge or opinion, whether they obtain it or not. "القطعي" (certain) refers to material and formal certainty like demonstrative syllogism. "الظني" (conjectural) refers to material uncertainty like rhetorical syllogism, or formal uncertainty like induction and analogy. The definition also includes different kinds of results: certain, conjectural, and ignorant (since seeking ignorance is not done by a rational person, but the definition includes the seeking of knowledge or opinion, not ignorance).

Key Terms
قطعي
ظني
يقيني
جهلي
برهان
خطابة
استقراء
تمثيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of nazar excludes invalid or unsound reasoning because it does not lead to knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that seeking does not require obtaining; even invalid reasoning seeks knowledge or opinion, so it is included.

Checkpoint
Why does the definition include invalid forms of reasoning?
A) Because they still seek knowledge or opinion
B) Because they are productive of certainty
C) Because the definition requires obtaining the result
D) Because they are always certain

Key Takeaway
The definition of nazar is comprehensive because it focuses on the seeker's aim (knowledge or opinion) rather than the logical form or outcome.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in thinking about theological matters, do you sincerely seek knowledge or opinion, or do you sometimes seek to confirm what you already believe? How might this affect the sincerity of your search for truth?
?
This lesson shows that the definition of nazar includes both certain and conjectural reasoning. Are you willing to accept conjectural results when absolute certainty is impossible, or do you pretend to have certainty where you only have opinion? How can you be honest with yourself about the level of certainty your reasoning actually achieves?
?
The passage states that the rational person does not seek compound ignorance. Have you ever avoided deep thinking because you feared discovering that your beliefs were based on ignorance? What would it cost you to honestly examine the foundations of your most important beliefs?

Defending Nazar Against Compounded Ignorance
Commentator refutes objection that nazar excludes compounded ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of nazar (speculative thinking) remains valid even when the premises are unknown due to compounded ignorance.

Core Idea
Nazar is about seeking knowledge, not attaining it; ignorance is never the goal.

The commentator now addresses a potential flaw in the earlier definition of nazar: what if someone attempts nazar but their premises are completely unknown (compounded ignorance)? Wouldn't that exclude them? This objection is answered by clarifying the nature of seeking—nazar is an act of intention, not of guaranteed success. Even a confused seeker is still seeking.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the core objection: that the definition of nazar might exclude cases where premises are unknown with compounded ignorance.
The objector suggests that extending conjecture (zann) to cover these cases is problematic.
2
Follow the commentator's response
Notice how the commentator first refutes a possible counterargument ('it cannot be said...') and then gives his main answer: the movement in thought is for seeking knowledge or opinion, not ignorance.
3
Grasp the key principle
Understand that 'talab' (seeking) does not require the attainment of the sought. This is the foundation of the response.
Seeking ignorance is impossible, especially for a rational person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لجميع أقسامه من الصحيح إلخ لأن الطلب لا يستدعي حصول المطلوب، ولا يستلزمه فإن قلت : يخرج عن هذا التعريف ما مقدماته مجهولة جهلاً مركباً، وتعميم الظن إيَّاه غلبة على ما سيشير إليه الشارح في التعريف الثاني، يأباه عبارة الغلبة هاهنا، لا يقال : في الجزم ظن لأنا نقول : لو سلم يلزم استدراك قوله علم، قلت: لا خروج لأن الفكر والحركة الواقعة في المجهولات لغرض طلب العلم، أو الظن لا الجهل لأن طلب الجهل يمتنع سيما من العاقل
He said: (to all its divisions from the valid etc.) because seeking does not necessitate attaining the sought, nor does it entail it. For if you say: what is excluded from this definition is that whose premises are unknown with compounded ignorance, and generalizing conjecture to it is dominant over what the commentator will indicate in the second definition, the phrase of dominance here rejects it. It cannot be said: in certainty there is conjecture, for we say: if granted, it would necessitate completing his statement 'knowledge'. I say: there is no exclusion, because the thought and movement occurring concerning unknowns is for the purpose of seeking knowledge or conjecture, not ignorance, because seeking ignorance is impossible, especially from the rational person.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection concerning the definition of nazar. The commentator is explaining the phrase 'to all its divisions' (li-jamīʿ aqsāmihī) from the earlier definition. The objection is that the definition, which requires seeking knowledge or opinion, might exclude cases where the premises are unknown with compounded ignorance (jahl murakkab). The objector suggests that such cases might be included if conjecture is extended to cover them, but the commentator refutes this. He argues that seeking (ṭalab) does not require attainment of the sought; it is the direction of thought. He then answers that there is no exclusion because the thought and movement occurring concerning unknowns is for the purpose of seeking knowledge or conjecture, not ignorance. Seeking ignorance is impossible, especially for a rational person. Thus, even in cases of compound ignorance, the nazar is not directed at ignorance but at seeking knowledge or opinion, so it falls within the definition. The commentator also notes a variant related to 'ghalabah' (dominance) that the base text author will discuss in the second definition, but he rejects its application here.

Key Terms
الجهل المركب (jahl murakkab)
الطلب (talab)
المطلوب (matlub)
الغلبة (ghalabah)
العاقل ('aqil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person with compounded ignorance cannot perform nazar because they don't know what they don't know.
Correction: Nazar is defined by the intention to seek knowledge or opinion, not by the initial state of knowledge. Even a person with compounded ignorance can seek knowledge through nazar.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator conclude that cases of compounded ignorance are not excluded from the definition of nazar?
A) Because the seeker may be ignorant but still obtains knowledge
B) Because the thought is aimed at seeking knowledge or opinion, not ignorance
C) Because compounded ignorance is a type of conjecture
D) Because the definition includes all types of ignorance

Key Takeaway
Even in compounded ignorance, nazar is valid because it is defined by the seeker's intention to seek knowledge, not by the absence of ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a topic where you are completely ignorant—perhaps even confidently wrong—do you still engage in thoughtful inquiry, or do you avoid it? What does your answer reveal about your true intention?
?
This lesson argues that seeking ignorance is impossible for a rational person. Yet many of us choose to remain ignorant on matters we find uncomfortable. Where in your life are you actively avoiding seeking knowledge, and what rationalization are you using to justify that avoidance?
?
Imagine you are in a state of compounded ignorance about a core belief you hold. Would you be willing to subject that belief to sincere nazar, even if it risks being wrong? Are you prepared to seek truth even when it shatters your current convictions?
